Development of a new parametric tool and assessment of
overheating and daylighting in oﬀices
Dimitra Moskoveli
Eleni Gkouvelou
Kongens Lyngby, 2019
==PAGE:2==
==PAGE:3==

==PAGE:4==
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Climate sensitive architecture calls for "future-proofed" oﬃce buildings to adapt to warmer climates with
respect to occupants’ health. As buildings become more insulated and airtight, the risk of overheating
increases. Thus, designers face the need to control solar heat gains through a proper facade design in
a way to reduce overheating risks with suﬃcient daylight access. As daylight is an eﬀective stimulant
to the human circadian system, the design of oﬃce environments should be planned carefully enhanc-
ing employees’ productivity. Achieving both overheating mitigation and adequate daylight provision is
challenging but is possible through simultaneous overheating and daylight assessment ensuring a well-
considered design from the early design stage.
This project aimed to develop a new tool through parametric analysis in Grasshopper 3D for Rhino in-
vestigating the eﬀect of facade performance through an integrated thermal simulation and daylighting
analysis using EnergyPlus and Daysim. Diﬀerent parameters (zone depth, orientation, thermal mass, win-
dow type, glazing to ﬂoor ratio, window placement) were assessed based on their compliance with Danish
building regulation and standards. An additional daylighting workﬂow was set up through Lark Spectral
Lighting plug-in to evaluate the non-visual health potential in terms of human circadian response. The
impact of orientation on the function of circadian rhythms was evaluated under an overcast and clear sky
for speciﬁc dates and time.
After the assessment and comparison of the eﬀect of each parameter in overheating and daylight, pre-
liminary design solutions were proposed in compliance with both criteria. Fulﬁllment of the daylight
target is a challenge in a narrow and deep zone, where a narrow and shallow zone suﬀers more from
overheating issues. North oriented windows provide a design freedom to achieve both overheating and
daylight requirements. Window types are important to ﬁnd the balance, with low solar heat gain coeﬃ-
cient and low visible transmittance to be more favorable in narrow and shallow zones, while high solar
heat gain coeﬃcient and high visible transmittance to be favored in narrow and deep zones due to their
daylight insuﬃciency. An increased glazing area is more preferable to beneﬁt the daylight provision, but
in windows facing North towards East and West as well, choosing the right window types depending on
the zone to minimize the overheating risks. Additionally, wider and higher-placed windows proﬁt the
daylight access. As for the non-visual health eﬀect of daylight in human circadian entrainment, it makes
sense to consider it in architectural applications in danish climates to enhance work performance.
The tool and hence, this thesis provide a synthesis of information and assist designers and engineers to
pass from ideas to a range of good starting solutions related to the facade performance on overheating,
daylighting and its non-visual beneﬁts.
Keywords: zone depth, orientation, thermal mass, window type, glazing to ﬂoor ratio, window head
height, window sill height, overheating risks, daylighting, non-visual health potential, circadian response

Preface
This report is the results of the project carried out in the process of completing a Master of Science (M.Sc.)
program in Architectural Engineering at the Department of Civil Engineering of the Technical University
of Denmark (DTU). The project was accomplished from February 11th to August 2nd 2019 corresponding
to a workload of 30 ECTS points.
The thesis was conducted by two students, Dimitra Moskoveli and Eleni Gkouvelou in collaboration with
the company of Ove Arup Partners Danmark A/S. The supervisors are the Assistant Professor in DTU
Civil Engineering Department Mandana Sarey Khanie, Head of Studies of M.Sc. Engineering program in
Architectural Engineering Christian Neyra Rønne in DTU Byg, Senior Engineer in Ove Arup Partners
Danmark A/S Nicolo Guariento and Facade & Mechanical Engineer in Ove Arup Partners Danmark A/S
Kasper Espenhein.
The project sought development of a holistic parametric-design tool by integrating a comprehensive sim-
ulation based study regarding daylighting and overheating for oﬃce buildings according to Danish indoor
climate requirements. Moreover, it is clearly stated that the parametric-design tool was developed based
on some plugins with a content developed by skilled programmers. Thus, the authors can not take any
credit for that work.

==PAGE:9==
Acknowledgements
This Master thesis becomes a reality with the kind support and help of many individuals. We would like
to extend our sincere thanks to all of them.
Foremost, we would like to thank Mandana Sarey Khanie, who was the supervisor of our Master The-
sis, for importing her knowledge and expertise in this study. Her constant supervision and consistent
guidance throughout the project was valuable to bring this report into success.
We would like also to express our gratitude to Christian Neyra Rønne, for his cooperative response to
any questions and also for his trust he declared to ourselves by recommending us to Ove Arup Partners
Danmark A/S.
Distinguished members of this endeavor were Nicolo Guariento who approved us to work in this project,
and together with Kasper Espenhein, they shared knowledge and constructive suggestions for the accom-
plishment of this thesis.
This thesis could not be completed without the eﬀort, dedication and excellent cooperation of each other
for delivering a complete thesis which we are proud of. We would like to devote this Master thesis to our
friendship which started in the beginning of this Master program and was strengthened throughout the
collaboration in the courses and many projects.
Last but not least we would like to deeply thank our families and friends who helped directly and indirectly
with their constant support and encouragement to complete our Master program throughout our study
period.

Chapter 1
Introduction
The reduction of energy consumption and the improvement of indoor climate are key concerns when
designing buildings in recent years. The commitment of European Union has set high objectives for in-
creasing energy eﬃciency to reduce the total greenhouse gas emissions by 20% by 2020 and by 90% by
2050 (European Parliament, 2010). Danish government has set an even more ambitious goal to shift com-
pletely from fossil fuels to renewable energy by 2050 (Danish Government, 2014). The building sector
plays a crucial role towards these targets, since it depletes 40% of the total delivered energy and 36% of
total CO2 emissions within Europe, which are primarily attributed to the production of energy consumed
in heating, cooling and lighting (European Commission, 2017). At the same time, the energy demand in
commercial sector increases by 1.6% yearly worldwide and is the fastest-growing energy demand sector
(U.S. Energy Information Administration, 2016). According to previous studies, great percentage of the
net energy demand of an oﬃce building is related to window heat losses and cooling demands induced
by solar irradiance (Grynning, Time, & Matusiak, 2014). Therefore, it is evident that buildings need spe-
cial attention to respond correctly to the restrictive energy regulations and adapt to new "drivers", as the
climate change. However, it is highly important to remember that buildings are constructed to accom-
modate occupants prioritizing their health and their comfort needs. So, the need for an energy-conscious
building design does not focus only on reducing the energy consumption, but it spreads to improve in-
door comfort conditions. Indoor environment inﬂuences the occupants’ well being and indirectly their
work performance. So the conditions of their workplace are important to be investigated.
The standards in Danish building regulations require higher insulation levels to reduce heating demand.
Thus, heat losses are reduced in cold climates by limiting thermal transmittance and air inﬁltration, mak-
ing the buildings more airtight (Kolokotroni, M., & Heiselberg, 2015). In an airtight space, even though
the energy eﬃciency is improved, the indoor air quality is poor due to the restricted air exchange. Indoor
and outdoor air is not transferred in airtight buildings, thus mechanical ventilation is needed to supply
fresh air and extract the indoor air to the outdoors (The Severn Group, 2019). When ventilation systems
do not supply conditioned fresh air in the occupied space, overheating risks are growing, especially in
summer period, since the heat gains may be high enough. Meanwhile, the problem is intensiﬁed due to
the rising outdoor temperatures as a result of climate change and local urban climate modiﬁcation leading
to an increased urban heat island eﬀect, especially during the cooling season when external temperatures
are higher (Taylor et al., 2014). Moreover, increased internal heat gains during operation of a building
magniﬁes the overheating problem (Kolokotroni, M., & Heiselberg, 2015). Unquestionably, solar gain is
important for passive heating, by reducing heating demands in cold periods. Passive cooling though is of
higher priority, since incident solar radiation can lead to large cooling demands in hot periods. All these
factors rendering overheating an emerging challenge both at the design stage and during operation of
==PAGE:24==
2
Introduction
the buildings. The overheating problem has a weighty impact on occupant health and well-being, espe-
cially in working environments where people spend a major part of their time indoors, up to 90% (Leech,
Nelson, Burnett, Aaron, & Raizenne, 2002) (Dodge Data & Analytics, 2016).
An important way to reduce overheating condition is reduction of solar gains through facade design.
Here immediately a sensitive matter emerges as the design strategies to reduce solar gains (Ruck et al.,
2000) should be implemented without minimizing the beneﬁts of daylight access. In other words, the
building design should focus on the reduction of overheating risks, by taking advantage of daylight as
well (Level, 2019). Danish Building Regulation 2018 (BR18, 2018) documents requirements for suﬃcient
daylight levels within work rooms. . From an energy conservation aspect, the planned use of natural
light in oﬃce buildings plays a signiﬁcant role in improving energy eﬃciency by minimizing lighting,
heating and cooling loads. Artiﬁcial lighting makes up for 15-30% of the electricity consumption of oﬃce
buildings in Nordic climate (Røseth Karlsen, 2016). Therefore, wise use of daylight in combination with
artiﬁcial lighting control can minimize the electric lighting energy, which may have positive eﬀects dur-
ing cold periods by generating thermal gains and reducing the heating needs, but increase the cooling
loads during summer. Thus, by cutting down the use of lighting, internal heat gains are reduced allowing
a reduction in HVAC system size (Navada, Adiga, & Kini, 2013). On the other hand, periods of daylight
access during sunlit working hours aﬀect the indoor thermal comfort conditions by potentially decreas-
ing heating demands in winter or, if not well planned, causing overheating risks in summer. Another
important daylight beneﬁt is its so called non-visual eﬀects as regulator of the human performance. Reg-
ular exposure to high illuminances during daytime could have long-term beneﬁcial physical and mental
health eﬀect. Therefore, there is a growing need for an appropriate daylighting design approach from the
early building design stage, which can inﬂuence the building energy performance as well as the interior
thermal and visual comfort for the occupant. For this reason, it is always necessary to consider a balance
between daylighting beneﬁts and energy requirements (Cammarano, Pellegrino, Lo Verso, & Aghemo,
2015).
Undoubtedly, the facade design is a climatic challenge and a determining factor for achieving an appro-
priate design where thermal and daylighting performance meet. Facade design is strongly related to the
indoor comfort design since the design and conﬁguration of their transparent and glazed components
deﬁne to what extent solar radiation is transmitted to the indoor environment. The relationship of the
facade with the indoor conditions lies also on the fact that the materials of the facade inﬂuence the build-
ing energy behavior not only in terms of thermal insulators but also in terms of thermal mass. Therefore,
quantitative information about architectural considerations concerning mostly the facade such as the
orientation, fenestration system, room depth, thermal mass is indispensable to prevent overheating or
dazzle at any given time of day or year. In this way, special attention must be given in the facade and the
building envelope from the beginning of the project phase, in order to provide an appropriate design that
meets the indoor climate requirements within the energy framework minimizing the cost of decisions.
Nevertheless, the parameters related to the facade design determining the building thermal and visual
performance are diverse with wide ranges. These parameters are structured from the initial architectural
concepts and must be considered in the conceptual phase. However, the time to choose the favorable
combinations of these variables that leads to a good design proposal is short. Most of the time, designers
and engineers waste a lot of time trying to evaluate probable dead-ends through traditional methods
resulting in a delayed deliverable project. The eﬀort to create and test various design alternatives can be
minimized by applying parametric design principles, thus providing a design process of high quality.
Another important challenge is the integration of strategies based on overheating and daylight as a way
of the climate-sensitive and energy-conscious architecture to provide an harmonious interaction between
==PAGE:25==
1.1 Statement of the problem
3
the occupant, building and climate ensuring their well being (Herde & Nihoul, 1994) by avoiding extensive
daylight, glare and overheating (Røseth Karlsen, 2016) while using daylight for enhancing visual eﬀects.
In this project, a new parametric tool is developed. The developed tool intends to achieve a design equi-
librium between the thermal and daylighting performance. Moreover, the present master thesis touches
upon not only the technical inputs that are necessary for the parametric arrangement, but also on the
coupling of daylighting and overheating strategies that are followed in an integrated way, being com-
patible with the Danish building regulations and standards, to allow for a sensitivity analysis of several
aﬀecting parameters. The core of this project was to develop a tool applying the principle of parametric
design to evaluate diﬀerent facade conﬁgurations and their eﬀect on the indoor comfort as regards the
overheating and daylight according to Danish indoor climate requirements. Consequently, after the map-
ping, testing and benchmarking of design alternatives, an equilibrium between performance parameters
can be achieved "driving" conscious design decisions early. So, the current thesis implements a novel tool
to evaluate diﬀerent aspects of the facade regarding daylight utilization and thermal discomfort related
to the overheating for oﬃce buildings in Denmark.
1.1
Statement of the problem
Daylight, thermal comfort and energy aspects inﬂuence each other in a complex manner. This indicates
the need for an integrated design method in order to obtain the optimal balance of low energy use and
suﬃcient thermal and visual comfort. Optimal exposure to daylight, if not regulated properly, could cause
problems such as overheating. The necessity of an integrated study of overheating and daylight in oﬃce
buildings is thus underlined. Currently, there are no integrated methods or tools to assess overheating
risks and climate-based daylight evaluations in one simulation work ﬂow. Hence, use of numerical simu-
lations within such an integrated building design, in order to carry out multiple assessments and support
design decisions, prove to be essential. A multi-dimensional workﬂow, to have an overview of aﬀecting
design parameters that quantify the eﬀect of facades on overheating risk and daylight potential, together
with the non-visual health beneﬁts based on Danish indoor climate requirements, is an important step.
This integrated approach needs to be done carefully with the implementation of the relevant codes and
regulations. Thus, a proper investigation of Danish building regulations for both thermal comfort and
daylight availability is an important aspect of the project, which will be thoroughly investigated. Further-
more, in architecture the need for a holistic assessment of daylight performance based on non-visual or
biological eﬀects of light on human led to further investigations using available methods for non-visual
health potentials.
The signiﬁcance of the current parametric-based study lies on the consistency in the evaluation of the
building performance provided by an integrated assessment method for thermal comfort and daylight
quality performing a simultaneous simulation analysis.
1.1.1
Aim & objective
The ultimate aim of the project, which is based on six months of research and work, is to assist engineers
and designers to develop a qualiﬁed facade design from the early stages of a design process referring
to overheating risks and visual, as well as non-visual daylight potentials according to Danish building
regulations and standards.
==PAGE:26==
4
Introduction
This project seeks to identify uncomfortable thermal conditions in terms of overheating with
daylight potentials, in terms of visual and non visual eﬀects, by the development of a parametric
tool.
To achieve this objective, a simulation-based study of a single zone is set up, where two diﬀerent simu-
lation workﬂows are brought together in one tool that can be used for thermal and daylight availability
concerns in oﬃce buildings. The developed tool, so-called parametric design tool uses diﬀerent design
options, with most of them belonging directly to the facade. Basically, the principle of parametric design
is applied to evaluate diﬀerent facade conﬁgurations and their eﬀect on the overheating and daylight
availability according to Danish indoor climate requirements. However, the tool does not focus on opti-
mizing solutions but provides a wide range of initial design alternatives which are complied with Danish
codes and guidelines so that they can be considered valid to be obtained in other cases. Consequently, an
equilibrium between performance parameters can be achieved "driving" conscious design decisions early.
Concretely, the steps followed in the tool are presented below:
1. A thorough parametric design study with parameters of room depth, orientation, thermal mass,
glazing to ﬂoor ratio, window type and window placement.
2. Literature survey of all Danish standards and adaptation of them in the assessments.
3. Set up of a simulation study on Grasshopper 3D visual scripting platform to use Energy plus (US
Department of Energy, 2019) and Radiance (Ward, Gregory and Shakespeare, Robert, 1998) for
building performance simulations for overheating and daylight respectively.
4. Coupling of the daylight conditions with the overheating levels by considering the reduction in
electricity consumption. An electric lighting control was implemented reducing the heat gains of
overheating when the daylight supply was higher.
5. Integration of Lark Spectral Lighting, a newly plugin for grasshopper, to investigate circadian light
metrics within a daylighting workﬂow and thus, assessing the non-visual beneﬁts of daylight. This
step is necessary to understand the risks and opportunities of solar radiation gains in the introduced
cases of the project.
6. Deriving ﬁndings and results to understand the risks and opportunities of diﬀerent design scenarios.
1.2
Project framework
The overall framework for the thesis project was a collaboration between the Technical University of Den-
mark (DTU) and Ove Arup Partners Danmark A/S (ARUP). Throughout the six months of the project
period, work was planned to carry out at ARUP on two particular weekdays where supervision or meet-
ings with the company’s supervisors could be possible and at DTU the rest days arranging meetings with
the professors on a regular weekly basis.
ARUP was the starting point for the topic of the project, and in connection with the university, research
was performed from the beginning, in order to incorporate technical knowledge in an new tool that is
complied with guidelines and rules. The initial goal of ARUP was to create a database of results and
directions for the initial design stages concerning the indoor climate based on Danish regulations. The
guidelines would be drawn based on several results from diﬀerent facade design parameters, and the
==PAGE:27==
1.2 Project framework
5
database of these results would be applied to their design tool. Having these in mind, the authors ended
up creating the parametric design tool applying a parametric facade design according to the Danish indoor
climate requirements, after a self-directive learning process regarding the simulation software. Through-
out the project, the investigation ﬁelds were more speciﬁed after a constructive discussion with all the
supervisors concluding that the eﬀect of diﬀerent facade design options in oﬃce buildings could be scru-
tinized in terms of overheating phenomena, daylight availability and sunlight exposure as crucial aspects
of thermal, visual and non-visual indoor comfort. Some of the facade design parameters, such as the spec-
iﬁed window types and the building envelope’s materials, were set by ARUP from their experience in the
industry, as they were regarded quite interesting to assess. Rest of the parameters were deﬁned based on
research. Certainly, the collaboration between the university and ARUP contributed to gain valuable ex-
perience from practitioners giving the unique opportunity to be able to contact with specialists from the
ﬁrm’s global network, as well as connection to the state of the art research in the ﬁeld from the academic
side. The context of the project was also broadened due to the increased goals set in the very beginning.
The thesis is structured along ﬁve interconnected tracks, to provide all the information and acquired
knowledge for the comprehension of the ﬁnal outcome, according to Figure 1.1.
1. Background - theoretical and technical aspects of the research area and the software used for the
project
2. Methodology - the procedure followed based on the research study in order to develop a tool
3. Parametric Design Tool - the development and the validation of its outputs
4. Results - the production of the ﬁnal outputs of the tool and their processing
5. Discussion and conclusion - the assessment of the ﬁndings based on the results
Figure 1.1: Diagram of the thesis structure
Background as presented in chapter 2 introduces overheating and daylighting assessments in buildings
and reports the corresponding Danish criteria for them. Diﬀerent aspects of overheating and daylighting
in buildings which are highly important for early design strategies are also presented. The last sections
of chapter 2 indicate the deﬁnition and advent of parametric design during the planning phase nowadays.
==PAGE:28==
6
Introduction
The chapter ends with stressing on the necessity of integrated building performance simulations and a
description of the simulation tools used in Grasshopper 3D.
Chapter 3, which is the Methodology, includes the information that this parametric simulation-based
thesis was relied on. The chapter starts with the reference model which was used as a base for the project.
The chosen investigated parameters and the speciﬁcations of the model were analyzed as well. The
chapter ends with the method followed for assessing the non-visual health potential. The description of
the developed parametric design tool is presented in chapter 4. This chapter includes detailed explanations
and illustrations of the simulation workﬂows.
