in an open discussion of the link between the investigated parameters in agreement with overheating
and daylight, together with an argumentation of the ﬁndings of non-visual health potential. Chapter 7
concludes with the guideline solutions which are within overheating and daylighting criteria, reporting
the necessity of human circadian regulation in a building design. The thesis report closes with Chapter
8, where the project’s limitations and recommendations for a further work are given.
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 2
Background
This chapter intends to present a literature review regarding opportunities and risks of solar gains in
buildings and the need for integrated overheating and daylighting assessments. Moreover, criteria of
exceeded overheating hours and achieving suﬃcient daylight autonomy in space according to Danish
building regulations and standards are indicated. The parameters which have a signiﬁcant impact on
both overheating and daylight are analyzed based on a literature survey of various parametric studies.
The necessity of building performance simulations with parametric modeling is stressed on from the early
stages of a design process. The chapter ends with a description of the simulation software of Grasshopper
3D, reporting the main characteristics of its simulation engines and its plug-ins.
2.1
Overheating
One of the primary requirements in respect of the indoor climate in a building is the thermal comfort in
both heating and cooling season. The thermal environment is a decisive factor aﬀecting energy demand,
which has to be addressed ﬁrst. Thermal comfort is deﬁned as that condition of mind that expresses
satisfaction with the thermal environment, meaning that does not require the environment to be either
warmer or colder (Mora & Bean, 2018). Thermal comfort levels are highly demanding, as aﬀect people’s
well-being and productivity, and are diﬃcult to be satisﬁed by everybody in a space. There are large vari-
ations of thermal sensation in an indoor environment depending on the person. The primary factors that
deﬁne the level of thermal comfort are illustrated in Figure 2.1. There are environmental-dependent (air
temperature, relative humidity, relative air velocity, mean radiant temperature), and personal-dependent
variables (metabolic rate and clothing insulation). These factors should be within a speciﬁed range to
satisfy the thermal conditions (Navada et al., 2013).
==PAGE:30==
8
Background
Figure 2.1: Six factors that deﬁne the level of thermal comfort
During the heating season, it is essential to assure acceptable thermal conditions keeping the indoor
temperature above a certain setpoint without causing excessive energy consumption. Thus, aiming for
improving energy eﬃciency, building regulations have introduced high standards to conserve the energy
in winter. These standards include enhanced thermal mass, insulation, and airtightness. However, these
higher thermal standards, along with the global warming eﬀect, increase the risk of overheating not just
in the summer period, but throughout the year (NHBC Foundation, 2012). Thermal satisfaction is mainly
linked to cooling demand, which is a major concern for oﬃce buildings, even in cold climates (Brambilla,
Bonvin, Flourentzou, & Jusselme, 2018). So there is a particular challenge to design a comfortable thermal
indoor environment in oﬃces during cooling season (Ślipek, 2017). The risk of overheating can be inten-
siﬁed in urban areas, through urban heat island eﬀect, as heat generated during the day is absorbed and
re-radiated at night. The building itself also contributes to overheating issues, by its orientation, location,
construction, internal gains, and window sizing.
Overheating results in high indoor air temperatures that lead to uncomfortable thermal conditions harm-
ing occupant health and well-being. The most common way to assess thermal comfort and hence, over-
heating problems is air temperature (ta). However, it is only one of the six factors that contribute toward
the actual perception of thermal comfort, as can be seen in Figure 2.1. Operative temperature is a more
accurate reﬂection of occupant comfort than air temperature alone, hence it is a useful metric for over-
heating assessments (Trimble Inc, 2019). More speciﬁcally, operative temperature (to) is deﬁned as the
uniform temperature of an enclosure in which an occupant would exchange the same amount of heat
by radiation and convection as in the existing non uniform environment (DS/EN ISO 7726, 2001). The
exact equation in Equation 2.1, states that the operative temperature is the average of the air temperature
and mean radiant temperature (tr) weighted by the heat transfer coeﬃcient by convection (hc) and heat
transfer coeﬃcient by radiation (hmr) as follows.
푡o = ℎc ⋅푡a + ℎr ⋅푡mr
ℎc + ℎr
(2.1)
Air temperature is the temperature of the air around the human body (DS/EN ISO 7726, 2001). Air tem-
perature is determined by the balance of gains into and losses out of a room or a building. Overheat-
==PAGE:31==
2.2 Daylight potentials
9
ing is caused when the gains outweigh the losses, and hence, passive or active cooling will be required
(Greengauge, 2019). Mean radiant temperature is the uniform temperature of an imaginary enclosure in
which radiant heat transfer from the human body is equal to the radiant heat transfer in the actual non-
uniform enclosure (DS/EN ISO 7726, 2001). Radiant temperature determines the amount of radiant heat
transferred from a surface and varies based on the material and the surrounding surface’s temperature
and emissivity. As the mean radiant temperature is deﬁned in relation to the human body, it is highly
inﬂuenced by the extent the occupant is exposed to direct sunlight (Greengauge, 2019).
Designers should perform an overheating assessment at a very early stage to predict likely overheating
risks. The approach to optimize the thermal comfort in summer should be such that passive and not
energy-consuming solutions are used. Passive strategies that designers can use to mitigate overheating
can be the reduction of window area, the choice of an appropriate fenestration system or an optimal orien-
tation (Greengauge, 2019). Additional appropriate measures, such as solar shading devices, should be in-
corporated into the design to protect the building from overheating (NHBC Foundation, 2012). However,
the solar radiation that enters the building should be reduced wisely with respect to adequate daylight
provision. An integrated, balanced approach can provide the best results.
2.2
Daylight potentials
Daylight is naturally a fundamental element in architecture, as used to determine materials, form and
space. Moreover, daylight is used to compose a series of spaces through diﬀerences in intensity, direction
and color, thus, creating a building concept. At the same, daylight is a component of solar radiation
which is of major importance to the indoor climate and subsequently the building’s energy performance
(Nielsen, 2012). The design of buildings should guarantee suﬃcient daylight penetration, because of
the several beneﬁts of daylight from the viewpoint of energy demand and indoor climate. The beneﬁts of
daylight exploitation include view to the outside, passive heat gain, and potential reduction in heating and
artiﬁcial lighting energy (Boyce, Hunter, & Howlett, 2003). Moreover, suﬃcient exposure to sunlight can
inﬂuence people’s mood, performances and sustain the operation of the human circadian system (Webb,
2006). The quantiﬁcation of daylight and its distribution in indoor environments is an element key to
properly design energy eﬃcient lighting and control systems and also to evaluate energy consumption
and saving (Bellia, Pedace, & Fragliasso, 2015).
The way and extent that solar radiation is transmitted to the indoor environment is deﬁned by the design,
conﬁguration and direction of the transparent part of the facade, in combination with the properties of the
glazing and the facade’s overall geometry. Recently, there is an enhanced focus on daylight optimization,
which together with the technological advances in facade elements, especially glazing and solar shading,
resulted in use of highly glazed buildings. However, the increase in glazed area yields increased trans-
mission heat losses, due to its lower insulation value, and increased solar heat gains due to the increased
transmitted solar radiation (Nielsen, 2012). So, the drawbacks of maximizing daylight potential are re-
lated to the thermal discomfort by causing overheating, increased cooling demand and visual discomfort,
in terms of glare risks. A more beneﬁcial utilization of insolation and daylight, with respect to energy
eﬃciency requirements and occupant comfort can be achieved by dynamic fenestration systems. These
systems include dynamic solar shading and natural ventilation through openings to regulate the amount
of solar heat gains and daylight. However, solar shading devices may prevent adequate daylight levels
for work performance and also, eliminate the view to the outside.
Before getting to these aforementioned solutions, daylight simulations are necessary to be performed si-
==PAGE:32==
10
Background
multaneously with energy simulations to document the energy saving potential in case of daylight utiliza-
tion as well as the visual comfort of the occupants. An appropriate approach to optimise the fenestration
system and its position for each orientation is essential without compromising the daylight admission.
Glare can be protected by using optimised visible transmittance or shading devices, providing though
good illumination levels into the depth of the space. On the other hand, optimised solar heat gain coef-
ﬁcient can reduce cooling loads and protect a space from heat during summer. Moreover, the placement
and size of the windows can have a decisive eﬀect on the daylight potential and thermal performance.
Practically, solar control systems and strategies such as shading devices or the right window types need
to be considered to maximise the use of daylight with minimised risks with regard to indoor comfort.
Therefore, it is necessary to explore the daylight potential sources to assure that adequate daylight lev-
els are achieved detecting possible visual discomfort and overheating risks, which can be addressed by
applying optimal solutions for daylight control.
Daylight availability and assessment methods
Daylighting strategies are relied on the daylight availability, which strongly depends on the climate,
and the latitude of the building site (Ruck et al., 2000). Some other factors that determine the daylight
availability are the orientation of the building, the arrangement of the site context, the interior factors of
a building, meaning the dimensions of the room, the interior space layout and the windows, the optical
properties of the interior surfaces and the sensor positions (Design Builder, 2019). The amount of daylight
which is received depends also on the time of day. Thus, daylight simulation is a computationally heavy
task due to dynamic nature of daylight. Considering daylight availability and potentials with minimised
risks, i.e. glare or overheating, however is not an easy task and it pertains to use of several diﬀerent
simulation platforms and metrics, such as the ones presented below.
2.2.1
Illuminance-based metrics
Daylight factor, DF: It is the ratio of the daylight illuminance at a point on a given plane inside a room
to the simultaneous global illuminance under an unobstructed CIE Standard Overcast Sky, expressed as a
percentage. Since daylight factor measured at a single point can not represent the illuminance distribution
in the space, the average daylight factor is introduced as a better indicator (Tregenza & Wilson, 2011).
Average daylight factor, DFavg: It expresses the percentage of the daylight illuminance on a given plane
to the simultaneous illuminance under an unobstructed CIE Standard Overcast Sky.
The daylight factor approach was considered partially favoured because of its simplicity rather than its
intrinsic accuracy. However, the contribution of sunlight under real skies, the prevailing climatic condi-
tions for the location of the proposed design, the time of day and orientation are ignored. So this static
approach to assess a daylight performance is outdated due to the loss of realism. Therefore, the necessity
to capture the seasonal short-term variation in daylight for a given building site together with irregu-
lar meteorological events, leads to an annual performance of dynamic daylight simulation (Mardaljevic,
2000). Dynamic or climate-based daylight performance metrics are introduced below and used to quan-
tify the daylight availability during the course of a year in a space. However, their use is not so broad
among the designers’ community partly because the existing standards are still based mainly on tradi-
tional metrics and partly because the calculation of them is not automated by all software (Galasiu &
Reinhart, 2008).
==PAGE:33==
2.2 Daylight potentials
11
Daylight Autonomy, DA: It is the fraction of occupied or daylight hours per year where the illuminance
at a point is greater than or equal to a target threshold value (Reinhart, Mardaljevic, & Rogers, 2006). DA
could act as an electrical demand indicator as it expresses the autonomy from electric lighting (Vogiatzi,
2016).
Maximum Daylight Autonomy, DAmax: It is the fraction of occupied or daylight hours per year
where the illuminance at a point is greater than a maximum threshold value. This maximum threshold
is 10 times the minimum daylight illuminance level, which is based on intuition rather than documented
research. DAmax is used to synchronously consider the likely appearance of glare. This upper threshold
criterion measures the occurrence of direct sunlight or other potentially glary conditions and indicates
how often the glare risk appears in a space (Reinhart et al., 2006).
spatial Daylight Autonomy, sDA300,50%: It is the fraction of ﬂoor area that exceeds a speciﬁed illumi-
nance level, e.g. 300 lux for a speciﬁed amount of annual hours, e.g. 50% of the occupied hours. This
metric is an indicator of daylight availability. A greater autonomy from electric lighting is achieved by a
higher sDA, but does not necessarily result in a comfortable work environment (Lutron, 2013).
spatial Daylight Autonomy max, sDAmax3000,5%: It is the fraction of ﬂoor area where a speciﬁed
illuminance level exceeds 10 times the required illuminance level (3000 lux) for 5% of work hours. This
metric indicates the quality related to all types of glare, such as direct sun, reﬂected sun, high ambient
brightness, and overly bright sky. Moreover, this metric can give an indication of where large illuminance
contrasts appear in a space (Reinhart et al., 2006). A low sDAmax3000,5% implies that the beneﬁts of daylight
autonomy are not overshadowed by glare conditions that decrease comfort and performance (Lutron,
2013).
Useful Daylight Illuminance, UDI: It is the fraction of occupied hours per year where the illuminance
at a point is between minimum and maximum threshold values (Nabil & Mardaljevic, 2006). The aim of
this metric is to determine when daylight levels are useful for the occupant, which is, neither too dark
(<100 lux) nor too bright (>3000 lux) (Reinhart et al., 2006). The UDI metric categorizes the hourly cal-
culated illuminance values into three categories, which are too low and UDI is not achieved and referred
to as UDI "fell-short" (UDI-f) (typically less than 100lux), useful (typically between 100-3000lux) and too
high and UDI is referred to as UDI exceeded (UDI-e) (greater than 3000lux) (Mardaljevic, Andersen, Roy,
& Christoﬀersen, 2009). However, UDI achieved range of 100 to 3000 lux, which is sometimes referred
to as UDI-combined (UDI-c), can be further subdivided into two ranges UDI-supplementary (UDI-s) and
UDI-autonomous (UDI-a) (Mardaljevic, 2015). UDI-s indicates the occurrence of daylight illuminances
between 100 and 300 lux during occupied time. This index implies the possible additional artiﬁcial light-
ing to supplement the daylight for task activities. UDI-a indicates the percentage of occupied time with
daylight illuminances in the range 300 to 3000 lux where additional artiﬁcial lighting will most likely not
be necessary. Furthermore, daylight illuminances in the range 300 to around 3000 lux are often perceived
either as desirable or at least tolerable (Mardaljevic et al., 2009). Daylight illuminances exceeded 3000 lux
are likely to produce visual or thermal discomfort, or both. However, it is signiﬁcant to mention that the
upper threshold of 2000 lux was used before because of the common use of CRT screens in oﬃces that
cause higher glare issues. Modern screens, though, can give more "space" for higher daylight levels. That
is the reason that this higher value of 3000 lux replaced the value of 2000 lux. Also, according to studies
regarding the role of illumination in maintaining the circadian rhythm propose that regular exposure to
high illuminances during daytime could have long-term beneﬁcial health eﬀects (Webb, 2006).
==PAGE:34==
12
Background
2.3
Non-visual health potential
The function of a human ocular system is dual, as it aids vision and resets the internal circadian body
clock, which is close to the 24-hour cycle in human beings. Light is a regulator of the human physi-
ology and performance since it controls the human biological clock (Aries, Veitch, & Newsham, 2010).
Specialized retinal photoreceptors located in the ganglion cell layer exclusively capture the light informa-
tion, separately from the cone and rod photoreceptors which are used for vision (Pechacek, Andersen, &
Lockley, 2008). Circadian rhythms regulate biological functions, such as sleep or wake cycles, alertness,
temperature ﬂuctuations of the core body and the production melatonin (Inanici, Brennan, & Clark, 2015).
Adverse consequences for human performance, health and safety can occur by insuﬃcient levels of light
exposure, as the circadian rhythms can be disrupted (Pechacek et al., 2008).
At typical interior daylight levels, the spectral content of light inﬂuences human circadian rhythms. As it
is known, light is part of the electromagnetic spectrum from 380 nm to 780 nm aﬀecting both visual and
non-visual systems. Short-wavelength blue light has a more signiﬁcant eﬀect on the circadian system
than longer wavelength warm or red light does. Thus, human circadian system is most sensitive to the
blue region of the visible spectrum at 460 nm, whereas the visual response peaks at a wavelength of 550
nm. Daily internal clocks are aﬀected by the timing, the spectral intensity and the duration of light along
with the exposure to it.
The following were considered essential to be mentioned to understand how the circadian system works.
The time of light can either advance the internal clock to an earlier time or delay it to a later time. Exposure
to sunlight during daytime reduces sleepiness and improves performance, whereas exposure to light at
night disrupts the circadian system. As for the intensity of light and the exposure to it, maximum levels of
nocturnal melatonin suppression can be obtained after exposure of 0.5 to 1.5 hour to 300 lux. For a more
prolonged exposure of 6.5 hours, the circadian response is saturated at 200 lux at the cornea. On the other
hand, the system is saturated at 600-1000 lux, while half of the response is initiated with exposure at 100
lux at the cornea (Inanici et al., 2015). All the above-mentioned illuminances are photopic values for a
particular light spectrum. The equivalent circadian illuminances are diﬀerent, and their computation is
necessary for evaluating the non-visual health beneﬁt from daylight.
Tristimulus color space is commonly used for computation of light throughout the visible spectrum be-
cause people perceive color through three channels in the visual pathway. In contrast, in the physical
world, lights and materials interact in full-spectrum, which stimulates both visual and non-visual re-
sponses simultaneously (Amundadottir, Rockcastle, Sarey Khanie, & Andersen, 2017). But spectral infor-
mation for lights and materials are determined and computed with RGB data instead of the full-spectral
data. Thus, the accurate computation of color-dependent lighting metrics, especially circadian light met-
rics which are independent on the photopic spectral sensitivity curves V(휆), is hindered. The photopic
curve, which is the most typical curve in Radiance, gives photopic-lux values based on corneas. The
spectral distribution of light in circadian metrics is not weighted as in photometry by the photopic curve
(Inanici et al., 2015). However, it is weighted by the spectral circadian eﬃciency function of curves that
are not standard yet. These curves may refer to Rea (Rea, 2015), which is the circadian spectral sensitiv-
ity curve, and Lucas, which is the melanopsin spectral sensitivity curve (Lucas et al., 2014). Lucas curve
peaks at 490 nm, while Rea peaks at 450 nm and both give circadian-lux values based on the intrinsically
photosensitive retinal ganglion cells (ipRGCs) (Inanici et al., 2015).
==PAGE:35==
2.3 Non-visual health potential
13
2.3.1
Non-visual daylight assessments
There is a general lack of research studies that perform lighting simulations to predict circadian light lev-
els. Most of them are based on the calculation of photopic light levels. Thus, a growing need is revealed
for quantifying the impact of light on non-visual responses in built environments. Unfortunately, the op-
timal daily exposure of light and dark for promoting circadian responses is unknown so far. The threshold
used for this evaluation depends on the occupant proﬁle (i.e., the age, health condition, chronotype, etc.)
(Amundadottir et al., 2017). In studies where lighting computations performed to assess the inﬂuence
of lighting levels on circadian rhythms rely on the results of the circadian units on some criteria for full
circadian entrainment. Circadian values are usually calculated with Rea and Lucas curves. Circadian il-
luminance values at the eye level allow the estimation of human circadian response. However, circadian
luminance values aid the designer to understand the circadian light spatially (Inanici et al., 2015). The
eﬀect of material color choice on the circadian response can be readily conveyed at a pixel level from the
graphical representations.
Circadian values were estimated through three channel simulations in some studies (Amundadottir et al.,
2017), (Pechacek et al., 2008), while point in time simulations were performed in the former study and
annual simulations in the latter one. Moreover, a nine channel annual lighting computation is performed
in other studies, (Inanici et al., 2015). Multi-spectral lighting simulations are performed according to
recent research due to the higher color simulation accuracy.
According to Mariana Figueiro from Lighting Research Center (Lighting Research Center, 2019), the use
of circadian stimulus (CS) as a metric to quantify the light’s eﬀect on human circadian system is highly
recommended rather than other metrics. CS is based on the extent to which a given light source of a
speciﬁc amount and spectrum suppresses melatonin, which a hormone produced during the darkness.
In the morning hours, high CS levels are recommended more than 0.3 with bright bluish white light,
whereas in the evening lower CS levels are recommended no more than 0.1 with dim yellowish white light.
Lighting Research Center has a circadian stimulus calculator by using only vertical photopic illuminances.
Correlated Color Temperature (CCT) is the temperature of the light source, which can not be the only
basis to estimate the circadian response, because the spectral composition of light is diﬀerent. Melanopic
illuminance is another metric which is incorrect. First of all, it is not in SI units. Secondly, it implies
how light stimulates one photoreceptor, which is the intrinsically photosensitive retina ganglion cells
(ipRGCs). However, the circadian response to light is based on all photoreceptors including rodes and
cones, as all of them play a role in visual and non-visual pathways (Lighting Research Center, 2019).
According to this publication (Amundadottir et al., 2017), a 3D rendered indoor environment was used to
assess a range of view directions to predict non-visual health potential by photometric values. The assess-
ment was carried out in point in time simulations for an academic building evaluating 6 daily moments
during a typical work-study period from 8:00 to 18:00. These hours were repeated across 4 semi-annual
days, which represent a symmetrical of half year. The model that was used to predict the integration of
lighting information into the non-visual system was dependent on the dynamic light input throughout a
day. Two sky types were generated, clear and overcast, assumed a daylight source of CIE standard indoor
illuminant ID65. Moreover, neutral grey scale colors were used to render the materials in order to avoid
the eﬀect of the colors. Moreover, the criterion in this study was estimated based on another study which
examined daytime light exposure of 1000 lux compared to a dim light less than 5 lux for the duration of 5
hours (Phipps-Nelson, Redman, Dijk, & Rajaratnam, 2003). The threshold of 4.2 for 2.7 W/m2 was used as
resulted from a cumulative response. The results of this study demonstrated that a circadian entrainment
was achieved in the spring months for both clear and overcast sky. Additionally, the eﬀect of view was
more clear in case of overcast sky, since a similar pattern was shown in all view directions for clear sky.
==PAGE:36==
14
Background
After 18:00 the circadian criterion was exceeded except for January. The circadian response to daylight
was slower in winter months when the days are short and dark, especially during the last hours of the
working schedule.
This paper (Inanici et al., 2015) simulates the daylight model in an oﬃce building in Seattle with a variety
of sky types with color variations beyond neural white light. This study uses the threshold of 80 lux
for 100% circadian response as equivalent to 300 photopic-lux, which was found on April 4th at 9:00 am.
Diﬀerent sky types were selected for each date in order to study the eﬀect of sky luminance distribution
spectral combinations. After the comparison of these coloured sky types with the default (white) Radiance
sky type, it was concluded that horizontal illuminance values and sky luminance values are the same, with
the only diﬀerence on the change of sky color based on the CCT. By setting a sky type with a CCT of 25000
K, blue sky is produced. So, the impact of the circadian sensitivity to blue light can be drawn, expecting
maximum circadian light levels. Moreover, a variation of color is applied to the architectural surfaces.
In this way, the impact of the light and material on circadian response can be identiﬁed. Blue coloured
surfaces which were interacted with blue color rich daylighting oﬀered much higher circadian-lux values
compared to the red coloured ones. Moreover, the simulation of a room applying either a blue or a red
color internal wall resulting in higher circadian-lux values to blue color, while photopic-lux values yielded
to similar values for both colors. For the diﬀerent viewpoints were selected resulting in higher circadian
values in case of a person facing the window. Not only photopic and circadian illuminance but also
photopic and circadian luminance maps were presented. Furthermore, as regards the circadian falsecolor
images, it was mentioned that it is believed these images do not constitute components of the evaluation of
the circadian system as intrinsically photosensitive retina ganglion cells (ipRGCs) are uniformly spread
throughout the retina. For this reason, many circadian studies concentrate the evaluation of circadian
metrics on illuminance, not on luminance. This paper also highlights the necessity for physically accurate
spectral material libraries for multiple channel lighting simulations.
2.4
Overheating and daylight criteria in Danish building regulations
Criteria for exceeded overheating hours
According to Danish standard DS 474 (DS 474, 1995), the nature, extent and occurrence of the acceptable
exceedings shall be indicate. In periods with extreme outdoor temperature or other conditions, which
exceed design conditions, it can be allowed that the requirements for thermal indoor climate are exceeded
for a prolonged period. With respect to overheating tolerance, on warm days when occupants are dressed
in light clothing and perform sedentary activity, the requirement may be that the operative temperature
during the time of occupancy shall not exceed 26°C for more than 100 hours and 27°C for 25 hours during
a typical year.
Criteria for daylight provision
According to Danish standard DS/EN 17037, adequate daylight is provided in a space if a target illu-
minance level is achieved across a fraction of the work plane withing a space for minimum 50% of the
daylight hours. In addition, a minimum target illuminance level is also to be achieved across the work
plane. The minimum target illuminance level for openings in a vertical and/or inclined surface is at least
100 lux and should be achieved across 95% of the work plane within a space. Moreover, the target illumi-
==PAGE:37==
2.5 Impacting building parameters on overheating and daylight
15
nance level is at least 300 lux and should be achieved across 50% of the work plane. However, diﬀerent
target illuminances have to be achieved for openings in a horizontal surface (DS/EN 17037, 2018).
A horizontal reference plane is deﬁned to indicate the points at which the illuminance values are calcu-
lated for all daylight hours of the year to demonstrate the aforementioned requirement. For work rooms,
the calculation grid is placed in a horizontal plane 0.85 m above the ﬂoor. All meshes in the calculation
grid should be equal, and the ratio between the smallest and largest side of the grid should be greater
than 0.7. An edge zone of 0.5 m from the walls is also recommended to be excluded from the calculation
range (DS/EN 17037, 2018).
Two calculation methods were recommended according to the Danish standard DS/EN 17037 using day-
light factor (method 1) and illuminance level (method 2) for more than half of the daylight hours over a
fraction of a space. The ﬁrst method, which calculates the daylight factor, assumes a constant ratio be-
tween internal and external illuminance based on standard overcast sky. The target daylight factor is 2.1%
as the ratio of an illuminance level of 300 lux to the median diﬀuse horizontal illuminance from the sky,
which is 14200 lux for Copenhagen. In addition to this method, the minimum target daylight factor is cal-
culated in the same way with an illuminance level of 100 lux in order to guarantee that the spaces are not
poorly illuminated. On the other hand, the second method is a detailed daylight calculation using hourly
illuminance values during a typical year using hourly climate data appropriate to the site. This method
states the criterion that the target illuminance of 300 lux is achieved across 50% of the reference plane for
half of the annual daylight hours. As for the minimum target illuminance, the criterion documents that
it should be achieved across 95% of the reference plane for at least half of the daylight hours.
According to Danish Building Regulation (BR18) (BR18, 2018) , recommendations are documented for
which criteria should at least be met in order for a room to have suﬃcient daylight access. Windows
should have appropriate areas to provide adequate daylight throughout the year. More speciﬁcally, a suf-
ﬁcient supply of daylight can be documented by the fact that the glass area without shading corresponds
to at least 10 per cent of the relevant ﬂoor area. The glass area must be adjusted for any correction, as
stated in the Guidelines of Transport, Building and Housing on light and view of the surroundings. Al-
ternatively, suﬃcient daylight can be documented by demonstrating that the internal light intensity from
daylight is 300 lux or more at least half of the relevant ﬂoor area for at least half of the daylight hours.
2.5
Impacting building parameters on overheating and daylight
The increasing awareness of the potential beneﬁts of daylight necessitates the exploration of several
architectural features from the early design stages in order to ﬁnd an equilibrium between the suﬃ-
cient daylight provision and acceptable thermal indoor comfort, with regard to overheating. This section
presents some of the most important user inputs which are the search objectives of early design stages.
Furthermore, these inputs constitute some of the most inﬂuential parameters on both overheating and
daylight levels as a function of the transmitted solar gains through facade and window.
The literature survey that is carried out for this section focused on parametric studies considering a wide
set of variables. However, these studies do not include an integrated analysis in order to ﬁnd optimal
solutions for minimizing overheating risks and maximizing daylight potentials. The parameters which
are analyzed below are the site context and building location, orientation, thermal mass, room size, glazing
area, glazing properties and window position.
==PAGE:38==
16
Background
Site context and building location
Before applying daylighting techniques, such as solar shading devices in facades, it is important to de-
termine the site of the building due to the diﬀerent amount of sun a site receives (Public works and Gov-
ernment Services Canada, 2002). The impact of surrounding obstructions on overheating and daylight
access depends on their height and the distance between them and the window. Daylighting is aﬀected
also by their reﬂectivity.
Another factor for solar access that is chosen in early design stages depending on the site, orientation
and the arrangement of the surroundings is the building location (Level, 2019). The building should be
placed in a way that maximizes the daylight availability, but minimizing the transmission of excessive
solar radiation in the summer. The site surroundings, or even the ﬂoor level of the rooms cause diﬀerent
thermal conditions to the buildings (Paparizou, 2018).
Orientation
Orientation is the directionality of the facade or the window with regard to the azimuth angle (Vogiatzi,
2016). Each orientation requires a diﬀerent design emphasis, based on the climate and the location of the
building. The extent to which solar radiation is transmitted through facades or windows depends on the
direction of the facade. Thus, the orientation of the glazing plays a substantial role in the control of solar
heat gains, and also in the daylight conditions.
Most of the daylight is received in rooms with south oriented windows as more direct sunlight is expe-
rienced. This is owing to the long sunlight duration in South. However, light abundance of the South
causes overheating and glare risks, which requires the application of an adequate solar shading system
to control the excess solar heat gains in the summer (Ślipek, 2017). Moreover, a combination of the right
window type with a suitable glazing to ﬂoor ratio without resulting in overheating, but achieving the
speciﬁed daylight target should be a solution (Vanhoutteghem, Skarning, Hviid, & Svendsen, 2015). Fur-
thermore, tilting the facade up to 30°towards East or West causes slight decreases in daylight performance
(Public works and Government Services Canada, 2002). The daylight amounts vary to a great extent in
east and west facades since the sun is operating in an exact time of the day (Public works and Government
Services Canada, 2002). Basically, it can be claimed that east and west oriented windows have the same
daylight amounts but reverse during the day (Vogiatzi, 2016). However, due to the low sun position, the
daylight penetration is diﬃcult to be controlled. In East, higher daylight levels occur in the morning,
meaning that their alertness is acutely improved (Pechacek et al., 2008). However, the accumulation of
solar heat gains from early morning hours in east oriented rooms necessitate appropriate solutions to
prevent overheating. Similarly, in west rooms, low sun angles can induce serious glare and overheating
problems at unwanted times (Public works and Government Services Canada, 2002). Northward rooms
rarely have direct solar access and thus, do not cause thermal or visual discomfort (Ślipek, 2017). Hence
it is not necessary to block direct sunlight by means of shading (Luca, Kiil, Kurnitski, & Murula, 2019).
However, north oriented windows experience near-constant availability of diﬀuse skylight (Public works
and Government Services Canada, 2002). Thus, windows facing North should be adjusted in the way to
use the solar radiation to a maximum extent.
==PAGE:39==
2.5 Impacting building parameters on overheating and daylight
17
Thermal mass
The ability of a material to absorb and emit heat is called thermal mass. Greater amount of heat can be
stored in heavier materials, with higher thermal mass, compared to lighter materials (Paparizou, 2018).
Consequently, thermal mass can be used as a passive solar technology, as it is one of the most inﬂuential
mitigation factors against summertime overheating, on the one hand, and on the other hand, it can be
used for passive heating enabling the accumulated heat gains during the day to be stored and released
at night. A thermal mass, correctly used, has a dual role; stabilization of the indoor temperature and
extension of the time of release of the stored heat improving indoor comfort and diminishing the energy
costs.
During summertime, buildings with higher capacity for thermal storage can eﬀectively absorb excess
heat through the daytime and release it during the night producing a cooler building during the day
provided that there is a signiﬁcant temperature drop. This eﬀect is called "thermal lag" (Hodkinson, 2019)
and it refers to the rate of heat absorbance and re-emission to the interior. However, in hot climates,
where the temperature during the night does not decline signiﬁcantly, thermally massive buildings cause
overheating, which can be prevented only if speciﬁc measures are undertaken to reduce solar gains by
shading or adequate night ventilation. Additionally, the eﬀectiveness of thermal mass during wintertime
is also considerable, since accumulated heat gains throughout a day are stored and released during night
reducing, in this way, the heating energy demand by producing a warmer building at night.
In oﬃce buildings, the use of thermal mass as a preventive passive measure of overheating is beneﬁcial,
since the heat that is accumulated during the day due to the solar and internal heat gains is retained in
the fabric and is ejected during the night when occupants are absent. As a result, the thermally heavy
materials cool down so that they can absorb heat the next day while the temperature rises, moderating
the eﬀects of high external temperatures and reducing the overheating hours in summer, and the heating
loads in winter (Hacker, De Saulles, Minson, & Holmes, 2008).
Having all these in mind, the thermal mass should be integrated with appropriate glazing areas and
window types facing appropriate directions. Moreover, thermal mass should be placed in buildings with
high levels of insulation in order to delay the heat ﬂow through the building envelope producing warm
buildings during the night in the winter and cooler buildings during the day in the summer. Although, as
aforementioned, the beneﬁt of thermal mass is more visible in large diurnal temperature ranges. However,
diurnal temperature ranges decrease due to global warming, leading to temperature rise. For this reason,
the thermal mass levels is an issue rendering the use of thermal mass in conjunction with sound climate-
appropriate passive design techniques, such as proper shading devices and ventilation strategies.
Room depth
The rooms should be sized in a way that adequate daylight is available into its depth. In general, there is a
rule of thumb that a zone can be characterized as daylit if its depth is within a distance of 1.5 to 2.5 times
the size of the window head height. Beyond this size, room depths may need artiﬁcial lighting during the
day (Public works and Government Services Canada, 2002). The exact daylit space highly depends on the
window type and the target illuminance level.
A study investigated that deep rooms with smaller width are preferable from a space heating demand
viewpoint in both south and north oriented rooms (Vanhoutteghem et al., 2015). On the other hand,
smaller or wider rooms with a shallow depth are favoured in daylighting because such rooms have a
==PAGE:40==
18
Background
larger window to ﬂoor ratio. However, according to the same study, both thermal comfort and daylight
suﬃciency can be achieved in either deep or shallow rooms facing North. Moreover, daylighting and
thermal comfort in deep zones is compromised at the same time, with an appropriate window design.
This means that the right combination of window type and glazing to ﬂoor ratio with a proper window
placement should be applied to the facade to illuminate deep zones.
Glazing size
The size of the windows has a great impact on adequately fulﬁlling daylighting requirements and achiev-
ing satisfactory thermal indoor environments, as increased window areas results in dramatic cooling
demand. Special attention should be given to glazing size from early stages of a design process to ob-
tain a balance between solar thermal load and daylight supply. Designing windows with reasonable sizes
contributes to energy eﬃciency in buildings. The optimal glazing area depends on many architectural
parameters, such as the room size, orientation, window type etc.
Window size is decisive in rooms with long distances to the window, because of the low illumination levels
in the back of the room. The choice of the glazing size has a greater impact for the energy saving in the
back of the room, where the dependence on electric lighting is greater than in the front (Acosta, Campano,
& Molina, 2016). According to an evaluation conducted in Denmark, users of an oﬃce environment
expressed satisfaction to either too small or too large windows (Dubois & Flodberg, 2013). Larger glazing
areas do not necessarily increase the daylight penetration into deep spaces. Therefore, this intuitive
approach should be avoided by raising the awareness of architects on using other daylighting techniques
to illuminate deep spaces (Dubois & Flodberg, 2013).
The relative window size is usually indicated by the window to wall ratio (WWR) or window to ﬂoor
ratio (WFR). Window to wall ratio is deﬁned as the fraction of the glazing area of openings to the facade
area, where the opening is placed. Whereas, window to ﬂoor ratio determines the fraction of the glazing
area to the total ﬂoor area. Great areas of openings extend daylight zones, improving the uniformity
of daylight (Zomorodian, Korsavi, & Tahsildoost, 2016). However, beyond a certain limit, beneﬁts in
terms of daylight autonomy and electricity savings decrease. In particular, according to two parametric
studies, which assessed diﬀerent architectural features of the building, which were mainly linked to the
glazing, a considerable increase of daylight occurs in WWR up to 40%, while after this ratio the beneﬁts of
daylight are marginal (Cammarano et al., 2015) (Dubois & Flodberg, 2013). From an energy conservation
perspective, the WWR should be restricted to the lowest values, providing though adequate daylight.
According to a study,
Glazing properties
The importance of window glazing on energy and daylighting performance is undeniable. Like all surfaces
exposed to the outdoors, similarly, the windows are inseparably linked to heat gains and losses (Ruck
et al., 2000). On that account, the glazing properties of the selected window type have optimally to
minimize the heat losses to the outside during heating season, constrain the extreme solar heat gains
during cooling season, while allowing the maximum daylight to penetrate the glazing, to lit the room.
Solar heat gain is aﬀected by the window type, the number of panes, and any glass coatings (Eﬃcient
Windows Collaborative, 2011). The transmission coeﬃcients of window type that inﬂuence the thermal
comfort and the daylight availability are the solar heat gain coeﬃcient and the visible light transmittance
==PAGE:41==
2.5 Impacting building parameters on overheating and daylight
19
and they are described below.
The solar heat gain coeﬃcient (SHGC) is the fraction of incident solar radiation admitted through a win-
dow, both directly transmitted and absorbed and subsequently released inward (Eﬃcient Windows Col-
laborative, 2013). SHGC is often expressed as g-value with values between 0 and 1. The less a window’s
solar heat gain coeﬃcient, the less solar heat it transmits. This coeﬃcient deﬁnes the ability to control
solar heat gains through glazing. The solar heat gain coeﬃcient is inﬂuenced on the number of panes,
the window type, the frame and glazing ratio and the coating of glass. High g-values are recommended
in colder climates to allow thermal gain in winter.
Visible light transmittance is an optical property and expresses the fraction of transmitted light through
the glazing surfaces. In other words, visible light transmittance deﬁnes the amount of light in the visible
spectrum that passes through a glazing material. The higher the visible light transmittance, the more
daylight can enter the interior space, depending on the window type, the number of panes and the glass
coating. In cord climates, double or triple pane window assemblies are mostly used with a coating cover
and a gas of argon or krypton between the panes (Vogiatzi, 2016). Moreover, high light transmittance
can be used to smaller glazing to ﬂoor ratios for daylighting (Vanhoutteghem et al., 2015). Another study
concluded that daylight autonomy signiﬁcantly increases by using a higher visible light transmittance,
following by a higher glare probability (Vogiatzi, 2016).
The right window type is necessary to be chosen from the early design stages avoiding overheating
problems and also providing a suﬃcient daylight into the space. However, the window properties have
to be chosen together with the right combination of other factors, such as orientation and glazing to wall
ratio to control the solar heat gains and assure daylight suﬃciency.
Window placement
The vertical and horizontal placement of the window aﬀects the illumination and daylight distribution
of a room to a signiﬁcant extent. On the contrary, the window position on the facade does not impact
thermal comfort since the same solar heat gains are transmitted through the glazing part into the space. It
has been found that higher windows enhance the amount of daylight and contribute to an even daylight
distribution letting the light to penetrate deeper into space (Reinhart, 2014) (Acosta et al., 2016). However,
the window positioning in lower parts of the facade increases the electric lighting demand especially in
deep rooms where the sunlight can not penetrate deeper. This ﬁnding was documented in an oﬃce
room study that examined the impact of window position and window characteristics on the daylight
penetration and thus, the annual energy demand (Bokel, 2007). Another study analyzed the impact of
window position with other window properties of a typical oﬃce room in Helsinki in daylight availability
at four latitudes (Vartiainen, Peippo, & Lund, 2000). This study showed that vertical placement of the
window on the facade has a greater impact than the horizontal placement. The optimal vertical position is
highly dependent on the window orientation, while the optimal horizontal position is in the center of the
facade. The same study has indicated that lower window sill heights can be acceptable in larger window
areas. Additionally, a lower-placed window is allowed in rooms with a shorter distance to the window.
Another paper, which investigated the eﬀect of window head height, windowsill height and window
wall ratio on daylight intensity and distribution, stated that reducing the height of window sill does
not improve, but reduces the uniformity and distribution of daylight. Moreover, by increasing window
head height (from 1.4 to 2.4 m), uniformity (min/average) declines while uniformity (min/max) increases,
without signiﬁcant diﬀerences though (Zomorodian et al., 2016). According to the study, decrease of
window head height and increase of window sill height, increase the daylight intensity and uniformity.
==PAGE:42==
20
Background
According to another study that focused on evaluating diﬀerent window conﬁgurations, it was found that
higher and middle positions of window perform considerably better in daylighting (Vogiatzi, 2016).
2.6
Parametric design in early design stages
Daylight is regarded as a common denominator for architects and engineers for producing healthy build-
ings with regard to thermal comfort. So designers should be aware of daylight potentials for energy saving
and users’ comfort purposes. Designing for adequate daylight with minimum overheating risks can be
an appropriate starting point on the development of the building performance and the implementation
of technical knowledge about energy and the indoor environment (Nielsen, 2012). Thus, an integrated
building performance analysis is essential at the early design stages in order to prevent the delivery of
buildings with unacceptable thermal and daylighting performance characteristics.
The early stages of the architectural process are characterized by a constant search for a design direction.
The design choices rely on the experience, intuition, and rules of thumb. However, since the design is
formed by vague and changing concepts in these stages, there is a risk of taking decisions which will have
a substantial impact on the project later on. Thus, right design directions are highly signiﬁcant from the
conceptual phase, in order to maximize the quality and minimize the potential of increased design costs
(Ochoa & Capeluto, 2009).
Figure 2.2 shows the eﬀect of the design changes at succeeding stages of a project relatively to the level
of the designers’ eﬀort. The solid curve shows that if eﬀort is put from the very early stages of the
design process, then the available impacts diminish thereafter. However, the dotted curve shows the
cost of implementing changes to improve the building performance which rises as proceed through the
construction phases (ASHRAE Handbook, 2006).
Figure 2.2: Impact of early design input on building performance (ASHRAE Handbook, 2006)
Architects require simple tools to acquire quantitative information to strengthen their design choices,
since the impact of the undertaken decisions at the very ﬁrst stage of the design process is not evalu-
ated by them. These tools have to be capable of suggesting and evaluating design alternatives so that
==PAGE:43==
2.6 Parametric design in early design stages
21
the suitable ones can be chosen according to design intentions. Therefore, a simple graphical representa-
tion is required without detailed and precise information, since the project is passing later in the design
process to a level of detail (Baker, 2018). The design options are not only to meet the individual per-
formance parameters at diﬀerent variation levels, but also to understand how they interrelate (Nielsen,
2012). Parametric tools facilitate this process by allowing model-based analysis and simulation (Baker,
2018).
What is parametric design and modeling?
Parametric design is an automated design process, founded on the generation of a geometry designing
formal relations among initial parameters (ParametricCamp, 2019). These relations are mathematical and
geometrical and result from the use of variables and algorithms that lead to a wide range of possible
solutions. The ability to modify these variables for exploration of various design options is achieved by
a parametric modeling process. These iterations enable engineers and architects to generate a certain
eﬃcient design and optimize it as well.
Parametric modeling techniques have been gradually adopted in design practice since the past few decades
as a tool of computer programming. Basically, parametric modeling functions have been incorporated
into the design computer programming code such as a script. Parametric modelling is used together with
visual programming, which allows users to create and edit codes by connecting components and run sim-
ulations. Rhino was the inventor of parametric modeling and it was made available through the platform
of Grasshopper. Grasshopper is probably the most widely used visual programming editor and generally
used for computational design. Therefore, parametric modeling is extremely useful at the stage of scheme
design, where modiﬁcations in the design are inevitable according to the requirement of the architects
and clients (Fu, 2018).
Which are the beneﬁts of parametric design and modeling?
• Speciﬁcation of key parameters that can be modiﬁed interactively
• Automatic update of the model by immediate recalculations tweaking the original parameters
• Minimization of the eﬀort needed to create and test design variants
• Elimination of the possibility of human error and the need for complex calculations due to its
automation
• Time saving in the scheme design stage as there is no need for redrawing a model
• Application of engineering practices of a high quality in the design process, instead of using intu-
ition or simpliﬁed studies
• Release from the tools’ boundaries giving the designers the opportunity for designing their own
tools
To summarize, conﬁgurations that are generated during the parametric design stage are based on the
engineering judgement and experience which are implemented in an integrated way in order to study the
most eﬃcient scheme amongst numerous design options. In this way, the logic of searching for a static
and deﬁned solution is shifted to the investigation of diﬀerent factors that create a family of possibly
outcomes providing quantitative feedback for the selection of the most eﬃcient one (ParametricCamp,
==PAGE:44==
22
Background
2019). As already stated in the previous section, critical design decisions at the conceptual stages of
design deﬁne the building’s functional performance to a very great extent. So there is an urgent need
for checking performance parameters during the initial design phase. Therefore, parametric thinking is
introduced in the engineers’ mindset promoting a deep understanding of the critical parameters of an
analysis and providing a cognition of a design before its ﬁnal generated geometry.
2.7
Simulation software
Due to the stricter requirements for energy eﬃciency in conjunction with ambitious goals for the indoor
environment in the built environment, the focus is driven towards the optimization of the building design
even from the conceptual phase through building performance simulations (BPS). As design decisions
have a major impact on ﬁnal building performance and costs, building simulations are necessary to be
integrated early. Hence, a proactive exploration of the performance parameters is promoted through
them in order to guide the design team (Østergård, Jensen, & Maagaard, 2016).
Aiming for a holistic building design, a large number of interdependent performance objectives is cal-
culated, such as energy demand, thermal comfort and daylight. Regarding the building performance in
thermal comfort and overheating, building simulation tools such as IDA or EnergyPlus are used. In order
to use climate-based daylight evaluations, simulation tools are based on Radiance engines for backward
raytracing. The implementation of these engines require the set up of several simulation workﬂows.
However, Grasshopper 3D as a visual scripting tool that brings these engines together is based on a
programming language, which supports Python as well. Plug-ins link visual scripting tools with BPS to
identify the most inﬂuential input parameters in subsequent analyses. Exambles of plug-ins for Grasshop-
per include Honeybee, Ladybug, and Lark Spectral Lighting which link to Radiance, and Daysim, while
the ﬁrst two plug-ins link to Energy Plus and OpenStudio as well (Østergård et al., 2016).
Ladybug and Honeybee
Ladybug and Honeybee are open source environmental plug-ins for Grasshopper to integrate an environ-
mental analysis easily and quickly to the architectural design process at the early stages. They are both
named after animals, like many of the other Grasshopper plug-ins. It is worth mentioning that, Ladybug
and Honeybee are used more as tools rather than tool-kits so as to aﬀord the possibility for customizing
a script by entering correct input parameters in a meaningful way.
More speciﬁcally, the original choice of "ladybug" name was based on the ladybug’s weather forecasting
ability, and indeed Ladybug allows designers to import standard EnergyPlus weather ﬁles (.epw) into
Grasshopper and provides a variety of 3D interactive graphics to support the decision-making process
during the initial stages of design.
==PAGE:45==
2.7 Simulation sofware
23
Figure 2.3: Link between Ladybug and Rhino & Grasshopper, weather data, and 3D interactive graphics
(Wintour, 2016)
On the other hand, just as the ability of a honeybee is to collect nectar from a ﬂower, so too the "Honeybee"
plug-in connects Grasshopper to validated simulation engines, such as EnergyPlus, Radiance, Daysim and
Open Studio for building energy, comfort, daylighting and lighting simulation. Through Honeybee, many
of the features of these simulation tools can be adjusted in a parametric way.
Figure 2.4: Link between Honeybee and Rhino & Grasshopper, Radiance, Daysim, EnergyPlus, Open-
Studio (Wintour, 2016)
==PAGE:46==
24
Background
EnergyPlus
EnergyPlus (US Department of Energy, 2019) is one of the most robust and used building energy simula-
tion tools available both at academic and commercial levels for the calculation of energy consumption for
heating, cooling, ventilation, lighting and water use in buildings (Corrado & Fabrizio, 2019). This widely
validated and acknowledged simulation tool in terms of accuracy and consistency uses the heat balance
model to predict thermal performance in buildings. In particular, EnergyPlus [version 8.9.0] has been
used in this project in order to obtain the thermal conditions inside the zone in an hourly timestep based
on the detailed treatment of solar radiation (Vanhoutteghem et al., 2015).
Radiance
Radiance (Ward, Gregory and Shakespeare, Robert, 1998) is a physically-based, image-generating, back-
ward raytracer with a powerful rendering engine, used for the analysis and visualization of lighting in
design. Radiance’s input ﬁles are based on modeling a 3-dimensional surface geometry, setting the ma-
terial properties and the luminaire data, determining the analysis period and the sky conditions. Ray
tracing techniques are used to compute radiance values, which include spectral radiance (ie. luminance +
color), irradiance (illuminance + color) and glare indices. These values are typically arranged to render a
photographic quality image by additionally specifying viewpoints and their position and direction. Radi-
ance engine uses a hybrid approach of Monte Carlo and a deterministic ray tracing, providing reasonable
accurate results in a reasonable time. It is characterized by its predictive power of the realistic images
that aid lighting designers, engineers, architects and researchers to evaluate more advanced lighting and
daylighting performances (AMcneil & RChadwell, 2012).
Daysim
As Radiance is the most preferred ray-tracing engine to perform annual daylight simulations since late
1990s, the Radiance-based daylighting analysis tool Daysim has been used to calculate the climate based
daylight metrics (Reinhart, 2011b). A unique feature of Daysim is its manual lighting and blind control
model, called "Lightswitch", which combines annual illuminance proﬁles and user occupancy with behav-
ioral patterns as energy saving due to daylight depends not only on the annual daylight availability but
also on the lighting controls and the occupant’s behavior in relation to the use of blinds (Reinhart, 2019).
In particular, Lightswitch 2002 is a simulation algorithm that predicts the lighting energy performance
based on probabilistic behavioral patterns that have been observed in a ﬁeld of studies in actual oﬃce
buildings in Canada, Japan, German, the UK and the United States. Moreover, it is worth mentioning that
this model applies on private or two-person oﬃces and not to open plan oﬃce concepts, in which there is
limited personal control over the occupant’s immediate environment due to the variance of user behav-
ior (Reinhart, 2004). Lightswitch-2002 is a dynamic model, since annual proﬁles of user occupancy and
workplane illuminances due to daylight are simulated in 5min time steps throughout a year. At the same
time, it is considered stochastic, because the switching decision (on/oﬀ) is determined by the occupancy
proﬁle after some stochastic processes.
Obviously, the model’s reliability depends on the underlying behavioral patterns, which are four user
types and are used as model inputs to characterize the behavior of occupants that varies among them.
==PAGE:47==
2.7 Simulation sofware
25
Table 2.1: Four user types
Lighting Control
Blind Control
User Behavior
active
active
using electric lighting when needed only
passive

active

passive

According to Figure 2.5, there are two switch-on behavioral patterns for the electric lighting, depending
on whether the amount of incoming sunlight is taken into account for the switch-on event or not. As
for the manual blind control, there is a dynamic and a static manual blind control corresponding to the
presence of active and/or passive users respectively. The former manual blind control lowers fully the slats
at angles of 0°, 45°or 75°when the incoming direct solar irradiance is more than 50 W/m2, otherwise the
slats are re-opened once a day in the morning after occupants’ arrival. The latter manual blind control
permanently lowers fully the blinds at 75°, which means that there is a rare operation of the shading
devices by the occupant and actually, a constant visual contact with the outside is maintained.
Figure 2.5: Switch-on probability based on the indoor horizontal illuminance in Lightswitch-2002 algo-
rithm (Reinhart, 2004)
These four manual control scenarios enable the designer to make a solid lighting design without knowing
the particular usage of the building. However, only the electric lighting control of Lightswitch model was
considered as a reference for the present project, since the eﬀect of shading systems was not taken into
account in the simulations to account for the need of controlling the glare and overheating.
Lark Spectral Lighting
Another plug-in that was used in Grasshopper to fully evaluate the daylight potentials providing informa-
tion concerning the health potentials in terms of human circadian rhythms, is Lark Spectral Lighting. Lark
allows the computation of circadian light metrics, which are color dependent lighting metrics, through
an N-step multi-spectral Radiance daylighting simulation workﬂow (Inanici et al., 2015). In the current
project, the simulation was carried in the standard three channels (RGB) because of the limited simulation
==PAGE:48==
26
Background
time. Essentially, through Lark, local skies, exterior context, glazing optics, surface materials, interior de-
sign, and viewer location based on spectral data are deﬁned in order to analyze the relative impact of the
design options on the non-visual, circadian system.
2.8
Concluding the review
From the literature survey it has been observed that there is a lack of methods of integrated overheating
and daylighting assessments in a building. Particularly, there is a lack of parametric tools to optimize
a project during the conceptual design phase, on which to base the ﬁrst, but crucial decisions about
the building characteristics which can be chosen to balance overheating risks with daylight potentials.
The design strategies, if selected appropriately, could control solar gains providing suﬃcient daylight
without thermal discomfort. Therefore, there is a need for investigations upon the allowance of the
optimal amount daylight in space with respect to thermal and visual demands of the occupant.
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 3
Methodology
In this Chapter, the adopted simulation-based study and the parametric set up procedure based on the
presented background is explained. The descriptions are in details so as to allow for reproduce for future
studies. Moreover, several decisions and assumptions that have been made throughout the project in
order to obtain the accurate results (chapter 5) and further discussion (chapter 6) to reach the conclusion
(chapter 7) of the thesis.
Simulation-based parametric study
As already stated, the project focuses on the development of a tool applying the principle of parametric
design to evaluate diﬀerent facade conﬁgurations and their eﬀect on the indoor comfort as regards the
overheating risks and daylight potentials according to Danish indoor climate requirements. This tool
is based on a simulation-based parametric study, which is performed for one single sided-window oﬃce
room. The scope of the project was to draw guidelines regarding design parameters applied to one simple
zone, which can be a basis in conceptual stages for producing an eﬃcient building design. Furthermore, it
is signiﬁcant to be mentioned that even though overheating problems are caused in certain periods of the
year, a yearly simulation was performed due to the fact that the annual daylight analysis was integrated
with the overheating simulation analysis.
The approach adopted for setting up the simulation-based parametric study in Grasshopper 3D platform
involves the following steps. The ﬁrst three steps were explained in sections 2.4 and 2.5 and are true for
the whole set up. Step 4 to 9 are explained in detail in following sections.
1. Danish codes and guidelines were investigated regarding daylight and overheating.
2. The assessment criteria for overheating and daylighting performance and daylighting health ben-
eﬁts were set.
3. Facade design parameters and their levels of variation were determined. The chosen parameters
are commonly used and explored in early design phase.
4. Reference model was set up. A reference model developed by MIT (Reinhart, Jakubiec, & Ibarra,
2013) was chosen for the study. The speciﬁcations of the model in terms of thermal simulation
==PAGE:50==
28
Methodology
including position and size of the zone, internal loads, HVAC systems, and weather data were
readjusted so as to be as close to reality based on the experience of the authors. The daylighting
speciﬁcations were including reﬂectance of the surfaces, external ground, facade and window sill
surface.
5. The DRY (Copenhagen) weather ﬁle was chosen and implemented in the model.
6. The outputs of the simulation study were determined in order to have an overview of what will be
evaluated serving the whole process of what should be included as inputs.
7. The ﬁnal workﬂow was set up applying parametric relationships between and within the parame-
ters.
8. The integration of the two assessment methods for thermal and daylighting simulations was im-
plemented so as to couple daylighting and overheating strategies which is the core of the study.
9. Daylighting and its non-visual beneﬁts were also addressed and implemented in the same workﬂow.
This step allows for evaluation of daylight access for potential beneﬁts on circadian response to
sunlight exposure as explained in section 3.3.
3.1
Reference model
The case studies, simulated in this thesis, were based on the MIT reference oﬃce (Reinhart et al., 2013).
The reference oﬃce was used as the baseline for a comparative analysis of diﬀerent facade conﬁgurations.
Its room geometry as well as some of the material properties and other design decisions, such as the
lighting control, were used to create a rectangular sidelit space with adiabatic interior walls. So, the
eﬀect of key design parameters is easier to be evaluated and compared to others, before being applied to
more complex geometries.
The "shoebox" model has a ﬁxed geometry, with a speciﬁed wall thickness, a determined furnishing layout
for the workstations in Rhino and a detailed window construction with speciﬁc frame thickness. However,
in terms of parametric modeling it is more suited to rebuild such reference model from scratch so as to
allow for automatic and parametric variations in the zones and facade. More importantly, the new set up
should be made in a simple way so as to be tailored easily to an actual building. In particular, most of the
inputs, and especially all the important parameters for the performance quantiﬁcation, on the early design
stages, should parameterized in a way that the script can be implemented in realistic oﬃce buildings later
on in order to deﬁne the most critical parameters and conduct conclusions of the ﬁndings for drawing
guidelines. Concretely, the geometry of the reference oﬃce was simpliﬁed into single surfaces in which
all the properties of the room elements (walls, roof, ﬂoor, window) were implemented in order to facilitate
the parameterization of the zone geometry, as well as to reduce the simulation time. Figure 3.1 presents
the Rhino model that was created from Grasshopper 3D.
==PAGE:51==

climate requirements and energy consumption, or on the industry standards and recommendations from
research papers. Some of the speciﬁcations of the model are presented comprehensively in the table
below, however, they are described in detail in the following sections.

Methodology
It is essential to clarify that some of the following inputs aﬀect only on the thermal simulation analysis,
such as the airtightness, the internal loads, the setpoints and the strategy of the operation of the heating,
cooling and ventilation system of the zone. On the other hand, inputs like the implementation of an ex-
ternal ground and facade surface, the thickness of the window sill, the optical properties of the materials,
the matter only from a daylight viewpoint. Weather data, and zone position and size aﬀect both.
Figure 3.2: Eﬀect of model speciﬁcations to overheating and daylight
3.1.2
Model conﬁguration
Position of zone
The investigated zone was regarded as a middle oﬃce room of a building, surrounded by similar condi-
tioned oﬃces, not only in the same ﬂoor level, right and left of the zone but also above and underneath.
The reason for this placement is that ﬁrst of all, the number of the oﬃce rooms in the middle of a building
is greater than that one on the ground or top ﬂoor, in general. Secondly, in terms of overheating, the
oﬃce rooms that are located among others are considered more critical as heat losses can occur only
through one external wall surface. Hence, for the indoor climate simulations, all internal room surfaces,
meaning the internal walls, ceiling and ﬂoor, were considered as adiabatic. The zone, therefore, has only
one external surface, where the window was placed. As for the accuracy of the daylight metrics, the zone
was placed more speciﬁcally, on the ﬁrst ﬂoor, at a height of 2.8m, assuming a ground ﬂoor height equal
to the one of the investigated zone.
==PAGE:53==
3.1 Reference model
31
Size
The shape of the investigated zone was kept as much as simple as possible so as the investigation’s
results to be comparable and can be applied to early designs. In particular, the zone width and height
were kept constant at 3.6 m and 2.8 m clear height from ﬂoor to ceiling based on the MIT reference oﬃce
geometrical characteristics, while the depth was deﬁned as one of the parameter of the investigation,
which is mentioned in subsection 3.2.1.
External ground surface
The external ground surface plays a signiﬁcant role in the daylight simulations, since the daylight is a
composition of direct sunlight, diﬀuse skylight and light reﬂected by the surroundings. From the sur-
rounding surfaces, the ground is the the more important since, there is no possibility under any circum-
stances to have no eﬀect in the distribution of solar radiation. Reﬂected indirect light can reach the facade
or ground even when sun is in such position that they are shaded (Ruck et al., 2000). Thus, it has been
estimated that reﬂected light by the external surface can reach more than 15% of the total light that hits
a building’s facade (Velux, 2019) .
Moreover, it must be denoted that direct and diﬀuse radiation that is reﬂected by the external ground
ﬂoor may aﬀect the total solar heat gains that penetrate into the zone. That fact is taken into account by
the EnergyPlus software for its simulations. However, in the default mode that is used in this thesis, the
ground surface is considered as unobstructed, meaning that any shadows occurred by the building itself
or other external obstacles are neglected (Bigladder, 2019).
For the accuracy of the calculations the external ground surface must be big enough to direct the rays
correctly to the source. As a rule of thumb it is usually designed three times the zone dimensions from
each side in front of the facade.
Building facade surface
As aforementioned, the investigated zone is located in the middle of a building with an unknown ge-
ometry and size. However, the building itself aﬀects the daylight potential with its facade playing a
signiﬁcant role in terms of the amount of the reﬂected sunlight to their surroundings and therefore the
external ground surface, rendering the building facade of high importance to be set around the investi-
gated zone. Hence, it was designed according to the reference oﬃce, where it was stated that the oﬃce
room can be considered as one of the multiple identical spaces in a building that its height is three times
the height of the room and its width is also three times the width of the room (Reinhart et al., 2013).
Window sill surface
Another surface that inﬂuences the daylight distribution in a room is the window sill, since it can partially
shade the glazing area, and/ or reﬂect some light into the zone. These two eﬀects cannot be ignored when a
daylight analysis is made, thus, a window sill surface around all glazing area has been added in the models
according to the width of the external wall.
==PAGE:54==
32
Methodology
3.1.3
Materials and surfaces
Concerning the photometric properties of the surfaces, the materials and colors of the ﬁnal coating of
building components aﬀect the daylight distribution inside the room, contributing signiﬁcantly to a day-
lighting strategy (Ruck et al., 2000). Thus, the implementation of accurate and representative values in the
simulations is of major concern for realistic renderings. As stated earlier, the properties of a glazing unit
are easy to be found from manufacturers, however, this is not the case for the rest of the room surfaces.
Thus, it is suggested to measure these values when possible, or use databases which provide measured
property values of multiple materials (Brembilla, Hopfe, & Mardaljevic, 2017). Each material is charac-
terized by its color, reﬂectance, specularity and roughness. When talking about opaque materials, which
usually are the walls, ﬂoor and ceiling of a room, the property that matters more, is the reﬂectance. Re-
ﬂectance is the material property which indicates the amount of reﬂected light out of the one that reaches
a surface (Tsangrassoulis et al., 2004).
For the purposes of this project, speciﬁc material characteristics have been applied based on the reference
oﬃce, and were kept as constants during all simulations. Although the reﬂectance of window sill was
not shown in reference oﬃce, it was assumed that window sill has the same reﬂectance with the building
facade as given in reference oﬃce (Reinhart et al., 2013). The values for all the characteristic properties
that were used as inputs for the simulations are presented in the following Table 3.2. It must be noted
that all surfaces were considered as neutral grayscale colored, thus, diﬀuse reﬂectances for red, green and
blue were assigned with the same value and are present in the table as one property.
Table 3.2: Optical material properties
surface
