In this context, it was noteworthy to refer once more that, in terms of energy and indoor climate simula-
tions all internal surfaces were considered adiabatic, whereas the thermal properties of the exterior wall
were under parameterization and its U-values can be found in subsection 3.2.3.
3.1.4
HVAC system
Heating and cooling
In the current situation when the performance of the zone was studied at the early design stage, the
exact HVAC system was not necessary to be speciﬁed. The purpose of this thesis was to evaluate neither
the eﬃciency of a speciﬁed system nor the energy consumption optimizing the outcomes. Therefore, a
speciﬁc heating or cooling system such as ﬂoor heating/cooling system or radiators was not predeﬁned.
Such a decision would require more speciﬁed settings, which was not in the investigation ﬁeld of the
project. Consequently, an ideal heating system was considered more relevant to be simulated representing
==PAGE:55==
3.1 Reference model
33
all possible systems. No cooling system was implemented since one of the purposes of the study was to
evaluate the probabilities of the overheating phenomena in the zone, and a cooling system would prevent
this.
According to the general guidelines described to DS 474 E, the heating and cooling systems should be
designed to provide an acceptable thermal comfort for the occupants. The general thermal climate is
expected to be satisfactory for at least 90% of the occupants when the operative temperature ranges
between 20°C and 24°C, for sedentary people dressed in ordinary indoor clothing during winter season,
and between 23°C and 26°C in summer season, as it is depicted in the Table 3.3 (DS 474, 1995). The limits
for the local eﬀect comfort, meaning the mean air velocity, the diﬀerence in vertical air temperature, the
radiant temperature asymmetries, and the temperature of ﬂoor, can be found in Table A.1, since they
are quite speciﬁed for early design stages, thus, they are not within the investigation ﬁeld of this thesis
report.
Table 3.3: Limits within which an acceptable thermal indoor climate may be expected for 90% of the
sedentary people (about 1.2 met) dressed in ordinary clothing appropriate for the season (DS
474, 1995)
general eﬀect:
operative temperature in the occupied zone [to]
(1.0 clo approx.)
20°C <to <24°C
summer conditions
(0.5 clo approx.)
23°C <to <26°C
It should be noted that in the simulation program EnergyPlus, there is only one ideal system available
that is called Ideal Loads Air System. That system represents a decentralized HVAC unit, that can supply
independently suﬃcient amount of heating or cooling air to each zone to meet its loads. Each unit was
modeled as an ideal VAV terminal that supplies air in variable levels of temperature and humidity, and
the airﬂow can ﬂuctuate from zero to the maximum setting, depending on the heating or cooling load,
zone humidity controls, and other constraints, if speciﬁed (Bigladder, 2019). That means that heating,
cooling, and fresh air supply systems are combined into one and single system. Thus the settings that
must be applied in order to achieve the desired operation of the system must be carefully adjusted. As
these settings of the Ideal Loads Air System are referred to the tool and its used simulation engine, it was
considered more relevant to be presented in details in subsection 4.3.6, and only the general strategy of
the HVAC system is described below.
The heating air system was turned on only during heating season and the heating setpoint during oc-
cupancy time was set to 23°C in order to avoid resulting operative temperatures lower than 20°C, as it
is required by DS 474 (DS 474, 1995). Moreover, a setback temperature of 15°C was deﬁned for the rest
of the hours, as this is a more realistic and eﬀective approach to the simulation of a heating system in
oﬃce buildings. In this way, the temperature did not drop lower than the setback, consuming less energy
and time to reach the setpoint when the heating system turned on. In order to prevent the system from
operating during summer period the heating setpoint was set to 15°C constantly. In the same way the
setpoint temperature for the cooling system was set to 100°C. The schedules and the setpoints as they
were deﬁned for the thesis are depicted in Table 3.4.
==PAGE:56==

It is worth mentioning that the heating and cooling periods were determined by acquiring the heating and
cooling degree-hours, based on the hourly dry bulb temperature retrieved from the weather ﬁle. Degree-
hours are deﬁned as the sum of the hourly diﬀerence between a base temperature and the average ambient
outside air temperature (Coskun, 2010). The base temperature for the heating period was set to 18.3 °C,
whereas for the cooling period at 23.3 °C, as these values are commonly used for this determination and
are the default settings in the Ladybug component that was used to retrieve the degree-days. As it is
illustrated in Table 3.5, the monthly cooling degree-hours, found for the Copenhagen DRY weather ﬁle,
were limited in the period from June to August, whereas the heating degree-hours of this period were
quite lower comparing to the rest of the months. Moreover, it could be assumed that the heating degree-
hours from June till August refer mostly to the unoccupied hours. So, the heating season is narrowed
from 1st of January till end of May and from 1st of September till end of the year.

Ventilation
The operation of the ventilation system must provide a satisfactory air quality and moisture with consid-
eration of the use according to Danish building regulations, where, it is stated that in other than residential
buildings or day-care facilities and teaching rooms, the dimensioning of ventilation must be carried in
consideration of the size and use of the room (BR18, 2018). These minimum requirements, deﬁned in
Danish standard DS 447, are dependent on the emissions from occupants and building, furnishing and
installations (DS 447 E, 2014). Thus, the total required air supply is determined by Equation 3.1:
==PAGE:57==
3.1 Reference model
35
푞tot = 푛⋅푞p + 퐴⋅푞B
(3.1)
where,
qtot is the total quantity of outdoor air supplied to the room, l/s
n is the desig value for the number of occupants in the room
qp is the quantity of outdoor air supplied per person, l/s per person
A is the ﬂoor area, m2
qB is the quantity of outdoor air supplied to consider emissions from building and furnishing, l/s per m2
The necessary outdoor air supply considering the occupancy emissions (qP) is conditional to the category
of indoor environment that is aimed, whereas, the air supply for emissions from buildings (qB) depends
on not only the category but also on the level of pollution in the building. In this thesis project, according
to Dansk standard DS 15251, category I was chosen to ensure the best indoor air quality for the occupants,
requiring 10 l/s per person and at least 1 l/s per ﬂoor area for low polluting buildings (DS/EN 15251, 2007).
The ventilation was adapted according to the number of present occupants in the room. Therefore, the
air supply rate drops to the minimum qB during the unoccupied hours, considering only the building
emissions.
The ventilation rates for each zone are presented in Table 3.6, based on the number of occupants, as it is
analyzed in subsection 3.1.5, and the ﬂoor area.

3.1.5
Internal loads
Inﬁltration rate, number of occupants, equipment and lighting power as well as their schedules aﬀect
signiﬁcantly the internal heat gains and losses, thus the indoor temperature. The schedules of the internal
gains are of high signiﬁcance for the thermal comfort analysis, whereas they do not aﬀect the daylight
simulations. However, the lighting is connected to the daylight simulations as its schedule depends on
the daylight availability and has to be extracted ﬁrst to be applied in the thermal comfort analysis.
Inﬁltration
The airtightness of a building envelope is characterized by the inﬁltration rate of air from outside to
inside and vice versa. It depends on the quality of the construction since, the accuracy in assembling
windows and doors in the external skin along with various cracks and gaps in it allow air leakages. The
driving forces of this uncontrolled air ﬂow is the pressure diﬀerence that occurs in the building’s facade by
wind or temperature diﬀerence (Younes, Shdid, & Bitsuamlak, 2011). This phenomenon impacts not only
the energy consumption but also the indoor temperature and air quality, thus, it is an important factor
==PAGE:58==
36
Methodology
that should always be taken into account when designing and constructing a building. Danish building
regulations have set some minimum requirements that each construction must meet. The inﬁltration
rate for new buildings heated to minimum 15°C is stated to be less than 1 l/s per m2 heated ﬂoor area at
a diﬀerence pressure of 50 Pa, whereas for the Building class 2020 it is reduced to 0.5 l/s per m2 (BR18,
2018).
Taking into account that new buildings from now on should focus on meeting the requirements for Build-
ing class 2020 and that an inﬁltration rate of 1 l/s per m2 heated ﬂoor area is currently considered as a
leaky building envelope, the inﬁltration rate set in the simulations was 0.5 l/s per m2. Moreover, since
the evaluation of overheating phenomena is one of the main purposes of this thesis, it was regarded that
a more airtight building envelope will be a more conservative representation of the reality, increasing
possibly the annual occupied hours of operative temperature that exceed the limit. Finally, the surface
through which air can ﬂow was limited in the investigated cases to only the external wall, surface that
is relatively small compared to ﬂoor area, rendering a higher inﬁltration rate unrealistic. The inﬁltration
rate is considered to be constant throughout all simulation period.
Occupancy
The occupancy schedule that was used in the simulation based study covered 10 hours from 8:00 to 18:00
on weekdays, and was selected based on the reference oﬃce. The choice of such a high occupancy rate
was made to gauge the potential impact of the overheating results on the space (Reinhart et al., 2013).
According to the reference oﬃce, two thirds of the total six occupants were considered to be present
simultaneously in zone I during the occupancy schedule. This assumption of four occupied work spaces
out of six oﬀers the occupants the possibility of movement around the space. Moreover, the reduction
of the occupancy to two thirds of seating capacity leads to more realistic internal gains, and in this way,
the eﬀect of the facade design on the indoor environment can be noticeable (Reinhart et al., 2013). Since
the total area of the zone II was decreased approximately to two thirds, the number of occupants also
dropped in total number to 4 accordingly. However, the occupancy schedule remained the same with
zone I, with two thirds of the workers to be present at the same time in the space, as well.
Additionally, the occupants’ activity was considered to be a regular oﬃce work using a computer. Ac-
cording to DS/EN ISO 7730, taking into account a sedentary activity for oﬃces, the metabolic rate of 1.2
MET corresponding to 70 W/m2 of an "average" individual was calculated based on the body surface area
of 1.8 m2 (DS/EN ISO 7730, 2006) (DS/EN ISO 8996, 2004), equal to 126 W per person. The activity level
schedule was also set according to the occupancy schedule.
Lighting
Artiﬁcial lighting is usually an outcome of a detailed analysis and design process in order to deﬁne the
number and layout of the luminaires, their power and light distribution that meet the requirements.
Deﬁning these details means that the requirements are speciﬁed according to a determined furnishing
layout, preventing architectural freedom in the conceptual phase. Basically, the initial purpose of the
thesis was not to generate a certain design, but to explore a wide range of possible alternatives that the
variable parameters will provide. Thus, the size and the location of the work stations were not deﬁned in
the zone due to ﬂexibility reasons, treating as the task area the entire ﬂoor area (DS/EN 12464-1, 2011).
==PAGE:59==
3.1 Reference model
37
However, the implementation of the heat gains from artiﬁcial lighting to the models was considered
essential for the thermal indoor climate assessment. Thus the power and layout of the luminaires were
taken from the reference oﬃce since the geometry of the investigated zones were, also, based on the same
model.
The lighting design of the reference oﬃce is based on a furnishing layout of six work stations, separated in
three rows. Each row, one close to the window, one close to the back wall and one in the middle of the zone
consists of two desks facing the side internal walls as it is depicted in Figure 3.3. The luminaires are placed
accordingly to this layout, and each row has installed lighting power equal to 100W (Reinhart et al., 2013).
The lighting control suggested for the reference oﬃce is a manual switch on/oﬀcontrol based on daylight
availability and occupants behavior. Moreover, due to the great depth of the room the reference oﬃce
suggests a lighting strategy of separated lighting control groups, one closer to the window with depth
5m, consisting of two out of the three rows, while the back row is considered on its own another lighting
control zone. In this way, the front and back lighting groups will operate independently, saving energy.
Zone I is formed totally based on the reference oﬃce thus, the lighting layout, power and control groups
are as aforementioned. The resulting power density for this zone is distributed according to the lighting
power in each row and it is 11.11 W/m2 in the front lighting group and 8.68 W/m2 in the back lighting
group. Concerning zone II, the assumption made compared to the reference model was the reduction
of the zone depth from 8.2m to 5m. Thus, no separation in lighting control groups was made. As two
rows of work stations could ﬁt in this zone according to the layout of the reference oﬃce, two rows of
luminaires were assumed, meaning 200 W/m2 similar to the front lighting group of zone I. Even though,
the calculation area for a daylight analysis is required to cover the entire task area excluding a band of
0.5 m from the walls, in the simulations the whole area was taken into account and a calculation grid of
0.5x0.5 m was placed at 0.85 m above ﬂoor (Reinhart, 2011a).
The location of the sensor is a signiﬁcant factor for the reduction of electric lighting energy. For this
reason, the placement of daylight sensors was regarded as essential to create the lighting control proﬁle
based on the occupancy proﬁle. Proper placement of the sensor point for lighting control is within a ﬁeld
of view where direct light from the artiﬁcial lighting and sunlight is avoided. Otherwise, the sensor’s
readings will be oﬀand the electric lighting system should not operate in precision. Hence, according to
some general guidelines, the daylight sensor had to be placed centrally at a distance from the window of
1.5 times the eﬀective window height (Lutron, 2009). Concerning the back lighting control group in the
case of the zone I, a central placement for the sensor was considered suitable. However, the sensors, due
to the simulation software used, had to be one of the grid points. This means that in some cases the exact
placement of the sensor, according to the guidelines, could not be achieved, thus a modiﬁcation was made
and the sensor was placed in the closer point behind or next to the desired position.
Figure 3.3: Electric lighting layout in reference oﬃce (Reinhart, 2012)
==PAGE:60==
38
Methodology
’
Equipment
The total load for the equipment was considered to be 8 W/m2 corresponding to one Energy Star rated
LCD monitor and laptop per occupant present (Reinhart et al., 2013). In particular, an equipment load of 8
W/m2 was applied to the zone II. However, in case of the zone I, where two lighting zones were created, as
the ﬁrst step was to generate the lighting schedules after the annual daylight simulations, the equipment
load of 8 W/m2 was set in each of those lighting zones. As a result, the heat gains from the equipment
were increased according to the ﬂoor area. The equipment schedule followed the occupancy schedule.
3.1.6
Weather data
The assessment of building technologies at a given location through a building performance simulation
generally requires weather data series which represent a typical year deﬁned by means of a statistical
process (Jentsch, Eames, & Levermore, 2015). Design Reference Years (DRY) is the data ﬁle used in Den-
mark, with hourly weather data for 12 months and in particular, the Copenhagen DRY weather ﬁle was
used in the current simulation parametric-based study.
Thermal comfort and daylighting analysis are aﬀected by a full set of climatic variables. In particular,
climate variables that may inﬂuence overheating through solar gains are global and diﬀuse radiation lev-
els and cloud cover, while wind speed and direction will inﬂuence the ventilative eﬀect in cooling and
the heat transfer from the facade, and humidity will inﬂuence the evaporative cooling. Moreover, the
sunshine duration and elevated daytime ambient temperatures inﬂuence overheating risk as well (Taylor
et al., 2014). However, since the current simulation parametric-based study is using one weather ﬁle, it
focused solely on the impact of the dry bulb temperature on overheating risk. With respect to daylight
amounts, a weather ﬁle is necessary to perform dynamic daylight simulations. Speciﬁcally, external lu-
minous conditions are calculated for each hour of the year by converting global and diﬀuse irradiance
values contained in the weather ﬁle using a luminous eﬃcacy model and by generating a sky luminance
distribution through a sky model (Mardaljevic, 2000). In essence, importing a weather ﬁle for a daylight
simulation is generally the basic initial step, as it provides an accurate design through the integration
with electric light, leading to diﬀerent reduced heat gains for the diﬀerent design options.
3.2
Investigated parameters
The parameters of the investigation were chosen, based on those factors that aﬀect mostly the indoor
thermal environment and daylight availability with focus only on the building conﬁguration and facade
and was relied on those parameters described in section 2.5. Figure 3.4 shows which parameters were
chosen to be explored in this simulation study amongst all the design parameters that have an impact on
overheating and daylight conditions. Moreover, the selected investigated parameters were divided into
two categories:
• the ones that belong to the building conﬁguration determining the direction and the size of a zone,
which are the room depth and orientation, and
==PAGE:61==
3.2 Investigated parameters
39
• those that are linked to the facade design, regarding its fenestration and fabric characteristics, which
are the glazing area and type, the window sill and head height, and the thermal mass.
Figure 3.4: Cause and eﬀect diagram of parameters in overheating and daylight
In particular, the identiﬁcation of the design parameters that were implemented and investigated in the
present thesis was relied on those parameters described in section 2.5. However, the current project does
not include any information about the site, which means that a ﬂat site without any shade elements, such
as trees and surrounding buildings was chosen. This was a conscious decision since the site selection
depends on the project and it was considered unnecessary to implement a speciﬁc building site. Thus, an
assumption of the site selection could be invalid since it would specify the nature of the results. Moreover,
since the building location depends on the arrangement of the surroundings and the site, is not determined
in the present study either. A particular building layout was not deﬁned as well. Instead, the present
thesis focuses on the indoor thermal and visual comfort of a simple rectangular zone located in an oﬃce
building, and not on the entire building’s performance. The reason lied on the intended later usage of
the parametric design tool, which is its application in zones with diﬀerent geometrical features and thus,
right directions for designing a building layout will be given to the future user.
The variation levels of the investigated parameters checked in this project are listed in Table 3.7 with the
abbreviation used for naming the cases, and each of them are explained in detail below. The diﬀerent
variation levels of the selected parameters were decided based on simple and more common alternatives
in the early design phases. The variation levels for each parameter were decided to be generic, covering
some extremes that would be within a certain requirement range and described in sections below. Also
it was kept in mind what are the main and critical decision making in relation to each parameter. And
ﬁnally they have been adjusted to the scope and time-limit of the project and can be extended in future
steps due the particular design of the set up and the developed tool (section 4.3 and section 4.4).

the selected parameters, thus, an assumption of the site selection could be invalid since it would specify
the nature of the results. A particular building layout was not deﬁned either and as can be seen in section
3.1, the reference model is a simple side-lit and generic oﬃce room. However, as mentioned before, the
set up and workﬂow is made in a generic way that any building can be evaluated using the developed set
up and tool explained in sections 4.3 and 4.4.

subsections, are illustrated in the Figure 3.5 in order to visualize the number of combinations of this

between the design parameters. Particularly, as Figure 3.6 shows, 448 cases and 1024 cases were simulated
for zone I and zone II, respectively.

3.2.1
Zone depth
Oﬃce buildings in Denmark are usually made in modular construction, shaping similar zones. The zone
size plays a signiﬁcant role to the thermal indoor environment, since the distribution of the heat gains
can diﬀer in diﬀerent zone volumes aﬀecting the resulting operative temperature. Thus, two main zones
are chosen to be investigated.
The ﬁrst zone model, named zone I, was based on the exact size of the MIT reference oﬃce. The di-
mensions of this oﬃce room are 3.6x8.2x2.8m. Having in mind that the reference oﬃce was created
deliberately deep, more than three times the ﬂoor to ceiling height, to make visible the eﬀect of light redi-
recting facade technologies, another less deep zone, mentioned from now on as zone II, was considered
important to be modeled and investigated. Thus, keeping constant the width and height of the reference
oﬃce, an alternative oﬃce zone was designed with a depth of 5m. The decision of that depth was based
on the separation of the reference oﬃce in one lighting zone with a 5m depth and another one with the
remaining depth of 3.2m, as it is described in detail in section 3.1.5.
==PAGE:64==
42
Methodology
Figure 3.7: Parameter of zone depth
3.2.2
Orientation
Daylight admission is strongly dependent on a building’s orientation that controls the solar heat gains
as well (Ruck et al., 2000). Orientation plays a signiﬁcant role in passive solar architectural concepts and
should be known from the time of the site selection as it is impractical and cost-eﬀective to reorient a
building later(Level, 2019).
Therefore, all possible cardinal and ordinal orientations were investigated in order to examine not only
the daylight potential, but also the overheating risk in each facade’s direction since diﬀerent orienta-
tions experience varying solar exposure and thus, varying solar heat gains. Figure 3.8 shows the eight
parameters of orientation, which were included in the simulation study.
Therefore, information about the positioning of a buildings with respect to suﬃcient amount of daylight
and minimum solar heat gains for preventing overheating will be extracted.
Figure 3.8: Parameter of orientation
==PAGE:65==
3.2 Investigated parameters
43
3.2.3
Thermal mass
Thermal mass is not a one-sided issue and as a result, careful planning is required from the planning
phase, in order to achieve an optimal design, as its probable future addition to the existing construction of
a building may charge the static system of the construction and reduce the interior space. So, the thermal
mass was chosen as a design parameter of the facade to investigate its contribution to the reduction of
overheating hours during the oﬃce hours in summertime.
Instead of applying the parameter of the thermal mass in all the construction elements, it was only applied
on the facade. Two diﬀerent constructions for the external wall were examined, one from sandwich
panel (concrete - insulation - concrete) with "TM1" abbreviation, and one from unitised facade (steel
panel - insulation - steel panel) with "TM2" abbreviation. These two thermal masses were intended to be
evaluated and they were set as the most common construction types for oﬃce buildings from experience
in the industrial building sector.
Figure 3.9: Parameter of thermal mass
Aiming for checking the ability of the construction materials of concrete and aluminium to absorb and
emit heat to the interior zone, the insulation thickness in both cases was kept constant to 0.20 m. How-
ever, the ﬁrst and last layer of the wall construction are those which diﬀer in thickness and properties
altering the ﬁnal U-value of the wall and its thermal mass. It should be noted that some wall layers were
not taken into account to the simulations for simpliﬁcation reasons, since their thickness and thermal
resistance are of a minimum signiﬁcance. For instance, installing ﬁnishes could reduce the eﬀectiveness
of the thermal mass. Therefore, the thermal mass materials were consciously exposed to the internal and
external environment in order to provide clear information about how much the thermal mass controls
the overheating levels by reducing the overheating hours.
Both cases comply with the Danish building regulations that require a U-value for the external wall sur-
faces of maximum 0.3 W/m2K. However, taken into account the requirements for the design transmission
loss in W per m2 of the paragraph 476 referring to the voluntary low energy class 2020, this value is re-
duced signiﬁcantly as for the whole building envelope excluding the windows and doors the rate shall not
exceed 5.7 W/m2 (BR18, 2018). That means that for a design temperature diﬀerence of 25°C the minimum
U-value of the building envelope is calculated to 0.2 W/m2K. Thus, a slightly lower value of loss trans-
mission was chosen to be applied, taken also into consideration the thickness of the insulation, placed
in new buildings is approximately 20cm. The thicknesses of the layers along with the U-value of each
parameter of external wall are presented in Table 3.8, while the thermal properties of the layers are pre-
sented in Table 3.9. In fact, the insulating properties of the two external wall’s constructions do not diﬀer
that much, as the U-values of the two parameters of thermal mass are quite close. After all, the purpose
==PAGE:66==
44
Methodology
was not to check the heat ﬂow into or out of the building through the building envelope, but the ability
of the building envelope to store and re-release heat throughout a day. Essentially, the thickness, density
and speciﬁc heat capacity of the materials are the ones which are supposed to aﬀect the lag times and
thus, the overheating levels.
Table 3.8: Parameter of the thermal mass of exterior wall

0.174963
In general, higher material density leads to higher thermal mass (Reardon, Caitlin, & Geoﬀ, 2013). As can
be seen in Table 3.9, the density of the steel is higher than the one of concrete. However, the weight of
the external concrete wall is greater (5040 kg) than the weight of the external unitised facade (2359 kg),
ignoring the glazing area. Moreover, the concrete wall is thicker and has a higher heat capacity, so this
thermal mass can store more heat for a longer time than the unitised panel. This means that the thermal
mass of the sandwich panel is expected to lead to less temperature ﬂuctuation and less overheating hours.

3.2.4
Window type
By the term window type, all properties of a glazing unit, that aﬀect in any way the energy transfer
between indoors and outdoors, are denoted. Glazing has several functions in the facade and building.
Daylight penetration and access to view are two of glazing foremost functions. As an attribute to energy
concerns, one of the key functions of the glazing is also to limit heat losses. These two can be addressed
separately as there are diﬀerent aspects of the solar gains that contribute to each.
Figure 3.10: Graphical illustration of the distinct penetration of light and energy through a window type.
The capacity of the window conﬁguration to resist this heat transfer through overall conduction, convec-
tion, and long-wave radiation is referred to as its U-value. More speciﬁcally, U-value represents the rate
of heat transfer through a square meter of matter, in a temperature diﬀerence of one degree Kelvin, mea-
sured in W/m2K. The U-value of the composition of the window is the combined thermal transmittance of
the glass panes and the inter-pane spaces with the frames of the window. As long as for the glazing part
only, its U-value is inversely proportional to the thermal resistance of the parts of the window, depending
on the number of panes, their dimensions, the type of gas within their cavity, and the characteristic of
coatings on the various glazing surfaces. Thus, the glazing part of a window has diﬀerent thermal resis-
tance from the total window assembly (Eﬃcient Windows Collaborative, 2011). This characteristic of the
window has a great impact on the conservation of energy required for heating.
Additionally to the previous glazing characteristic, the solar heat gain coeﬃcient (SHGC) plays a signif-
icant role in the energy performance of a window. This coeﬃcient expresses the ability of the glazing
to control the penetration of some solar radiation that is responsible for the heat gains. Consequently, it
aﬀects substantially both cooling and heating loads of buildings. As a coeﬃcient, it is unit-less and ranges
from 0 to 1. The higher this value is, the higher are the solar heat gains. A part of the solar radiation,
direct and indirect, is reﬂected and other is absorbed by the panes depending on the type and number of
glazing panes, as well as on the occurrence of any coating. Typical values of SHGC varies from more than
0.8 for uncoated white clear glass, being reduced slightly by applying Low-E coatings and signiﬁcantly
when adding a tint up to 0.2 (Eﬃcient Windows Collaborative, 2011). It should be noted that the frame
of the window has a very low SHGC resulting to reduced values of SHGC when referring to the whole
window.
==PAGE:68==
46
Methodology
Concerning the visible part of the solar spectrum with visual eﬀects, the amount of visual solar radiation
that penetrates the glazing unit is referred to as the visible transmittance (Tvis) and is that property
of the glazing, responsible for the illumination of the space. High values of Tvis mean high levels of
daylight inside the zone, reducing the need for electrical lighting and its associated heat gains. Similarly
to the SHGC, the Tvis is inﬂuenced by the number, type and coating of the panes. Again, the Tvis of the
assembly of the window diﬀers from the glazing part only, since the frame area has zero Tvis (Eﬃcient
Windows Collaborative, 2011).
The ratio of visible transmittance to solar heat gain coeﬃcient reﬂects the ability of a window type to
separate and control diﬀerently the visible transmittance and the solar heat gains. Until recently, since
both of these factors depend on the same glazing properties and characteristics, they were inseparably
linked to each other and a reduction of one of them meant simultaneously the reduction of the other one.
However, an innovation of the glazing industry has allowed the reduction of solar heat gains, without
having to compromise the visible transmittance. Thus, as it is depicted in Figure 3.10 light and energy
transmittance can be controlled separately, resulting to diﬀerent portion of light and energy to penetrate
the glazing unit.
The selected window conﬁgurations, simulated in this thesis, were all, construction of 3-panes with argon
gas (90%) with a 16mm cavity between them and the combination of panes was formed based on recom-
mendations of the company. The chosen window types are widely used in Denmark and were selected
from the online program Pilkington Spectrum in the site of Pilkington Danmark A/S (NSG Group, 2019).
Four parameters of window type were chosen to be investigated and their selection has been based on
the two latter characteristic values of the ﬁrst external pane, as they have been described above, while
the other panes were kept more or less similar in all cases in order to keep the U-value constant to 0.6
W/m2K, enabling the evaluation of the eﬀect of the other properties. At this point, it should be mentioned
that the U-value in the simulations was considered equal to 0.9 W/m2K, since the U-value of the window
frames is usually much higher than the glazing one, as it is given from the manufacturer, thus the eﬀect
of the frame in all thermal evaluations could not be neglected. In contrast, the eﬀect of the frame for
the daylight analysis is disregarded since the shading eﬀect of the frames plays a minor role in the early
design phases, where the division of glazed area is not determined. Moreover, this decision facilitates the
simulation ﬂow and simpliﬁes the needed decision making in the initial design.
In Table 3.12 the characteristic values of the chosen window types are presented, while in Appendix A
are the ﬁles of the created glazing units in Pilkington Spectrum.

3.2.5
Glazing to ﬂoor ratio
The size of the glazing of the windows is one of the most important indicators on the energy performance
of buildings, since it aﬀects not only the solar heat gains but also the thermal heat losses from the opening.
==PAGE:69==
3.2 Investigated parameters
47
Therefore, the choice of the glazing area has a strong inﬂuence on the cooling energy demand and the
thermal indoor conditions (BR18, 2018). Additionally, larger glazing surface areas let more daylight enter
a space, and as a result reduce the required energy demand for lighting.
The glazing area was determined according to BR18, which state two solutions estimating that they can
provide roughly the same suﬃcient daylight level. The ﬁrst solution deﬁnes a glazing surface of min-
imum 10 per cent of the relevant ﬂoor area. However, the minimum glazing area should be corrected
for the conditions that reduce daylight access to a window. On the contrary, there are other situations
that utilizes daylight entry signiﬁcantly through several glazing surfaces, such as side-lit windows and
skylight. The glazing area should also be corrected for these situations (Johnsen & Lumbye, 2018). The
second alternative solution for suﬃcient daylight provision documents that the internal illuminance val-
ues should be 300 lux for at least half the relevant ﬂoor area for at least half of the daylight hours (BR18,
2018) as mentioned in section 2.4. Based on the above mentioned requirements, it was considered of high
interest to investigate if the results of daylight access through the minimum glazing area correspond to
the second solution of suﬃcient daylight. So, the glazing to ﬂoor ratio was set as a parameter to determine
the area of the glass that is applied on the facade, with a range as Figure 3.11 depicts.
Figure 3.11: Graphical illustration of parameter of glazing to ﬂoor ratio
The glazing area conﬁgurations were modelled, ﬁrstly, by the corrected minimum glazing to ﬂoor ra-
tio according to Danish guidelines. More speciﬁcally, in Traﬃc, Construction and Housing Agency’s
Guide on Light and Visibility, the most common conditions that contribute to both daylight reduction
and greater daylight exploitation are described. Their correction factors are stated there that indicate the
required glazing area. The correction due to reduced daylight penetration is implied for the reduced light
transmittance of the pane, the shadowing eﬀect, the room depth and the thickness of wall where the win-
dow is placed. On the other hand, the correction due to the improved daylight amounts is indicated for
the glazing surfaces in multi-faceted windows and skylight. Basically, the corrected glazing area should
be equal or higher than the minimum glazing area of 10% of the ﬂoor area with respect to the daylight
suﬃciency, and is calculated based on Equation 3.2 (Johnsen & Lumbye, 2018). However, the minimum
glazing area of 10% of the ﬂoor area was adjusted by those correction factors concerning only the reduced
light transmittance, the wall thickness of the window and the room depth, since the rest conditions are
not existing in this study.

Since a lower light transmittance is indicated for many windows by the window manufacturer, they must
be corrected for a lower light transmittance than the reference one, which is 0.75. The correction factor
of the light transmittance is calculated according to the Equation 3.3 (Johnsen & Lumbye, 2018). The light
transmittance of each of the parameter of the window type was applied and it was corrected accordingly.

Furthermore, the correction of the wall thickness of the window is of great importance since increasing

0.26 m and 0.50 m. Regarding the glass area, the minimum glazing area of 10% of the ﬂoor area for each
zone corrected for each of the parameter of light transmittance, was taken into account. The resulting
glazing area was roughly more than 2m2 in every case. So, no correction for the thickness of the wall was
considered.

Rooms with depth more than 5m are rarely experienced as well lit, as there is a considerable contrast in
daylight between the area near the window and area at the rear of the room. In particular, the size of

distance from the window (Johnsen & Lumbye, 2018). Since a large room depth of 8.2m is investigated in
this project, its correction was taken into account for the calculation of the required glazing area. Table

At this point, it is necessary to bear in mind the glazing area should be less than the area of the external
wall where it is applied. The area of the external wall surface for both zones is the same and is 10.08
m2. However, the glazing surface area is dependent on the ﬂoor, hence is diﬀerent for each zone. After
calculating the glazing area resulting from each glazing to ﬂoor ratio as Table 3.16 indicates, it was found
that a glazing area of 35% of the ﬂoor area of zone I is larger than the external wall surface. Therefore,
==PAGE:72==
50
Methodology
the percentages of 35% and 45% were excluded from the simulation model of zone I. As regards zone II,
the glazing to ﬂoor ratio of 45% gives a glazing area, which already covers 80% of the wall surface. In case
of adding a glazing to ﬂoor ratio of 55% in the range of this parameter, the glazing to wall ratio would be
98%, which means a window including the frame construction could not ﬁt. Therefore, only these four
parameters of percentages of glazing to ﬂoor area were selected in this study. Moreover, it is important
to clarify that the case of the window type with a visible transmittance of 0.46 applied to a 25% of glazing
to ﬂoor ratio in zone I results approximately in the same glazing area with the minimum glazing to ﬂoor
ratio for the same visible transmittance. For this reason and since the required glazing area resulted from
the minimum glazing to ﬂoor ratio is slightly higher, the case of the second parameter of glazing to ﬂoor
ratio (25%) is excluded for the ﬁrst parameter of window type with a visible transmittance of 0.46.
3.2.6
Window placement
The placement of the window and the distribution of the glazed area in the facade have a major con-
tribution as far as the daylighting in the zone is concerned. However, in terms of the indoor thermal
environment, as long as the glazing area remains constant, the heating gains from solar radiation are,
more or less, also, same, as well as, the radiant temperature of the external wall. Thus, the operative
temperature in the middle of the zone remains almost the same. Many guidelines and research papers
conclude that the higher a window head is, the deeper can a zone be lit, whereas areas of glazing below
a certain height have a minimum eﬀect (Hasim Altan & Jitka Mohelnikova, 2015) (Robinson & Selkowitz,
2013). The correlation of the depth of the room to the window head height is suggested to be from 2
times bigger to 3.5 depending on the window placement and sky conditions (Ibrahim & Hayman, 2005).
Moreover, the daylight illuminance levels and uniformity in space are inﬂuenced by the height of the
window sill and head (Zomorodian et al., 2016).
Having these facts in mind and in order to better understand and evaluate the consequences of these
factors under the speciﬁed spectrum of daylighting in oﬃce buildings in the Danish climate and stan-
dards, two diﬀerent heights for window sill and head were chosen to be simulated for each case. The
combinations that arose are illustrated in Figure 3.12. It must be denoted that the total area of the glazing
is determined by the glazing ratio parameter, thus, what is changed by the application of these two more
parameters is the way that the total window area is distributed in the facade. The window sill is set to
either 0m or 1m above the interior ﬂoor level, whereas the head alters from as high as the ceiling of the
room is, meaning 2.8m in the current model, to the ordinary door height of 2.2m. Therefore, the width of
the window is automatically adjusted to the height keeping the total window area constant. Moreover,
although having the position of the window in terms of horizontal position as a variable would change
the daylight distribution in space, the overheating hours would not be aﬀected. However, the center of
the facade is considered the optimum for the window placement (Vogiatzi, 2016). Thus, in all cases, the
windows are placed centered to the width of the zone as a ﬁrst design option.
==PAGE:73==
3.3 Model seting for circadian light metrics
51
Figure 3.12: Graphical illustration of parameter of head and sill height
3.3
Model setting for circadian light metrics
The attempt of the thesis by adding the simulation of non-visual health potential was to raise awareness
about the design of healthier buildings rather than to deﬁne conclusively the circadian stimulus. More-
over, the novel human-centric approach for predicting non-visual health potential was integrated into the
method of this thesis, to provide information for a more holistic evaluation of the daylight performance
in buildings. The data were collected based on studies and also, were assumed by the authors. The level
of this simulation was quite simpliﬁed in order to make an objective assessment of the human circadian
response to sunlight.
It is considered necessary to mention that the simulation was carried out in 3-channels rather than 9-
channels, which would improve the lighting simulation accuracy. The reason is that multi-spectral light-
ing simulations are time-consuming. These three channels were post-processed by the photopic curve
V(휆) to derive photopic luminance and illuminance values per pixel, and by the circadian spectral sen-
sitivity curves C(휆) to derive circadian luminance and illuminance values per pixel. Calculation of the
circadian-units along with photopic-units was deemed necessary to be employed in this study to evalu-
ate the non-visual and visual responses to the zone.
To assess the eﬀects of human exposure to daylight in terms of non-visual health potential, a predeﬁned
position and view of an occupant inside the zone, had to be selected. The layout of the working spaces
of the reference model, as described above, was taken into consideration for the chosen position. The
simulations were made for the occupant sitting in the middle row of oﬃces, looking at one of the side
walls, as it is depicted in Figure 3.13. This assumption was made to avoid the extreme cases of sitting next
to the window or, in contrast, to the back of the zone. So, the occupant was set to have a view direction to
the side walls avoiding direct sun exposure to represent a more realistic case. Moreover, this assumption
was done because in case that the person was facing the window, the expected circadian values would
reach mostly the maximum levels and thus, the assessment of the results would be biased.
==PAGE:74==
52
Methodology
Figure 3.13: Eye position and view direction of occupants
As the circadian responses are synchronized with the 24 hour daily cycle, a daily time series approach to
simulation was needed. In this way, the circadian variations throughout a day were examined. For the
evaluation of a typical working schedule in oﬃce buildings, six hours within a day, with intervals of two
hours, were selected starting from 8:00 till 18:00, in order to consider the duration of human exposure
to sunlight. It is also assumed that the person enters at 8:00 and leaves at 18:00 in order to estimate the
non-visual light consumed over time. At this pint, it should be stressed out that daylight hours before or
after the deﬁned period are not considered in the analysis. Figure 3.14 portrays the selected date and time
instances within the working schedule, showing also the duration of sunlit hours during the whole year.
According to this ﬁgure, the assessment was made for 4 days, representing a symmetrical half of the year.
In particular, the dates selected are January 13th, February 28th, April 14th and May 30th (Amundadottir
et al., 2017).
Figure 3.14: Selected date and time instances for the evaluation of health potentials
==PAGE:75==
3.3 Model seting for circadian light metrics
53
Moreover, the simulation was performed under two typical sky types, the clear and overcast. These
two sky types can reﬂect the impact of diﬀerent weather conditions that might occur during the year in
Denmark. For the generation of the sky models the spectral power distribution of CIE standard indoor
illuminant ID65 was assumed. This assumed daylight source was set to have a constant color tempera-
ture of 6500 K, which is typically the Correlated Color Temperature (CCT) of an overcast sky (Inanici et
al., 2015). Finally, the photometric material properties of the zone surfaces, as deﬁned in section 3.1.1,
composed a neutral gray scale color scene. This assumption prevented the change of the spectral power
distribution of daylight source, which can be aﬀected by the material colors before it reaches the eye.
Thus, the information that were acquired about the spectral power distribution are based on the illumi-
nance values and not on the color temperature of the sky and materials. In this way, the sensitivity of
non-visual system can be observed more clearly, which is higher to blue light, compared to visual sys-
tem. This approach can overcome the limitations of existing studies that cannot accurately predict the
circadian light levels, thus their daylight models are limited to the calculation of photometric quantities
(Amundadottir et al., 2017).
It must be mentioned that for reducing simulation time, and since the occupant’s position is representative
of both zone I and zone II, only zone I was evaluated. Moreover, as this assessment is added to the
daylight analysis performed based on criteria of Danish building regulation, some cases were chosen to
be simulated. So, instead of simulating the full set of the combined parameters, only those cases that
yielded extreme overheating conditions were chosen and are reported in Table 3.17.
Table 3.17: Simulated cases for the assessment of human circadian responses
Case name
performance in overheating risk
I_TM2_lowe_S0_Hmax_R25
worst
I_TM1_5025_S0_Hmin_Rmin
best
Firstly, the case with the worst overheating hours was identiﬁed. This case was applied to southeast ori-
entation and a facade with the lighter thermal mass. Moreover, the highest visible transmittance applied
to a glazing area of 7.38 m2 (Tvis of 0.73 and 25% glazing ratio) with the same height of the facade’s height.
These factors led to the highest overheating risk. One more case was intended to be assessed in case of
best overheating scenario. This case consists of a glazing area of 7.38 m2, which is applied for the lowest
visible transmittance (Tvis of 0.46 and 25% glazing ratio). The best case in the overheating problem was
oriented to north, with the glazing surface placed at a sill height of 1 m. However, since this case had an
insigniﬁcant overheating diﬀerence of 1 hour with the case with a glazing surface at a sill height of 0 m,
which represents the worst case in daylight performance, the latter sill height was considered. In this way,
the upper and lower limits of the expected values were speciﬁed, and all the rest cases were assumed to
range between them. What is more, it was decided to keep orientation as a single parameter because the
available simulation time for this additional analysis was restricted. So, the combined parameters of the
southeast and north orientation were taken into account for the simulation changing only the orientation
degrees. It is considered remarkable to mention that these two cases concern a glazing surface with the
same glazing to ﬂoor ratio, but with diﬀerent window types. Hence an assessment of the window type
could be possible as well.
==PAGE:76==
==PAGE:77==
Chapter 4
Parametric design tool
The lack of studies that incorporate daylighting and overheating strategies within an integrated context
calls for the development of a tool to handle this problem. As mentioned earlier, the overall goal is to
evaluate design performance parameters and elucidate potential synergies preventing overheating risks
and maximizing daylight potentials. With a clear deﬁnition of the purpose of the project, the process
followed to implement an integrated daylighting and overheating approach was governed by a combina-
tion of the systematic and argumentative engineering rationality of the authors. The process is made up
of a series of iterative loops, which support the reciprocal action among the parameters, speciﬁcations,
and requirements. This parametric process minimizes the need for subsequent time and cost-consuming
revisions later in the design. Once again, this project is not engaged in optimizing solutions to the prob-
lems. Alternatively, it speciﬁes the problems caused by non-agreement with the standards and guidelines,
through the tool, which serves the overall objectives of the project.
This chapter outlines solely the development of the parametric-design tool through Honeybee, Ladybug
and Lark Spectral Lighting plugins for Grasshopper 3D in Rhino after the whole set of parameters and
model speciﬁcations described in chapter 3. This was considered paramount in order to provide an an-
alytical description of the simulation methodology in an illustrative way. An additional reason was to
procure information about those inputs that were set in the tool based on the software’s requirements or
limitations. In other words, this chapter describes the method that was followed to implement an algo-
rithm in Grasshopper 3D for Rhino for calculating the daylight metrics and overheating hours based on
Danish criteria.
In the following sections, the parametric design tool is described analytically via print screens and com-
ments, which represent only the main parts of the entire workﬂow developed in Grasshopper 3D platform.
4.1
Main characteristics
Designers can make use of this tool by establishing diﬀerent width, height and depth for the room, sizing
the glazing area related to the ﬂoor area, positioning the glazing based on the sill height and head height
that they decide and also, applying the desired glazing and a construction type on the facade. The tool has
the merit of setting complex relationships between diﬀerent parameters and being based on a considerable
quantity of annual climate-based and radiance-based simulations. At the same time, it is a simple and
quick-to-use tool for the early design stages when designers can follow step by step the daylighting and
==PAGE:78==
56
Parametric design tool
overheating workﬂow in order to quantify the eﬀect of the facade on overheating and daylighting. Before
getting into a detailed description, the main characteristics of the parametric design tool are presented
below.
1. All the components of the plug-ins of Grasshopper 3D can be seen as a whole presenting a syn-
thetic picture of all the necessary elements implemented for the establishment of an appropriate
daylighting and thermal comfort analysis.
2. This tool is characterized by the integration of two simulation analyses. The computation of ther-
mal indoor environment was dependent on daylighting and these two analyses were set in one
workﬂow, meaning that the tool was developed in one single design environment. However, even
though the workﬂow of Lark Spectral Lighting was relied on the speciﬁcations and parameters set
for the daylighting analysis, it is considered independent.
3. The link between these two analyses is the implementation of the lighting control, which reduces
the electricity consumption for artiﬁcial lighting depending on the daylight availability. So, the
internal heat gains can be reduced, leading to less overheating and thus to energy savings.
4. The tool simply shows the steps for an overheating and daylighting simulation, hence gives the
information for what is important to be assessed.
5. The tool determines combinations of the investigated parameters that are able to provide high,
acceptable or low daylight and overheating levels within a zone.
6. The parameters are connected with the speciﬁcations of the model creating multiple relationships
among the inputs, by taking into account every factor that can aﬀect the results.
7. The inﬂuence of zone depth, orientation, window type and glazing area depending on the ﬂoor
area, window sill and head height can be assessed on the daylight availability and overheating risk.
8. This type of tool has an intended audience architect which can verify quickly the inﬂuence of the
investigated design parameters on daylight and overheating conditions within simple oﬃce envi-
ronments. It can be useful for the designer to make conscious decisions on preventing overheating
while maximizing the daylight potentials in the early design stages improving the design.
9. It can also be used as a research tool, since various designs can be compared and their agreement
with the Danish regulations can be veriﬁed.
10. The variables, their ranges and the model speciﬁcations are complied with the Danish codes and
can be ﬁxed according to the designer’s needs in every design step.
11. The layout of the tool was intended to be as simpliﬁed as possible considering that the workﬂow has
a great complexity due to the multiple relationships between the inputs. Therefore, the description
of the parametric design tool is structured in a way separating the inputs from the outputs.
12. It constitutes a starting point for a wider study on drawing guidelines based on Danish indoor
climate requirements.
13. The tool itself provides an immediate reading of the parametric analysis results allowing a design
team to make preliminary design decisions on the daylight amount and overheating risk.
==PAGE:79==
4.2 Simulation workflow
57
14. It provides visual graphs of the results for every combination of the design parameters. More specif-
ically, the hourly operative temperature of the zone for all the year is illustrated in a 3D chart (Figure
B.1). A monthly bar chart is also shown with all the building-wide energy balance terms, such as
heating, solar, lighting, people, inﬁltration, mechanical ventilation, conduction, cooling, and stor-
age energy (Figure B.2). A colored mesh with numerical data as regards the daylight autonomy, the
maximum daylight autonomy, and the classes of the useful daylight illuminance was also visualized
(Figure B.3).
4.2
Simulation workﬂow
Figure 4.1 represents the simulation software that were used in the simulation tool. Particularly, the
building energy and daylight simulations were carried out through EnergyPlus and DAYSIM, respectively,
using Honeybee and Ladybug plug-ins. Moreover, a workﬂow for calculating the circadian metrics was
added using Lark Spectral Lighting. Additionally, basic principles of Python were used to write functions
or conditions of the script that were not possible to work through any of the components of Grasshopper
and also, to automate the simulation process. Finally, the simulation results were arranged in Excel ﬁles
through Excel Writer of TT Toolbox plugin. TT Toolbox features a range of diﬀerent tools, but only the
Excel Writer tool was used among them. Through Excel Writer, the data were written in Excel through
some option settings. For example, the simulation results were saved in ﬁles that were created in speciﬁc
folders, and also they were inserted in speciﬁc columns or rows.
Figure 4.1: Relations between engines and plug-ins of Grasshopper 3D in Rhino
The methodologies that were followed in this thesis aiming for evaluation of the overheating risk and day-
light availability together with health potential from sunlight exposure, were implemented in a simulation
tool where diﬀerent design parameters were set and iterated providing results for multiple combinations
of those options. Figure 4.2 presents a simpliﬁed overview of the general workﬂow that was used in
Grasshopper 3D to emphasize the complexity of the tool.
==PAGE:80==

Parametric design tool
Figure 4.2: Main ﬂowchart for the parametric design tool
4.3
Inputs of script
This section provides an analytical description of the connections between the inputs and the compo-
nents of Grasshopper 3D. These components are written in italics to be separated from the rest text.
After letting the Honeybee and Ladybug ﬂy, the weather ﬁle was imported online through the component
Ladybug_Open EPW And STAT Weather Files from https://www.energyplus.net.
Figure 4.3: Honeybee, Ladybug and Open EPW And STAT Weather Files components
==PAGE:81==
4.3 Inputs of script
59
Figure 4.4: Set up of the direction ﬁle and case name
An essential part of the script is the creation of the ﬁle named by each of the investigated parameters
as Figure 4.4 depicts. In this case, the results that arose for each conﬁguration of the design parameters
were saved in a speciﬁc Excel ﬁle in a folder determined by the authors. More speciﬁcally, the ranges of
each parameter were written in a Panel and were connected as an input in List Item with an index to be
the number of the parameter in the list which is currently simulated. This number is deﬁned through a
Number Slider, which is primarily used for setting the variation levels of the parameters.
4.3.1
Zone composition and surrounding surfaces
Regarding the composition of the models, the ﬁrst step was the creation of the basic geometry of the two
diﬀerent investigated zones, that would represent the reference oﬃce and its alternative design with less
depth, named henceforward as zone I and zone II respectively.
==PAGE:82==
60
Parametric design tool
Figure 4.5: Set up of the geometry of zone I
Figure 4.6: Set up of the geometry of zone II
The geometry on both zones is generated by placing a point in [0,0] and the desired height above the
ground, at 2.8 m, using the Construct Point. Using this point in space as an origin, a plane perpendicular in
the z axis is launched by the Plane Normal, in order a rectangular of the zones dimensions to be grounded.
The Rectangular takes as input of x the width of the zones and as y their depth.
In the case of the zone I, two Rectangular components had to be used in order to create two subzones, one
in the front of the room and one in the back, as it was necessary for creating two diﬀerent light control
groups in this zone, as described in subsection 3.1.5. The depth of each one of the subzones is deﬁned by
two Number Sliders, one for the depth of the front zone at 5 m, and another for the total depth of zone I
at 8.2 m, meaning that subtracting from the total the depth of the ﬁrst subzone the depth of the second
one can be found and implemented in the component, as it is depicted in Figure 4.5.
Finally, the zones were deﬁned using a Box Rectangular and an input of the clear height of the zone to
2.8 m. For zone II, the two created boxes had to be next to each other being connected to one of their
surfaces. Hence, the back subzone was moved by the depth of the ﬁrst sub-section, with Move component.
==PAGE:83==
4.3 Inputs of script
61
It must be noted that all connections and relations among dimensions and components are automated and
driving solely by the 4 or 5 Number Sliders representing the height of the ground, the width, depth and
height of the zone, and in case of zone I also the depth of the ﬁrst subzone.
As already mentioned, the ground surface is of high importance to be set up in a daylight analysis. Re-
specting the guidelines, the external ground was created by the 4Point Surface, deﬁning its corners with
Construct Points, whose coordinates were set up according to the width (x) and depth (y) of each zone.
Mathematical relations among these dimensions were established to form the desired size of the external
ground and enable an automatic adjustment of ground size to the zone’s dimensions. The coordinates of
this surface in relation to the zone’s dimensions are illustrated in Figure 4.7 for better understanding the
description below.
Figure 4.7: Graphical illustration of external ground
The ground should outspread in front of the zone’s facade and in each of its sides three times. Therefore,
from the starting point of the zones in the ground level, which is the point [0,0,0], one corner of the
ground is placed to minus three times the width and depth, while the opposite corner is set to be at four
times the width and 1 meter more of the depth.
==PAGE:84==
62
Parametric design tool
Figure 4.8: Set up of the external ground
A similar process was applied to create the facade of the building, part of which the zone is supposed to
be. Two surfaces with the corresponding coordinates are set up in each side of the zone, while for the
formation of the above and underneath facade the Extrude component was used. Firstly, with Deconstruct
Brep the top and bottom edge of the geometry of the Box Rectangular were selected by the List Items
and connected to Join Curves. Then, they are extruded in Z-axis (in negative and positive value) by the
Amplitude by the value of the height of the zone.
Figure 4.9: Set up of the building facade
4.3.2
EnergyPlus and Radiance Materials
The deﬁnition of the materials as described in subsection 3.1.3 was set up through three components for
the Energy simulation and two for the corresponding daylight.
==PAGE:85==
4.3 Inputs of script
63
As far as the opaque materials for EnergyPlus are concerned, the parameters that have to be deﬁned in
Honeybee_EnergyPlus Opaque Material are the roughness, thickness, conductivity, density, speciﬁc heat,
thermal absorbance, and ﬁnally the absorbance of solar radiation and of the visible light. These properties
of the materials, created for the energy simulations, are reported in the Table 4.1. The thermal absorbance,
the absorbance of solar radiation and of the visible light are similar in all materials, set to the defaults
of the Honeybee_EnergyPlus Opaque Material, since these values are the most representatives for non
metallic materials. For clarity reasons, the thermal absorbance was set to 0.9, whereas the absorbance of
both solar radiation and of visible light to 0.7.
