Figure 4.11: Set up of the Energy Plus window materials
The construction of the ﬁnal building elements is assembled by the Honeybee_EnergyPlus Construction,
as depicted in Figure 4.10. The resulting U-value and thickness of the construction are derived from Hon-
eybee_Decompose EP construction and from Mass Addition, respectively. The glass material is represented
by a single layer of Honeybee_EnergyPlus Window Materials, with the solar heat gain coeﬃcient and vis-
ible transmittance taken by the manufacturer, while U-value is set to 0.9 W/(mK) for all window types,
as it has already been mentioned in subsection 3.2.4. The glass material is connected through Honey-
bee_EnergyPlus Construction as well. All the EnergyPlus constructions of the glass material are added in
List Item. The minimum and maximum numeric value is added in a Number Slider that represents the list
item, as is displayed in Figure 4.11.
Concerning the opaque materials for Radiance, the same value of diﬀuse reﬂectance for red, green and
blue color was given in Honeybee_Radiance Opaque Material. In Honeybee_Radiance Glass Material the
transmittance of the glass material for the three colors was given based on the visible transmittance. So,
as can be seen in Figure 4.12, four glass materials were also created for Radiance that, once more, were
connected to a List Item with the same Number Slider that controlled the window type parameter.
==PAGE:87==
4.3 Inputs of script
65
Figure 4.12: Set up of the Radiance opaque and glass materials
The material of each zone surface was assigned to Honeybee_createHBSrfs component. As input in ge-
ometry the face of the Box Rectangular, that corresponds to the desired zone surface through Deconstruct
Brep and List Item, was applied. As surface type, "0" is used for a wall, "2" for a ﬂoor, "3" for a ceiling.
Also, the heat ﬂow through the surface was deﬁned in EPBC by setting "Outdoors" for the external wall
and "Adiabatic" for all the rest surfaces. In this component, the EnergyPlus constructions and Radiance
opaque and glass materials were applied, either as text values in a Panel, in case that materials have been
added to the EnergyPlus library with the corresponding component, or directly by the created material
as in case of Radiance Material component. As can be seen in Figure 4.13, the parameter of thermal mass
is set through the List Item and Number Slider.
Figure 4.13: Assignment of material properties
==PAGE:88==
66
Parametric design tool
In the case of zone I, it is essential to highlight that the type of surface that was assigned in the face of each
one of the subzones that coincide, was the Air Wall, represented by the number "4". Due to the signiﬁcance
of creating two diﬀerent lighting control groups and applying two diﬀerent lighting schedules in one
thermal zone, it was considered imperative to separate the zone into two adjacent subzones. Otherwise,
EnergyPlus takes into account one lighting schedule, even though two lighting schedules are applied for
the energy simulation. The air wall was located in a distance of 5 m from the window to allow a subzone
of 3.6 x 5.0 m to be created in in zone I. This is the lighting zone in the front, while the rest area constitutes
the lighting zone in the back which is 3.6 x 3.2 m. Figure 4.14 illustrates the Air Wall in zone I.
Figure 4.14: Location of air wall in zone I
Air wall is a virtual partition surface, which allows the automatic mixture of the air between the zones.
This mixing is just a constant ﬂow based on the surface area between the zones connected by air wall.
This interzone surface of air wall allows conduction of sensible heat between two zones, since it has low
thermal resistance, helping to move radiant exchange between two zones. No solar gains passed in the
back subzone since the only way to pass solar and daylight from one zone to the next is through glass
door or window (Any, Or, & Any, 2016). The simulations clariﬁed it since solar gains passed only in the
ﬁrst zone. Detailed analysis of the air wall is presented in chapter 4.
4.3.3
Generation of Honeybee zone and window
To deﬁne the zone for the simulations, all the Honeybee_createHBSrfs components were connected to
Honeybee_createHBZones, as it is depicted in Figure 4.15. In the case of the zone I, two components were
used for generating the subzones. It is possible to visualize the given names of the Honeybee_creatHBSrfs
in Rhino by connecting the Honeybee_Label Zone Surfaces, as it can be seen in Figure 4.14, where the
names of the surfaces of the back subzone are labeled. This component is useful to check if the mate-
rial and construction of each surface have been applied correctly. This zone constructed by the Honey-
bee_createHBZones component is of high signiﬁcance since all rest settings are produced for this zone as
==PAGE:89==
4.3 Inputs of script
67
a whole.
Figure 4.15: Composition of Honeybee zones
The window was added by Honeybee_Glazing based on ratio and the Honeybee_Glazing Parameter List as
illustrated in Figure 4.17. In particular, since the Honeybee_Glazing based on ratio component requires
the glazing ratio to the wall surface, it was important to determine the glazing area in advance.
Figure 4.16 portrays the process followed to deﬁne the glazing area in zone II. In order to implement
the correction as regards the visible transmittance, the list of all the visible transmittance values was
necessary with the index to retrieve the corresponding value. A mathematical division of 10% multiplied
with the reference visible transmittance of 0.75 with the investigated visible transmittance was added.
This ratio result is ﬂoated in two decimals through Number, which is entered as a ﬁrst list item, while the
rest ratios of 0.25, 0.35 and 0.45 follow. With a Number Slider, the parameter of glazing to ﬂoor ratio is
applied. Therefore, the glazing to ﬂoor ratio is multiplied with the total zone area resulting in the glazing
area. The area of the zone is calculated through Area component, as shown in Figure 4.5. Additionally,
Figure 4.16 shows the condition that was added to the script stating that a glazed area should be less than
the wall area, otherwise the simulation can not run and the user must change the parameter of the glazing
or the wall size. In this way, possible errors are identiﬁed when applying the glazing area based on the
ﬂoor area on the wall surface.
Figure 4.16: Generation of glazing area
==PAGE:90==
68
Parametric design tool
Dividing the glazing area with the external wall area, which was found by the area output of the Area
component, which takes as input the output of Honeybee_createHBSrfs component. The result was the
fraction of the glazing to the external wall surface and is applied to the south orientation through Glazing
Parameter List component. Its output entered as an input to the Honeybee_Glazing based on ratio compo-
nent, accepting values of window to wall ratio of 0 to 0.95. This was another reason why the maximum
glazing to ﬂoor ratio was chosen to be 0.45 and not 0.55, resulting in a window to wall ratio of 0.98. The
Honeybee zone was connected to the Glazing based on ratio component from Honeybee_createHBZones.
A simple Boolean Toogle was used to send a True argument to the component in breakUpWindow_ as list
in order to enable the breakUpDist_ as list option underneath. This way the distance that the window
would be applied could be set manually and therefore, equal to the length of the facade. The height of
the window was based on the zone height, the sill height and the head height. In particular, two List
Items were created by subtracting the sill height, which consisted of two values, from the zone height
and two additional List Items were created by subtracting 0.8 m from the previous subtraction. Thus, the
parameter of window sill and head height was added to the List Item with an index varying from 0 to 3
as the variation levels of this parameter are four. Besides, the sill height was also connected to the Hon-
eybee_Glazing based on ratio component. Finally, the EnergyPlus constructions and Radiance materials
of the glass were applied to this component as well. The glazing surface was generated by using Boolean
Toogle with a True argument.
Figure 4.17: Generation of window in Honeybee Zone
As aforementioned, the thickness of the wall where the window is placed matters from a daylight view-
point. Figure 4.18 depicts the way to extrude the window. As input in Honeybee_Decompose Based On
Type, the output from Honeybee_Glazing based on ratio component entered, which was the zone and the
glazing surface. This component was used to break down the geometry of the zone depending on the
surface type. The edge curves of the window brep were extracted through Brep Edges and they were
joined by using Curves as list. The Extrude component was used to extrude these joined curves by the
wall thickness. To deﬁne the direction of this extrusion, theEvaluate Surface and Amplitude components
were used. The former was necessary to evaluate the local surface properties randomly at [0.5, 0.5] co-
ordinates, whereas the latter was used to set the amplitude of the vector. This amplitude was connected
with the List Item of the thermal mass parameter, which means that the thermal mass aﬀects the daylight
conditions indirectly as this extrusion casts some shadowing eﬀect. So another Honeybee surface was
created, importing the extrusion output, to the geometry of the Honeybee_createHBSrfs component with
==PAGE:91==
4.3 Inputs of script
69
its name. Only the Radiance material was added to this component for this window extrusion as it does
not matter for the energy simulation.
Figure 4.18: Implementation of wall thickness for daylight analysis
4.3.4
Lighting groups, test points and control sensors
Generation of test points
Since the user of the tool can change many inputs according to his/her desired design outcome, it was
considered important for the validation of the script, to determine the maximum grid size according to
Danish standard DS 17037 (DS/EN 17037, 2018). This was calculated by a script that was implemented,
through python as Figure 4.19 illustrates. This addition was helpful for the designer at the same time, as
the designer usually changes the grid size randomly without knowing the existence of a requirement in
the grid spacing.
Figure 4.19: Calculation of maximum grid size based on DS 17037
In particular, the calculation and veriﬁcation of the illuminance values over the entire work plane, located
0.85 m above the ﬂoor area are indicated by a grid system of points, that their grid cells shall have a
maximum grid size as the Equation 4.1 depicts (DS/EN 17037, 2018).
==PAGE:92==
70
Parametric design tool
푝= 0.2 × 5푙표푔10(푑)
(4.1)
where,
p is the maximum grid cell size [m] and should be equal or lower than 10 m
d is the longer dimension of the calculation area [m], but if the ratio of the longer to the shorter side is
equal or higher than 2, then d becomes the shorter dimension of the area.
More speciﬁcally, since the geometry of the zone can vary, the width and the depth of the zone were
connected with a condition that if the ratio of the depth to width is lower than 2, then d is equal to the
depth, otherwise it is equal to the width. In this way, the maximum grid size is calculated in the script
that is considered as a meaningful addition for the tool.
Through the component Honeybee_Generate Test Points, the ﬂoor surface where the test points would
be applied, from the component Honeybee_createHBSrfs, was set together with the size of the test grid,
which was 0.5 m and the distance from the ﬂoor surface was deﬁned at 0.85 m. For zone I, two of these
components were used as the test points needed to be applied in the each ﬂoor surface of the thermal
subzone.
Figure 4.20: Generation of test points for daylight analysis
The component Honeybee_Lighting control Recipe was used to deﬁne the electrical lighting control for
each lighting control group, as illustrated in Figure 4.21. For zone I there are two lighting control groups,
as already mentioned, while zone II has only one. The lighting control type was manual on/oﬀswitch.
The lighting power was set in each lighting group was based on Figure 4.22 and it was set in Watts.
Whereas the output of test points from the component Honeybee_Generate Test Points, were linked in List
Item. The sensor point in each lighting zone was diﬀerent and the process of their deﬁnition is described
further below.
==PAGE:93==
4.3 Inputs of script
71
Figure 4.21: Lighting control recipe
Figure 4.22 presents the lighting power in each lighting zone of zone I. It is remarkable to mention that
the lighting power in zone II was the same with the one in the lighting zone of the front subzone of zone
I, where two thirds of the total lighting power were applied.
Figure 4.22: Lighting power in each subzone of zone I
In order to locate the sensor point on the grid surface, it was necessary to ﬁnd which one of the created
test points coincided with the place that the sensor point is desired to be put. The parameter that aﬀected
the sensor placement in x direction is the width, whereas the depth and the window height changed
the sensor position in y direction. However, the width was standard in the present study as it did not
constitute a variable, but it can change according to a future user’s needs. The generation of the test
points was based on the applied width and depth in the tool. This means that setting diﬀerent width and
depth values changes the number and layout of the test points. So the sensor point in lighting zone of the
front subzone and back subzone respectively was determined automatically depending on all the above
variants as Figure 4.23 and Figure 4.24 present.
For zone II and font lighting zone of zone I the following process was followed to deﬁne the sensor point
which had to be part of the test point, approximately in a distance from the window, 1.5 times the eﬀective
window height. The number of sensor points in x direction were calculated by dividing the width of zone
with the grid size, as Figure 4.23 shows. The arising number of sensor points, which was named Sx
in Panel, should be integer. The sensor position in y direction was deﬁned by multiplying 1.5 with the
eﬀective window height, and then dividing it with the grid size. The arising number of sensor points,
which was named Sy in Panel, was rounded down using the ﬂoor output from the Round component.
Then, the number Sx was multiplied with Sy and then the result was added to the half of Sx to deﬁne the
point of sensor in the lighting group of 3.5 x 5.0 m.
==PAGE:94==
72
Parametric design tool
Figure 4.23: Sensor position in zone II and front lighting zone of zone I
For the back lighting zone of zone I, the same process was followed. The parametric relationships in
x direction were the same as in the other lighting zone. However, in y direction the number of sensor
points (Sy) were divided by 2, since the sensor point was intended to be placed in the center of the back
zone. Once the arising number was rounded down, it was multiplied by the number of sensor points in x
direction and then the half of them was added to determine the sensor point in the back lighting zone.
Figure 4.24: Sensor position in back lighting zone of zone I
Eventually, the panels of each sensor point were plugged into the index input of List Item as Figure 4.21
showed. Regarding zone II and the front lighting zone of zone I, the sensor point in y direction was
allowed to be placed on the grid line in y direction which is close to 1.5 times the eﬀective window
height. However, concerning the back lighting zone of zone I, the sensor point was placed in the central
grid line in y direction or in the next grid line. The reason for this sensor setting in the lighting zone
II, was that the same logic was followed to deﬁne the position of the sensor both in case of one single
lighting zone and in case of two lighting zones. It is important though to mention, that the sensor point
in the back zone can be distant from the central grid line 0.5 m. However, daylight can not penetrate that
far of the zone and subsequently, this 0.5 m is not that signiﬁcant for the back lighting to take advantage
of daylight. So, as the sunlight does not reach the back lighting zone of zone I that much, the electric
lights are activated more frequently, compared to the front lighting zone, where the daylight provides a
considerable energy beneﬁt in terms of the electric lighting energy. Moreover, the automation in sensor
position according to the generated geometry of the zone reminds the future user to place a sensor and
also, helps him/her to think of where the daylight sensor is better to be positioned before the performance
of a lighting simulation.
==PAGE:95==
4.3 Inputs of script
73
Figure 4.25: Sensor placement in zone I
Figure 4.26 presents some necessary components for the simulation analysis. Flatten Tree was used to
combine the lighting zones into one component. In this way, the results of zone I were listed in one list.
Through Mesh Join each analysis mesh from Generate Test Points were joined to one single mesh. This was
done for generating coloured meshes with numerical data. Through Merge the test points were merged
into one grid surface. The vectors of the test points were combined through Flatten Tree.
Figure 4.26: Merge of test points, vectors, analysis mesh and lighting zones of zone I
4.3.5
Zone loads and schedules
The occupancy schedule was set from 8:00 to 18:00 only for weekdays, as already stated in section 3.1.5
using Honeybee_Annual Schedule component. No occupancy was set in the weekends and holidays. As
mentioned in section 3.1.5, the occupancy was reduced to two thirds of seating capacity, and this was
applied in Honeybee_Annual Schedule component, as Figure 4.27 shows. The annual hourly values of
occupancy schedule were extracted using Honeybee_Convert EnergyPlus Schedule to Values component.

4.3 Inputs of script

Figure 4.33: Inﬁltration schedule
Figure 4.34 indicates how the ventilation schedule was generated. Through Ladybug_Separate data, the
hourly values of the occupancy schedule were retrieved from Honeybee_Convert EnergyPlus Schedule to
Values. The hourly values of the fractional occupancy schedule were multiplied with the number of occu-
pants per ﬂoor area. This reduced occupancy density was multiplied by the ﬂoor area of each subzone, in
case of zone I, to deﬁne the reduced number of occupants. The formula that calculates the total ventila-
tion rate in the zone according to Danish standard DS 15251, was set through Expression component. The
reduced number of occupants was multiplied by the ventilation rate of 0.01 m3/s per person, which is the
required one for category I. Then the corresponding ﬂoor area was multiplied with the ventilation rate
required for the building emissions, which is 0.001 m3/s per ﬂoor area for category I and low polluting
buildings. These two ventilation rates were added to give the reduced hourly ventilation rates during all
year. Therafter, the ventilation rates which took into account the two thirds of occupancy were divided
with the ventilation rate that considered 100% occupancy. These were the fractional hourly values of
ventilation schedule that were plugged into Honeybee_Create CSV Schedule.
Figure 4.34: Ventilation schedule
==PAGE:100==
78
Parametric design tool
As regards the lighting schedule, it was generated through Honeybee_Read Annual Result I which was
used to extract the climate based daylight metrics as presented in subsection 4.4.2. This component gen-
erated the lighting schedule for the annual energy simulation by linking the lighting control groups, the
illuminance ﬁles from the daylight simulation, the test points with their vectors and the occupancy ﬁle.
The generated lighting schedule was connected into the Honeybee_Daysim Annual Proﬁles component.
The output was the annual lighting switch proﬁle which was summed and through Explode Tree compo-
nent the sum of the annual lighting switch proﬁle was retrieved for each subzone in case of zone I. This
sum was multiplied with the corresponding lighting power in Watts of the zone and then, was divided by
1000 to calculate the kWh. This process was done to verify that the lighting energy through EnergyPlus
simulation was found the same. The lighting schedule was extracted through Explode Tree component
for each subzone in zone I and it was linked to Honeybee_Create CSV Schedule.
Figure 4.35: Lighting schedule
Afterward, all the aforementioned schedules were applied in the zone through Honeybee_Set EnergyPlus
Zone Schedules as shown in Figure 4.36. In case of zone I, two components were used for each subzone,
while in zone II only one. The same occupancy schedule was applied in each subzone of zone I taking its
schedule output from Honeybee_Annual Schedule. Moreover, the occupancy activity schedule was linked
from the same component to Honeybee_Set EnergyPlus Zone Schedules for each subzone of zone I. The
rest schedules were linked with the same process, while in case of zone I the two ventilation and lighting
schedules were linked to the corresponding Honeybee_Set EnergyPlus Zone Schedules components for each
subzone.

4.3.6
HVAC System
The Honeybee_Assign HVAC System component was used to assign the HVAC system to HBZone as Fig-
ure 4.40 shows. An Ideal Air Loads system was selected from Honeybee_HVACSystemList. This system
works in one of the following two ways. One way is based on a speciﬁc air ﬂow rate and the supply air
temperature is moderated to meet heating and cooling loads. The other way is that a speciﬁed supply
air temperature is set and the volume of air is moderated to meet the cooling load (Davidson, 2015). The
Ideal Air Loads system in the project took into account the ﬁrst way, which is more energy-consuming.
Basically, the Ideal Air Loads system moderates the supply air temperature in such a way keeping the
speciﬁed air ﬂow rates constant as they were set by the user based on the ventilation schedule. Therefore,
no economizer was set in Honeybee_HVAC Air Details component in order to provide constantly the same
amount of the volume ﬂow rates that were set, without increasing them. This prevented the removal of
the excessive heat during cooling season, which would mitigate the overheating problem.
==PAGE:103==
4.3 Inputs of script
81
Figure 4.40: Assignment of HVAC system
To be more speciﬁc, during heating season, the supply air temperature increased in order to meet the
thermostat setpoints during occupancy schedule. The Ideal Air Loads system uses the outdoor air and
adds heat in order to produce a supply air stream at the speciﬁed conditions. The maximum supply air
temperature is 40°C when the system is in heating mode. Such a high temperature was usually supplied
at 8:00, which is the ﬁrst oﬃce hour, in order to heat up the zone quickly. However, during the rest oﬃce
hours the supply air temperature varies around the heating setpoint. During the unoccupied hours, the
heating system is working only when the operative temperature drops below 15°C, which is the heating
setback. In case that the zone does not need to be heated up, the supply air temperature is the outdoor
air temperature. This occurs in the cooling season as well, when the heating setback is 15°C. Since the
operative temperature did not drop below 15°C during occupied hours, the heating air system did not
operate and fresh air was supplied at the outdoor air temperature. The volume ﬂow rates were also kept
according to the required ventilation rates.
As regards the Ideal Air Loads system which was used to ventilate the zone, the following strategy was
initially meant to be followed. The supply air temperature should be in the range of 18°C to 25°C. When
the outdoor air temperature was lower that 18°C, the supply air temperature could be at 18°C. However,
when the outdoor air temperature was higher than 18°C, the supply air temperature could be the outdoor
air temperature, but less than 25°C. This means that when the outdoor air temperature is higher than 25°C
the supply air temperature would be at 25°C, requiring some energy to cool down the air. In that way,
the system would reduce the accumulated heat in the zone. However, this was not suited in this study
since the objective was to assess the eﬀect of passive solutions in overheating. Additionally, the software
==PAGE:104==
82
Parametric design tool
did not allow the setting of the supply air temperature to speciﬁc temperatures. Therefore, the supply
air temperature during cooling season was the one of the outdoor air temperature, which exceeded 25°C
only for 11 to 12 hours and peaked at 26.5 °C. Figure 4.41 presents the way these values were retrieved.
Through Ladybug_Import epw the dry bulb temperature was linked to Sub List to extract only the values
during summer period using Domain. Using Dispatch the values during the occupied hours were retrieved.
Through List Length the number of the hours where the dry bulb temperature is higher than 25°C was
deﬁned. The same workﬂows were set to deﬁne how many hours the supply air temperature was more
than 25°C, using Honeybee_Read EP HVAC Result in order to read the supply air temperature, as shown in
Figure 4.42 and Figure 4.42. The maximum supply air temperature was determined using Sort List which
sorted the values from the minimum to maximum and then the values were reversed through Reverse
List. Through List Item the ﬁrst item was retrieved which indicated the maximum value of supply air
temperature.
Figure 4.41: Outdoor dry bulb temperature during occupied time in summer season
Figure 4.42: Maximum supply air temperature
==PAGE:105==
4.4 Outputs of script
83
Figure 4.43: Supply air temperature during occupied time in summer season
This overheating phenomenon occurs for a short time of the annual occupied hours in Denmark, which
can be "acceptable". In case that the tool would be modiﬁed for the requirements of another climate, these
hours should be checked so that the designer knows the eﬀect of the climate in the ventilation strategy.
4.4
Outputs of script
In this section, the outputs of the simulation workﬂows are presented in three diﬀerent parts, which are
the outputs of the energy simulation, daylighting simulation and lark simulation.
4.4.1
EnergyPlus simulation parameters and outputs
In order to run the energy simulation through EnergyPlus, the simulation parameters were important to
be set up. Figure 4.44 shows the components that were used for setting the parameters of the Energy-
Plus simulation with their inputs. The analysis period was set to be the entire year from January 1st to
December 31st. The starting hour was set at 1 which represents the ﬁrst hour of the day after midnight,
and the end hour was set to 24 which is the last hour of the day before midnight. These information of
the analysis period was set through Ladybug_Analysis Period.
Honeybee_Energy Simulation Par was used to set the EnergyPlus shadow parameters. The number "1"
was set to calculate the solar distribution, indicating that only direct sun accounts on the solar calcula-
tion. With this assumption, only solar beam solar radiation entering the zone falls on the ﬂoor indoors.
Moreover, for the distribution of incoming diﬀuse solar energy between interior surfaces, a simple win-
dow view factor calculation was used. A set of simulation controls were set from Honeybee_Simulation
Control component. Basically, only the maximum number of warmup days were set in this component.
The number of 30 warm up days was enough to be set for a correct initial set up for the simulation to
run before starting to record the result from January 1st. The surrounding terrain of the building was set
to "0" representing a large city center, since the simulation accounts for an oﬃce zone in Copenhagen.
The component of Honeybee_Generate EP Output was essential in order to set all the outputs need for the
EnergyPlus to run.
==PAGE:106==
84
Parametric design tool
Figure 4.44: Simulation parameters
All the above components were necessary to be set in order for their outputs to be inputs of the Honey-
bee_Run Energy Simulation component, which is depicted in Figure 4.45. The parameter of orientation
was set in north input of this component, using List Item component to set all the orientations that were
intended to be simulated. The weather ﬁle was linked also with the HBZone output of the last component
before the energy simulation. A Boolean Toggle was set to True for writeIdf and runEnegyPlus to run the
simulation. An IDF ﬁle is written including all the inputs and once EnergyPlus has ﬁnished witting it, the
component runs IDF and a CSV result ﬁle is generated.

Figure 4.47: Generation of the bar chart for energy balance
Figure 4.48 presents the way the overheating hours were calculated for the entire year. The speciﬁc
ﬂow was taken from a case in zone I, where two subzones were created. Through Honeybee_Read EP
Custom Result, the resultFileAdress from EnergyPlus simulation was linked. As the metric for overheating
condition assessment is the operative temperature, the zone operative temperature was set as a keyword.
According to Danish standard DS 474, the indoor temperature should not exceed 26°C for more than 100
hours per year and 27°C for more than 25 hours per year, in order to avoid overheating and maintain a
pleasant indoor climate. Using Separate data the text strings were separated from the numbers. As it is
depicted in Figure 4.48, for zone I a Explode Tree component was used to separate the values for each
subzone, that was not needed in case of zone II. The component Dispatch was used to separate all the
values of the operative temperature from the list that were higher than 26°C through Larger Than. The
List Length was used to measure the number of the values which were found above this temperature,
which basically indicates the annual overheating hours above 26°C. The similar process was followed
for calculating the annual overheating hours above 27°C. Additionally, the annual hours the operative
temperature was below 20°C were found just to see if the ideal heating system maintains the indoor
temperature above 20°C most of the year.

Separate data. The component Dispatch was used to link this list of the numerical values of the operative
temperature and extract only the operative temperature during occupied time. This was accomplished
using Separate data that was connected with Honeybee_Convert EnergyPlus Schedule to Values so that
only the hourly operative temperature during the occupied time could be taken. Through Explode Tree
the values of each subzone of zone I were extracted. The overheating hours of zone II was determined
similarly with the front subzone of zone I, without the use of Explode Tree component. The same process
as explained before, was followed to calculate the overheating hours above 27°C.
Figure 4.49: Calculation of overheating hours during annual occupied hours
The point where the operative temperature was measured for each zone is meaningful for the overheating
evaluation, as it aﬀects the results. In chapter 4 a detailed explanation of the choice to deﬁne the operative
temperature in the middle of the front zone is given. Due to the way that zone I was simulated, the
operative temperature was assumed critical to be measured in the front thermal subzone. Moreover,
EnergyPlus measures operative temperature in the middle of the zone. This point is the same point for
measuring the operative temperature in zone II. This means that the distance from the window was kept
constant. However, the radiant temperature of the surfaces of the internal walls were diﬀerent in these
==PAGE:110==

two zones. In this way, the overheating problem was perceived by the occupant from the same point so
that the results could be more comparable.

Daylight simulation parameters and outputs
Radiance simulation parameters which were set in Honeybee Honeybee_RADParameters are listed in Ta-
ble 4.2 based on reference oﬃce’s suggested radiance parameters and also on the required simulation
time. The quality of the simulation results, as well as the duration of the simulation is aﬀected by these

Table 4.2: Radiance simulation parameters for daylighting workﬂow
ambient bounce
3
ambient division
500

ambient accuracy
0.05
Run Daylight Analysis is the component to run the daylighting simulation. Since interior air walls were
just used for the partitioning of thermal zones, the function exportAirWalls on the daylighting simulation
component helps to remove air wall surfaces by setting the default by False. Depending on what metric
is intended to be calculated, the relevant recipe is chosen. It was intended to report Daylight Factor (DF)
values for comparison against traditional guidelines. The component Honeybee_Daylight Factor Simula-
tion is the recipe for Daylight Factor Analysis. The test points, the vectors of each point and the radiance
parameters were connected with this component. As HBObject, all the surfaces that matter in a day-
light analysis were applied, such as the zone with the window, window sill, external ground surface and
external facade surface. The results are DF values at each test point. The component Honeybee_Annual
Daylight Simulation is an analysis recipe for Annual Daylighting Simulation to calculate climate based
daylight metrics. The input north represents the degrees from y axis to make North, so the orientation
parameter was applied, as in the Honeybee_Run Energy Simulation component. The weather ﬁle was also
applied, as these metrics are dependent on the climatic conditions. Test points and their vectors together
with the radiance parameters were connected as inputs to this component as well. Through this an-
nual daylighting simulation, illuminance ﬁles of annual daylight illuminance values were used to derive
the following daylight metrics: daylight autonomy (DA), maximum daylight autonomy (DAmax), useful
daylight illuminance (UDI) and spatial Daylight Autonomy (sDA).
==PAGE:113==
4.4 Outputs of script
91
Figure 4.54: Honeybee_Run Daylight Simulation
As already mentioned, Honeybee_Read Annual Result I was used to calculate the dynamic performance
daylight metrics. As can be seen in Figure 4.55, the two Daysim lighting control groups were assigned for
zone I through Flatten Tree, while the other one for zone II was just assigned from the output of Lighting
control Recipe. This is the way that lighting control linked daylighting and energy simulation.
Honeybee_Read Annual Result I calculates UDI based on the previous upper limit of 2000 lux. Even though,
Honeybee_Read Annual Result II, can get as an input a threshold which is speciﬁed by the user, to calculate
the metrics of UDI, it was not chosen in the workﬂow. The reason is that Honeybee_Read Annual Result
II does not consider daylight saving through Daysim model.
So climate-based daylight metrics were calculated based on the daylight autonomy value for an illumi-
nance of 300 lux. This was decided in order to follow the requirements of the Danish Building Regulation
2018 (BR18) regarding the required 300 lux of internal illuminance in more than 50% of the space for more
than 50% of the daylight hours. Actually, it was regarded essential to run the daylighting simulation for
the spatial Autonomy (sDA) based on the daylight hours. However, the component Honeybee_Read An-
nual Result I required the occupancy ﬁle as an input for calculating the climate-based metrics. So the
schedule of sunlight hours could be possible to be applied in that component, in case that another day-
==PAGE:114==
92
Parametric design tool
lighting simulation was performed only for calculating this metric of spatial Daylight Autonomy. As this
issue was realized after the simulations, all the metrics were calculated based on the occupied hours,
stressing the necessity for a later correction of the spatial Daylight Autonomy for having a script totally
complied with the Danish regulation.
The calculation of the climate-based daylight metrics in each test point is shown in Figure 4.55.

Daylight Autonomy and spatial Daylight Autonomy was received as an output of Honeybee_Read Annual
Result I, setting illuminance threshold of 300 lux. UDI values were calculated based on the daylight auton-
omy which was calculated by setting an illuminance threshold (Mardaljevic et al., 2009). More speciﬁcally,
UDI100 was calculated setting a threshold of 100 lux in Honeybee_Read Annual Result I. UDI100-300 was cal-
culated based on the range of the daylight autonomy with illuminance values higher than 300 lux and
100 lux. The space-averaged value for the occurrence of illuminances in the range 100 to 3000 lux during
the occupied time is 80% and that is why 80% of UDI100-300 added to UDI300-3000, according to a study
(Mardaljevic, 2015). In this way, a percentage of the time below 300lux and above 100lux where artiﬁ-
cial lighting may not be necessary for the task activities is considered for the UDI-autonomous. UDI3000
was calculated, also, setting a threshold of 10 times of the minimum illuminance level. DAmax was also
included in the workﬂow for evaluating likely glare risks.
Using the components of TT toolbox plug-in, Write Options and Write to Excel, the metrics DA, UDI100,
UDI300, UDI100-300, UDI100-3000, UDI300-3000, UDI3000 for each test point were written in columns of excel
ﬁles for each case. Figure 4.56 shows a representative example of how the results of metrics were saved.
Besides this, the values of each test point were illustrated in the grid surface through Recolor Mesh of
Ladybug plug-in. The metric values were inserted as analysisResult and the output of mesh from Generate
Test Points was applied as an input of the aforementioned component. Through Legend Parameters legend
parameters, such as color or range, can change.
Figure 4.56: Writing data of daylight metrics for each test point in Excel
Due to the multiple plots that were created to show the metric value in each test point, it was decided to
calculate some more speciﬁc values of them in order to insert them in one Excel ﬁle for data analysis.
The sum of all the DF point values, calculated by Mass Addition were divided by the number of the test
points, using List Length, in order to calculate the DFavg, as Figure 4.57 depicts. In the same logic, all the
others metrics were calculated.

4.4.3.2
Spectral sky deﬁnition
In order to create the spectral sky for the 3-channel analyses, the spectral material of the sky had to be
prepared. The followed process is illustrated in Figure 4.66. Convert_SPD - Write Spectral Radiance Materi-
als converts spectral data that were fed into Lark, to radiance materials. A CIE standard indoor illuminant
ID65 was used. The ﬁlepath of ID65, including the reﬂectance of the material in each wavelength, was
fed into this component to spectral power distribution (spd_input) with a wavelength increment of 10
nm. Since this data of daylight series was derived from the Rochester Institute of Technology Excel Day-
light Series Calculator, the Boolean Toggle was set to true. The sky type was selected to material type
by a Panel with the value "2". The Convert_SPD - Write Spectral Radiance Materials gave ﬁle paths for 3
channel materials, while, its channel_output became an input for Build spectral radiance sky deﬁnitions
component. The values for latidude, longitude, and coordinated universal time (UTC) were taken from
the Ladybug_Decompose Location, as described above. Sky type, month, day and hour were connected to
Sliders that controlled these parameters in Honeybee_Generate Standard CIE Sky. This component gener-
ated a 3 channel spectral sunny and cloudy sky deﬁnitions for the location of Copenhagen for a speciﬁc
time by using speciﬁed global illuminance and gen_reindl command.
==PAGE:121==
4.4 Outputs of script
99
Figure 4.66: Build spectral radiance sky deﬁnitions
4.4.3.3
3-Channel radiance simulation
For the evaluation of the non-visual responses, two types of analysis were used. One image-based analysis
for the luminance levels that were perceived from the environment and another grid-based analysis for
the illuminance values that were recorded falling on the eye in the vertical axis. The recipes for these
simulations were prepared as is illustrated in Figure 4.67. For the image-based recipe, a camera in rhino
was placed at the exact position of the eye of the occupant and its name was assigned through a Panel
to the Honeybee_Image Based Simulation component. Fisheye was chosen as a camera type, with the
simulation type to be luminance, and an image of 600 pixels. The Honeybee_Grid Based Simulation used
as testPoints a point that was created by Construct Point in the exact position of the occupant eye and as
ptsVectors a Unit X connected ﬁrstly to the Negative component to point the view to the correct direction.
A Panel "0" was connected to the simulationType to inticate the illuminace as metric. In the skyﬁle input
of both recipes, the ﬁle path created by Build spectral radiance sky deﬁnitions was connected. The settings
in Honeybee_RADParameters component were similar to the setting used for the calculation of the global
horizontal illuminace from CIE sky.
==PAGE:122==
100
Parametric design tool
Figure 4.67: Image and grid based analysis recipes
Each one of these recipes was connected with a Honeybee_Run Daylight Simulation as can be seen in
Figure 4.68. It is important to clarify that the HBObjects that were connected in these components were
the same that were created for the other simulations.

The component Lark 3-channel circadian luminance was used to calculate circadian values with both Rea
et al. circadian spectral sensitivity curve and Lucas et al. melanopsin spectral sensitivity curve. The image
ﬁlepaths from luminance result ﬁles (.pic or .hdr) from Image Based Simulation and the irradiance ﬁlepaths
from illuminance result ﬁles from Grid Based Simulation were linked to the aforementioned component.
In this way, circadian luminance pictures and circadian illuminance values could be extracted for the
results.

High Dynamic Range (HDR) could be used to render the health-based performance model with a wide
ﬁsheye speciﬁcation. The generation of hemispherical ﬁsheye images was done to simulate the viewpoint
of a person sitting at the middle desk of zone I at the height of his eye or the camera, which was at 1.2 m.
Luminance maps in hemispherical ﬁsheye projections were not shown in the project since the eﬀect of
the color can not be seen due to the spectral neutrality of the space. Thus, section 5.4 incorporates only
circadian-illuminance results.
4.5
Automation of simulation workﬂow
In order to run all the simulations for each combination of parameters, two scripts in python of rhino were
created. The ﬁrst one automated the Sliders of each parameter for the energy and daylight simulations,
while the second one automated the lark simulation. The scripts that were composed are illustrated in
Figure 4.70 and Figure 4.71, respectively. The ﬁrst step to create the python scripts was to assign to each
Slider value of the parameters a variable for the loop. The range of these variables corresponded from 0
to the number of the parameter level, iterated by 1. Another variable named n representing the number
of the case, consequently, the number of row and column of the excel ﬁles for the results, was created
and increased by 1, every time that each case was simulated. In this way each case was written to the
next column or row in excel. In order to create all the combinations of the parameters and run the script
multiple times nested loops of type for were used. Each loop representing one parameter and its variation
levels was nested in the rest loops of the other parameters.

Chapter 5
Data analysis and results
This chapter provides the results of overheating, daylight, and health potential in terms of regulation
of circadian rhythms and is divided into four main sections. In the ﬁrst two sections, the inﬂuence of
each of the investigated parameters (zone depth, orientation, thermal mass, window type, glazing to
ﬂoor ratio, window placement) is analyzed under the scope of overheating and daylight respectively. At
the end of each of these sections, a synthesis of the results is presented. The third section includes the
results obtained from the lark simulation analysis. Finally, the chapter ends with a combination of those
conﬁgurations that were complied with both overheating and daylight criteria.
The purpose of this thesis was to attempt to generate rules of thumb between diﬀerent design parameters
in oﬃce buildings and agreement with Danish standards and regulations for assessing the risk of over-
heating and daylight insuﬃciency in oﬃces. Agreement with criteria concerning overheating tolerance
and suﬃcient daylight admission was based on passing both the following two criteria:
1. According to Danish standard DS 474 (DS 474, 1995), on warm days dressed in light clothing and
with sedentary activity, the requirement may be that the operative temperature during the time of
occupancy shall not exceed
(a) 26°C for more than 100 hours (Criterion 1a: Hours of exceedance < 100) and
(b) 27°C for more than 25 hours (Criterion 1b: Hours of exceedance < 25),
during a typical year.
2. According to Danish Building Regulation 2018 (BR18) (BR18, 2018), suﬃcient daylight can be doc-
umented by demonstrating that the internal light intensity from daylight is 300 lux or more in at
least half of the relevant ﬂoor area for at least half of the daylight hours. However, as mentioned
in subsection 4.4.2, due to limitations of the script, this criterion was based on the occupied hours
(Criterion 2): sDA300,50% > 50%).
In order to interpret the results according to the aforementioned criteria, an explorative analysis of the
data obtained from Grasshopper 3D was carried out. Based on the above mentioned criteria, the ﬁrst
approach was to identify the failure percentage of each variation level of each investigated parameter
for all the cases of zone I and zone II, respectively. The results are based on a bin analysis method
where all the cases that pass the mentioned criterion are identiﬁed (Marples, 2017). Using the same
==PAGE:128==
106
Data analysis and results
principle, the simulation cases are evaluated based on the output values where the median output values,
as well as lower and upper quartile are shown for better understanding of the obtained results. Since the
parameter of the zone depth was quite critical for the overheating and daylight assessment, the results
of the impact of the parameters both on overheating and daylight levels are illustrated for each zone
allowing a more visible comparison. Additionally, in order to dip into the details explaining why some
phenomena occurred, other graphs such as line charts, scatter plots, box plots and pie charts are used to
display patterns, relations among metrics and distributional characteristics. The ﬁnal analysis is done in
three steps and presented in three sections. As the ﬁrst step, the overheating results are analyzed and
the eﬀect of each parameter on this phenomenon is shown. Secondly, the daylighting analysis based on
dynamic daylight performance metrics is carried out. Finally daylight health potentials are shown. The
data analysis was conducted using Excel, Tableau 2019.2 and Matlab to represent and synthesize the tables
and graphs which are presented below.
5.1
Inﬂuence on overheating hours
The assessment of overheating risks is based on the agreement with both criteria 1a and 1b simultane-
ously, since there may be some cases that fail criterion 1a but pass criterion 1b or the opposite. Although,
according to the ﬁndings of this thesis, criterion 1b is the determinant factor in the evaluation of the
overheating risks in both zones since in all cases that were simulated and failed criterion 1a, also failed
criterion 1b.
The main results are presented in tables of bin analysis. These tables give the failure percentage of each
variation level of a parameter in criterion 1a and 1b, showing the cases with an overheating problem dur-
ing a year for the two diﬀerent zone depths. Additionally, the impact of the parameters on the reduction
of overheating is presented in each orientation level.
Since overheating hours during the annual occupied time were already retrieved, it was considered es-
sential to evaluate the quantity of the overheating problem, apart from its duration. Therefore, temporal
maps which show the timing and the hourly excess of the operative temperature from the threshold
temperatures are illustrated.
5.1.1
Zone depth
Figure 5.1 documents the relation between the agreement with criterion 1 and the parameter of zone
depth. Zone I represents the zone with the higher depth, 8.2 m, while zone II is the zone with the depth
of 5 m. These kind of tables are constructed as follows. The bin percentage of the total number of
results refers to the number of cases of the corresponding parameter level to the total cases that were
simulated. Next to this, the number of cases failing or passing each criterion is reported. Additionally,
the percentage of cases failing of passing the criterion 1a and 1b is presented, based on the simulated cases
for each variation level of the parameter. The columns that portray the percentages are scale-colored for
the reader to understand faster the deviation among the results.
As can be seen in Figure 5.1, the cases that were simulated for zone I were much less than those ones
which were simulated for zone II. At this point, it is substantial to remind that the cases of zone I have
less variation levels of glazing to ﬂoor ratio parameter (Rmin, R25) compared to zone II. As explained in
section 3.2.5, a glazing to ﬂoor ratio more than 25% would lead to a larger glazing surface than the wall
==PAGE:129==
5.1 Influence on overheating hours
107
area. Moreover, the cases of the window type of 5025 were simulated only for the minimum glazing to
ﬂoor ratio. The reason was that the minimum glazing to ﬂoor ratio, which is 25% in zone I with a window
type of 5025 resulted in a similar glazing area with the glazing to ﬂoor ratio of 25% with the same window
type.
Figure 5.1: Criterion 1: Bin analysis for zone depth
According to Figure 5.1, the percentage of cases that exceeded 100 hours for an operative temperature
of more than 26°C was almost the double for zone II. On the other hand, it is distinct that approximately
the same percentage, which is over a half of the simulated cases for both zones, failed criterion 1b, which
is the determinant criterion for the simulated cases to state an overheating problem. Thus, it can not be
said that zone II has more risk to fail criterion 1.
The scatter plot in Figure 5.2 shows the relation between the peak solar gain per unit of ﬂoor area and
agreement with criterion 1, for each simulated case, colored by the parameter of zone depth. The dot
lines depict the limit of the peak solar gains per unit ﬂoor area that leads to fail the criterion.
Figure 5.2: Relation between peak solar gain per ﬂoor area and criterion 1 for zone depth
The overheating hours diﬀer in each zone, in terms of amount and value of temperature, as can be seen
in Figure 5.2. According to this ﬁgure, it can be concluded that spaces with a depth of 5 m subjected to 22
==PAGE:130==
108
Data analysis and results
W/m2 peak solar gains are at no risk of exceeding criterion 1. Similarly, spaces with a depth of 8.2 m are
at no risk of exceeding 27°C for more than 25 hours when they subject to an inconsiderably higher peak
solar gain of 24 W/m2. Moreover, from this graph, the number of aggregated overheating hours can be
viewed, which were increased by three times by the extreme cases of zone II compared to these ones of
zone I.
Boxplots presented in Figure 5.3, give a better view of the distributional characteristics of peak solar gain
per ﬂoor area and the exceeded overheating hours above 27 °C of each simulated case in each zone. The
cases are shorted by their values, and they are grouped to four equal groups, called as quartile groups. So,
each group is composed of 25% of the total cases of each group with the particular characteristic, which
is the zone depth in this graph. The colored boxes represent 50% of the cases that are distributed around
the median value. The light color is for the 25% above the median, while the dark for the 25% beneath it.
The cases under and above these colored boxes are the other 50% of the total (25% above and 25% under),
while the interquartile range is depicted by the whiskers, exposing the outliers.
Figure 5.3: Boxplots of peak solar gain per ﬂoor area (left) and aggregated hours above 27 °C (right) for
zones I and II
As Figure 5.3 depicts, even though over 50% of both cases in both zones exceeded the limit for overheating
as already discussed, more than 25% of the cases of zone II exceeded 27°C above 100 hours per year, which
is a considerable amount to reduce. Moreover, 75% of cases of both zones had more than 20 W/m2 peak
solar gain. However, in zone II, the values of peak solar gain that the cases reached over 120 W/m2, spread
out on a broader range. So, it is preferable for the solar radiation to be dissipated over a greater percentage
of space, resulting in better heat distribution and thus, in better thermal comfort.
The time series data presented in Figure 5.4 and Figure 5.5 show how the operative temperature and peak
solar gain per unit of ﬂoor area ﬂuctuated across time for one extreme case of zone I and zone II. The cases
that were chosen as extreme ones are I_TM1_SE_lowe_S0_Hmax_R25 and II_TM1_SE_lowe_S0_Hmax_R45,
having the same other parameters, except for the zone depth and the glazing to ﬂoor ratio. In these
diagrams, the outdoor air temperature shielded from radiation and moisture is also depicted, marked as
outdoor dry-bulb temperature. Even though the glazing to ﬂoor ratio was diﬀerent, the glazing areas are
similar (7.38 m2 for zone I and 8.1 m2 for zone II). Thus, the transmitted solar gains are expected to be
==PAGE:131==
5.1 Influence on overheating hours
109
similar. However, the peak solar gain per ﬂoor area was higher in zone II, because of its lower ﬂoor area.
Figure 5.4: Operative temperatures (left) and peak solar gain (right) for zones I and II during a day with
extreme peak solar heat gain
Figure 5.5: Operative temperatures (left) and peak solar gain (right) for zones I and II during a typical
week of the summer
More speciﬁcally, Figure 5.4 depicts the results of August 6th, which is the date when the highest solar
peak gain was reported. The higher peak solar gain in zone II leaded to an increase in operative tem-
perature and also, a longer duration of the overheating problem. Additionally, a typical working week in
summer from July 19th to 24th is shown in Figure 5.5. More profound diﬀerences between the zones can
be observed, as far as the overheating phenomena are concerned. According to this graph, the peak solar
gain of zone II was double of the corresponding case of zone I, resulting in the expected rise in operative
temperature. What is noticeable, though, is that the operative temperature of zone I exceeded the limit
of criterion 1b only for limited hours during this week. On the contrary, the operative temperature of
zone II ﬂuctuated above this limit for a longer period as the graph shows, revealing a lasting overheating
problem. Additionally, the fact that the operative temperature climbed up above 30°C indicates the eﬀort
that must be put to control these extreme temperatures inside zone II, in terms of cooling energy.
Despite the aforementioned diﬀerences, the agreement with the criteria was not aﬀected to a great extent,
since similar percentages of simulated cases in both zones exceeded 27°C for more than 25 hours. The
main diﬀerences that should be emphasized were that the amount of hours above the limit of 25 as well
as the degrees above the 27°C in zone II are comparatively more. Thus, it is considered more diﬃcult to
achieve the agreement of zone II with the criteria of Danish standards compared to zone I, where fewer
alterations can be applied.

110
Data analysis and results
5.1.2
Orientation
Figure 5.6 displays the bin analysis of the orientation levels for each zone. Except for the four main
orientations of South, West, North, and East, the intermediate ones, meaning South-West, North-West,
South-East, and North-East, were chosen to be evaluated as well. However, these orientations are not
presented in all graphs to make them more readable.
Figure 5.6: Criterion 1: Bin analysis for orientation
As it was expected, the most critical orientations were proved to be South-West, South-East and South
with East and West orientations to follow closely, in both zones revealing the highest percentages of
failure in overheating criteria. It is noticeable that all cases of zone I, simulated in South, South-West and
South-East failed criterion 1b, with no exception, while the failure percentages were too high in West and
East. Similar outcomes are noticed in zone II, where high percentages are observed in all orientations,
but no orientation showed 100% failure. The only obvious and 100% safe choice seems to be the North
orientation in deep spaces, while this orientation in less deep spaces can be considered a low risk option.
This fact reveals the critical matter of solar control in most orientations.
Taking into account that peak solar heat gain can vary within a range of 0 to 170 W/m2 depending on
the orientation, bin analyses are presented for each zone and orientation for shorter ranges in Figure 5.7,
Figure 5.8, Figure C.7, Figure C.8 and Figure C.9. An insight into this parameter’s levels and their relation
to peak solar heat gains can be given through those ﬁgures.
==PAGE:133==
5.1 Influence on overheating hours
111
Figure 5.7: Bin analysis for orientation in relation to peak solar heat gains (0-30 W/m2)
In the range between 0 and 30 W/m2 peak solar heat gain, as presented in Figure 5.7, 86% and 93% of cases
for zone I and zone II respectively, complied with the criterion 1. This denotes that if these zones are
subject to no more than 30 W/m2, they have great probabilities to comply with the overheating require-
ments. Attention should be given for zones in South, South-West and South-East, especially for deep
zones. As aforementioned, all cases of zone I in these orientations have failed criterion 1b, thus some
extra measures must be taken to avoid the operative temperature to exceed 27°C for more than 25 hours.
Consequently, a lower limit than 30 W/m2 of peak solar heat gain has to be deﬁned for these orientations.
According to Figure 5.9 and Figure 5.10, the orientation of South, South-East and South-West started to
fail the criterion of overheating from the range of 27-30 W/m2. Moreover, as regards the cases of zone II
that faced North, a signiﬁcant number of 120 out of 128 of them were subject up to 30 W/m2. However,
only 12 out of 120 of them failed criterion 1b in this range of 0-30 W/m2 of peak solar gain, while the
rest 8 simulated cases of this orientation failed in the range of 30-60 W/m2 as depicted in Figure 5.8. In
general, north-oriented facades in zone I are safe in terms of overheating.
Out of the total 448 cases simulated for zone I, 45% were subject to 30-60 W/m2 and 95% of them failed
criterion 1. Similarly, zone II presented high percentages of failure to almost 90% rendering this range
of high risk. Obviously, as the peak solar heat gains per area rose above this range, all cases failed the
criterion 1, as expected. The bin analyses of Figure C.7, Figure C.8 and Figure C.9 in Appendix C illustrate
that all the rest cases totally failed in the rest ranges from 60 to 170 W/m2.
==PAGE:134==
112
Data analysis and results
Figure 5.8: Bin analysis for orientation in relation to peak solar heat gains (30-60 W/m2)
Additionally, scatter plots which illustrate the relation between peak solar heat gain per ﬂoor area and
the hours of exceedance above criterion 1 for each zone are presented in Figure 5.9 and Figure 5.10 re-
spectively. In these graphs, the variation levels of the orientation parameter are colored as well to be
clearly distinguished and show the risk of their failure to the criterion 1b. The distribution of the cases
in the scatter plots of both Figure 5.9 and Figure 5.10 displays the North, North-East and North-West
orientations as the least favourable to be subject to high solar heat gains, rendering them as the most
probable orientations within which a zone is anticipated to comply with the criterion 1. Moreover, an
approximate value of 30 W/m2 maximum peak solar gain is drawn as the highest possible limit to comply
with overheating criteria. Deﬁnitely, there were cases that even within this range failed the criterion, or
were subject to higher values and complied with it, but they were limited, and all of them failed for more
than 45 W/m2 peak solar gains.
In particular, limits of peak solar heat gain for every orientation, within which agreement with the stan-
dards is predicted, can be drawn for the diﬀerent zones. Zones similar to zone I with relatively a long
distance to the window, when facing North are of no risk to fail the standards according to the ﬁndings.
East in zone I is considered an unfavorable orientation for higher than 24 W/m2 of peak solar gain. Deep
zones, such as zone I, facing South, South-East and South-West totally failed the criteria when exposed
to more than 27 W/m2 of peak solar gain, while zones facing West risk the agreement over 37 W/m2.
North-East and North-West orientations in zone I which are exposed to more than 45 W/m2 peak solar
gain failed the agreement with criterion 1. On the other hand, shallow zones facing North are possible to
fail the criteria subjected to more than 22 W/m2, while South, South-East, South-West or North-East risk
the agreement over a peak solar heat gain of 27 W/m2. When designing a zone, such as zone II, facing
East,as a limit could be considered the 33 W/m2. As far as the rest orientations are concerned, exposure
to more than 37 W/m2 is possible to lead in failure. It is also plainly viewed that cases in zone I reach a
peak solar gain per ﬂoor area up to 90 W/m2, whereas cases in zone II reach a peak solar gain per ﬂoor
area up to 170 W/m2.

First of all, the ﬂuctuation of operative temperature and solar heat gains is quite distinct in North. So-

temperatures.
5.1.3
Thermal mass
In this section, the abbreviation "OH" (Overheating Hours) is referred to the hours that the indoor tem-
perature exceeds the operative temperatures of 27°C. In addition, the abbreviation "OT" refers to the
operative temperature.
Figure 5.13 indicates the bin analysis for the variation levels of thermal mass parameter for each zone,
which are the concrete (TM1) and steel (TM2) construction of the external wall. In general, a small rela-
tion can be drawn between thermal mass and overheating criteria, as can be seen from the percentages
between the levels of the thermal mass. However, a slightly higher failure percentage can be seen due to
the thermal mass of steel panel construction (TM1). The percentages of the cases that failed criterion 1
for both zones are quite similar.
==PAGE:137==
5.1 Influence on overheating hours
115
Figure 5.13: Criterion 1: Bin analysis for thermal mass
The pie charts in Figure 5.14 demonstrate the percentages of the cases with thermal mass TM1 that ex-
ceeded in aggregated hours above 27°C (OHTM1) more than the corresponding cases with thermal mass
TM2 (OHTM1), and the opposite. In this way, the performance of the two diﬀerent thermal masses were
compared under the same circumstances, revealing the likelihood of one thermal mass to have a greater
eﬀect on mitigation of overheating. The results are presented separately for the two zones.
Figure 5.14: Percentage of cases in zone I (left) and II (right) with an increase in overheating hours
depending on the thermal mass level
According to the pie charts, concerning zone I, a decrease of overheating hours occurred in 58% of the
cases with a concrete wall system (TM1), which is thermally heavier than the steel wall system (TM2).
However, the thermal mass did not inﬂuence the reduction of overheating hours in 22% of the cases due
to the equal number of overheating hours in those cases. The cases of zone II with the concrete sandwich
panel (TM1) in the facade, displayed less overheating hours than the cases of the steel wall system, in a
percentage of 68%. The corresponding percentage, where cases with unitized steel facade (TM2) showed
less overheating hours was 25%. The impact of thermal mass was none in 7% of cases in the less deep
zone.
For a thorough investigation of the eﬀect of thermal mass, two cases of each zone with diﬀerent thermal
mass are presented with all the rest parameters to be the same. The examined cases, which display the
==PAGE:138==

Data analysis and results
most considerable number of overheating hours arose due to the thermal mass of steel panels (TM2)
compared to TM1, are I_NE_lowe_S0_Hmax_Rmin and II_SE_lowe_S1_Hmin_R45. The time series data
in Figure C.1 and Figure C.2 in Appendix C show the outdoor dry-bulb temperature temperature and
operative temperature (OT) ﬂuctuation for the two thermal masses of the selected cases in zone I and
zone II respectively, during the overheating period from April to September. The ﬂuctuations of the
operative temperature of the cases with TM1 and TM2 in both zones followed the same trend and in most
of the simulated period, they even coincided. Their trend rose and fell according to the outdoor dry-
bulb temperature ﬂuctuations, denoting no particular eﬀect of the thermal mass in the reduction of the
overheating hours.
Moreover, the reported total hours of exceedance above limits of criteria 1a and 1b for these cases are
presented in Table 5.1 for each zone and thermal mass. However, the duration of the exposure to higher
temperatures than the reference ones is not the only indicator of thermal discomfort. The value of the
operative temperature has a signiﬁcant impact on the ﬁnal perception of an occupant for the indoor
thermal environment, as well. Thus, the annual exceedance of operative temperature of the limits for
each case in Kelvin hours per year is stated in Table 5.2. The corresponding temporal maps of each case,
which are shown in Figure C.3 Figure C.4, Figure C.5 and Figure C.6 in Appendix C, illustrate not only
the duration but also the quantity of the exceeded operative temperature of the limits of criteria 1a and

Figure 5.15: Operative temperature of zone I with two diﬀerent thermal masses on August 2nd
A representative example of the case of zone I is given for a hot summer day, on August 2nd. This date
was chosen because the behavior of the thermal mass can be clear providing there was a temperature
drop at night. More speciﬁcally, the outdoor dry-bulb temperature exceeded the operative temperature
inside the zone that day, as shown in Figure 5.15. Basically, the outdoor dry-bulb temperature reached a
maximum of almost 27°C at 12:00 at noon. However, the outdoor dry-bulb temperature at night was quite
lower, falling to 15°C.
Even though the outdoor dry-bulb temperature was sharply increasing, the operative temperature inside
the zone inclined with a lower tilt, with the operative temperature never reaching that high levels. This
is due to the heat that the thermal mass absorbed, which was released after the working schedule. This
delay of the heat ﬂow from the time the outdoor dry-bulb temperature reached the peak to the time that
the heat was re-emitted in the zone is called time lag. Whereas the quantiﬁed deviation in temperature
degrees is called damping (Hodkinson, 2019) as mentioned in section 2.5. These two eﬀects are attributed
to the thermal capacity of the building elements of the zone. As thermal lag is dependent on the ther-
mal conductivity, thickness, insulation levels and temperature diﬀerences either side of the external wall
(Chris Reardon, 2013), it would be expected that the operative temperature of the case with thermal mass
TM1 would have a longer delay, which is not visible in the graph above. The small diﬀerence in degrees
Celsius between the operative temperature of TM1 and TM2 that is observed in the time series data can be
explained by the diﬀerence in the thermal capacity of the two diﬀerent facade constructions. Moreover,
it is worth mentioning that the operative temperature of the case with TM2 exceeded the limit of 26°C of
criterion 1a for a short time that day, reporting overheating, whereas the case of TM1 was able to keep
the operative temperature below the critical level.
==PAGE:140==
118
Data analysis and results
Figure 5.16: Operative temperature of zone II with two diﬀerent thermal masses on August 6th
On the other hand, Figure 5.16 represents a day, which is August 6th, where the operative temperature in-
side zone II in the corresponding case was extremely high, reaching above 40°C. Moreover, the diﬀerence
between operative temperatures are negligible, which can be justiﬁed by the fact that a concrete material
was also used for the ﬂoor. This case is regarded as representative where thermal mass was unable to
overtake some of the heat loads in the zone and protect it from overheating. It must be clariﬁed that
in this case of zone II the glazing to ﬂoor ratio was the highest simulated, with a total glazing area of
8.1 m2 out of the 10.08 m2 of the facade. This means that the thermal mass that was parameterized was
implemented to rest 1.98 m2 of the facade, thus the thermal mass would not be eﬀective in these cases.
The thermal mass, as explained above, showed an insigniﬁcant impact on the overheating phenomena
for the extreme two cases that were studied. Additionally, the bin analysis showed insigniﬁcant relation
between the overheating criterion and thermal mass. Thus, there is no need to demonstrate a comparison
between each thermal mass and each orientation. Moreover, the possible relation of the parameter of the
thermal mass with the orientation was chosen not to be shown, for avoiding any misleading impressions.
The only visible eﬀect in overheating would be tied to the orientation.

Window type

around 40% of cases with 5025 in both zones failed criterion 1b, while three quarters of the simulated
cases failed this criterion with a window type of lowe. This equality was anticipated since the solar heat
gains are indissolubly linked to the window type and the cases that simulated for each zone and for each
window type correspond to similar portion of the total.

limited overheating risk, as the percentage of 3% depicts. However, a considerable increase to 61% of
cases arose for R25 rising to 86% of cases with R45. This rapid increase of failure percentage in cases
with R25, R35 and R45 occurred due to the greater glazing percentage applied to the facade, which is
around the double of those ones with Rmin arose for each window type. Basically, the minimum glazing
to ﬂoor ratio in zone II created a glazing surface that does not exceed 29% of the facade area, while the rest

of glazing to ﬂoor ratio increases the acceptable levels of peak solar gain should be reduced in order for

5.1.6
Window placement
According to the window sill and head height parameter, not all of the combinations were presented. This
decision is connected with a limitation of Grasshopper 3D and the simulation set up, which is important
to be explained further as follows. The glazing area was determined by the parameter of glazing to ﬂoor
ratio. However, the distribution of this area in the facade of the zone was dependent on the window sill
and head height. In some cases with large glazing to ﬂoor ratios, the glazing area that was assigned in
the zone was bigger than the area the window sill and head height allowed to create. In these cases,
the window was placed based on the assigned glazing area and not on the sill and head height that
were speciﬁed by the authors. Since the impact of these combinations would give wrong estimation of
the results, they were excluded. Therefore, Table 5.3 and Table 5.4 depict which glazing to ﬂoor ratio
combinations were simulated according to the speciﬁed window placement, and they were taken into
account for the evaluation.

Data analysis and results
Figure 5.33: Criterion 1: Bin analysis for window head height
As it was expected, no relation between sill height and head height and agreement with criterion 1 was
drawn. This is because the same glazing area was distributed to the facade regardless of the head or
sill height. These two parameters aﬀect only the adjustment of the glazing area to its facade’s width.
Increasing window sill height widens the window while increasing window head height narrows it. Thus,
since the glazing area is the same, the same solar gains were transmitted through the glass, without
showing any relation with the overheating risk.

Synthesis in overheating
In this section, a summary of the all the aforementioned results of the overheating assessment is pre-
sented together with the combination of the most inﬂuential parameters in overheating. The table below
indicates for each parameter the number and percentage of cases that passed criterion 1 for zone I and
zone II respectively. The range of the percentage of cases that complied with criterion 1 is visualized with
data bars, with the longer bar to represent the highest value.
==PAGE:153==
5.1 Influence on overheating hours
131
Figure 5.34: Criterion 1: Bin analysis for the synthesis of all parameters
Looking at the total numbers and percentages of simulated cases that complied with criterion 1, there were
no signiﬁcant diﬀerences between zone I and zone II. Based on the number of simulated cases for each
zone, minimal diﬀerences between the zones were indicated in terms of agreement with the overheating
criterion. However, a relation of the zone depth and the aggregated overheating hours above the limits of
the criteria as well as the operative temperature diﬀerences was found, with the zone II to mark higher
values.
As for the thermal mass, even though a clear relation with the criterion of overheating could not be drawn,
a slightly higher percentage of cases with thermal mass TM1 that complied in both zones can be seen.
As regards the orientation, no case with South, South-West and South-East complied with the criteria
of overheating in the deep zone. This was stemming from the quite higher glazing areas applied to the
facade based on the greater ﬂoor area of this zone I. On the other hand, all cases of the deep zone that
facing North were safe from overheating. In the less deep zone II, approximately a quarter of the simulated
cases with South, South-West, and South-East passed criterion 1, whereas there were some cases with a
North orientation that did not pass. Moreover, West and East orientations did not aﬀect the agreement
in overheating criteria in zone II. Conversely, east oriented facades in the deep zone I were riskier in
overheating than west oriented facades. This can be explained by the consideration of only the working
schedule in the simulation, which takes advantage of the morning solar radiation.
==PAGE:154==
132
Data analysis and results
Concerning window type, the percentages of cases that are safe in terms of overheating are similar for
both zones. It is also noticeable that over half of cases with 5025, whereas a quarter of cases with lowe
passed.
Concerning glazing to ﬂoor ratio, it is quite interesting to demonstrate that almost all cases in zone II
with the minimum glazing ratio were safe in terms of overheating, except for an insigniﬁcant percentage
of 3%. The application of rest glazing ratios, though, increased the risk in overheating dramatically. It is
also highlighted that the safe cases with Rmin in zone I were slightly over double the cases with R25 since
the latter ratio includes glazing with high solar heat gain coeﬃcients allowing higher solar radiation to
enter the zone.
As regards the window head and sill height, there was no impact of them on the overheating risk, since
the same glazing area based on the height parameter was widened or narrowed along the width of the
zone.
Among the parameters that were investigated, orientation was found to be the most determinant one,
followed by the glazing to ﬂoor ratio and window type. As the orientation is not always deﬁned by the
designer, all the rest parameters are to be combined in an optimal way in order to provide the desired
outcome. Thus, the glazing to ﬂoor ratio as one of the ﬁrst designer’s choice is the one to be assessed and
specify the rest set of parameters. For this reason, it was chosen to present for each glazing ratio scatter
plots of the relation of peak solar gains and criterion 1, ﬁltered by the orientation and window type. The
corresponding ﬁgures for each orientation and zone showing the scattering of cases for the glazing to
ﬂoor ratio and window type can be found in Figure C.21 to Figure C.28 in Appendix C.
Figure 5.35: Relation between peak solar gain per ﬂoor area and criterion 1 regarding the synthesis of
orientation and window type for the glazing to ﬂoor ratio Rmin in zone I (left) and II (right)
The glazing to ﬂoor ratio in zone I, as already mentioned, resulted in higher glazing area since the ﬂoor
area of zone I was greater than the ﬂoor area of zone II. This is the reason why using the glazing ratio
of Rmin in zone I, there were less cases and combinations of parameters that complied with criterion 1
compared to zone II, according to Figure 5.35. In zone II, there were only a few cases of Rmin with a
window type lowe in South, South-East and South-West that exceeded the overheating limit, but for not
more than 30 hours above 27°C. Therefore, window with the minimum glazing to ﬂoor ratio is recom-
mended to be placed in North, North-West, North-East, West and East with whichever of the window type
levels. On the other hand, even with a minimum glazing ratio in zone I, no case passed the criterion in
South, South-East and South-West, whereas all cases with North, North-East and North-West orientations
passed.
