5.2
Inﬂuence on daylight
The eﬀect of the parameters on the daylight illuminance levels is depicted in the two diﬀerent zones in
this section. Moreover, this section draws the agreement of each parameter with criterion 2 of sDA300,50%.
Considering a target illuminance of 300 lux, the evaluation of the parameters in terms of daylight avail-
ability within a space was carried out through the following metrics:
• spatial Daylight Autonomy (sDA300,50%)
• mean Daylight Autonomy (mean DA)
• average Daylight Factor (DFavg)
The following metric values were considered to indicate the electrical lighting demand:
• the percentage of space that Useful Daylight Illuminance is lower than 100 lux for more than 50%
of the occupied time (% of space UDI100>50%)
• the percentage of space that Useful Daylight Illuminance is within a range of 100 and 300 lux for
more than 50% of the occupied time (% of space UDI100-300>50%)
• the percentage of autonomous space where Useful Daylight Illuminance is within a range of 300
and 3000 lux for more than 50% of the occupied time (% of space UDI300-3000>50%)
This thesis did not perform a glare analysis for assessing the visual comfort, but the following metrics
were used for indicating likely glare conditions:
• the percentage of space that Useful Daylight Illuminance is higher than 3000 lux for more than 50%
of the occupied time (% of space UDI3000>50%)
• spatial Daylight Autonomy max (sDA3000,5%)
• mean maximum Daylight Autonomy (mean DAmax)
In this section, tables that show the failure percentage of cases for criterion 2 of spatial Daylight Auton-
omy (sDA300,50%) are presented for each parameter. Boxplots were chosen to illustrate the ranges of the
metrics for assessing each parameter in terms of daylight availability, artiﬁcial lighting demand and glare
occurrence. Particularly, the target DFavg for Copenhagen was 2.1%, as mentioned in section ??. More-
over, based on criterion 2, sDA300,50% should be more than 50%. Additionally, mean DA, as it expressed
the average value of the test points where the daylight autonomy was calculated, a minimum limit of 50%
and an upper limit of 65%, which is generally an indicator for quite adequate illuminance levels during
the annual occupied hours were set.
==PAGE:157==
5.2 Influence on daylight
135
It is considered essential to note that the ranges of mean DA can not be related to the ranges of sDA300,50%.
As mentioned in section 4.4.2, mean DA was calculated as the average of the daylight autonomy values
in the grid surface. However, sDA300,50% was calculated as the sum of the test points that have daylight
autonomy at least 50%, divided by the total number of test points. Therefore, the percentage of cases with
a mean DA more than 50% does not correspond to the percentage of cases with sDA300,50%.
5.2.1
Zone depth
Figure 5.38 documents the relation between criterion 2 agreement and the parameter of zone depth.
Figure 5.38: Criterion 2: Bin analysis for zone depth
As it is observed in the table above, more than half of the cases of zone I did not comply with criterion 2,
as good illumination levels could not be provided into its depth. However, the cases of zone II that failed
criterion 2 were half of the failing ones of zone I. Therefore, a strong relation between the zone depth and
the agreement with criterion 2 was obvious.
In Figure 5.39, the boxplots depict the numerical range of sDA300,50%, DFavg and mean DA for each zone
depth.
Figure 5.39: Boxplot of sDA300,50% (left), DFavg (middle) and mean DA300 (right) in relation to zone depth

According to Figure 5.39, in zone I and in zone II, 75% of cases complied with DFavg of 2.1%. However,
the opposite was depicted for sDA300,50% for the cases of zone I. In terms of spatial Daylight Autonomy,
only 25% of the space of zone I was autonomous in daylight, while zone II achieved sDA300,50% for more
than the double percentage of cases of zone I. Additionally, a great percentage of space of zone II ranging
from 80 to 100% achieved 300 lux for the half of occupied time in 25% of cases. At this point. it is worthy
noticing that cases in zone I were not so extreme as the ranges of sDA300,50% vary from around 25% to
70%. In contrast to zone II, where the extreme cases that did not comply with criterion 2 ranged from
10% to 50% of sDA300,50%, while those which complied reached 100%. This might be relevant to the great
range of glazing to wall ratio in zone II, which varies from 18% to 80%. Acccording to the right boxplot,
it is clear that the mean DA above 50% of the occupied time for zone I was hard to be achieved, whereas
more than 25% of the cases of zone II had higher than 65% of mean DA.

zone depth.
Figure 5.40: Boxplot of percentage of space with UDI100 > 50% (left), UDI100-300 > 50% (middle) and
UDI300-3000 > 50% (right) in relation to zone depth
Darkness was indicated in considerably more cases in zone I than in zone II. Figure 5.40 indicates that 10%
to around 50% of the space in zone I can be characterized dark for a considerable number of 75% of cases,
for half of the occupied hours. On the other hand, 25% of extreme cases in zone II experienced darkness
in a range between 10% to 70% of the space. However, this signiﬁcant range of the percentage of space
that was dark in zone II can be attributed to the smaller glazing areas created due to the minimum glazing
to ﬂoor ratio. Basically, this occured in 25% of cases which may indicate the corresponding percentage of
simulated cases with Rmin in zone II.
Furthermore, based on the middle boxplot which indicates the UDI-supplementary in space, 25% cases of
zone I needed possible additional artiﬁcial lighting for more than 20% of space. On the contrary, less than
20% of the space of cases in zone II needed artiﬁcial lighting for the same percentage of cases, despite
some extreme cases that were in need to almost 50% of their ﬂoor area.
Based on UDI300-3000>50%, the percentage of space that can be regarded autonomous by daylight in 75%
of cases ranged from about 40% to 50% in zone I, whereas in zone II the range was from about 40% to

100%. Essentially, concerning the right boxplot, 50% of cases in zone II did not need artiﬁcial lighting in

ranges of 100, 300 and 3000 lux can not be determined from these graphs, but only the autonomy in space

In Figure 5.41, the distributional characteristics of the metrics sDA3000,5% and mean DAmax are depicted
for each zone for the evaluation of glare risk. Thus, a limit of 5% as used for the evaluation of sDA3000,5%,
which is stated in the right boxplot of mean DAmax to set a duration that the glare problems could
be tolerable. These metrics refer to glare risks starting the evaluation of glare from this minimum strict
percentage of 5% of the occupied time. Both metrics are considered of high importance in order to perceive
the extent of duration, as well as the percentage of the space that glare problems may occur. Additionally,
the percentage of space with UDI3000>50% is also presented, to evaluate the possibility of glare, thus the
need for shading devices, in more than 50% of the occupied time. This metric was meaningful to estimate
if the beneﬁts of considerably high sDA300,50% in the space were overshadowed by glare conditions.

The sDA max result in zone II show that 50% of the cases have glare risks in 10 to 50% of their space above
the 5% threshold, meaning that up to half of the space is glary above 5% of the time. However, as seen in
the UDI result, there are not many instances that above 50% of the time is glary and this is limited to 10%
of the space in most cases. However, the sDA max result in zone I show that 50% of the cases had glare
occurrence in 1/3 of their space. The extreme cases of this zone had glare up to half of the space, while
25% of extreme cases in zone II had glare risks in 80% of their space. This spatial diﬀerence among the
two zones was anticipated since illuminance levels hardly reached over 300 lux in more than half of the
space of zone I as it was depicted in Figure 5.39. Looking at the mean DA3000, around 50% of the cases of
both zones did not reveal glare risks for less than 5% of the occupied hours.

5.2.2
Orientation
Figure 5.42 presents the relation between criterion 2 agreement and the parameter of zone orientation.
In particular, the orientations in cases of zone I with high risk in agreement with criterion 2 were North-
East, North, followed by East and North-West. Orientations with medium risks for not achieving 300 lux
for more than 50% of the space for at least half of the occupied hours were West and South-East. Low risk
orientations were South-West and South in zone I. The same risk scale arose for the orientation in zone II.
In fact, the table data for zone II are more representative for the evaluation of the eﬀect of orientation in
daylight, because the high failure percentages in zone I were strongly related to its depth. The color scale
of the data also show that the orientations of North tilted to east or west directions revealed higher failure
percentages than the orientation of south directions. Moreover, as can be seen, there is no orientation that
provided agreement with criterion 2 with all combinations of parameters. Therefore, attention should be
paid to the choice of the combinations which can be appropriate for a suﬃcient daylight provision.
Figure 5.42: Criterion 2: Bin analysis for orientation
Since Daylight Factor is calculated under an unobstructed CIE Standard Overcast Sky, there is no eﬀect
of orientation in its calculation, thus it was pointless to present it in a graph. So, just the distribution
of cases across spatial Daylight Autonomy in each orientation for each zone was depicted in Figure 5.43
through boxplots.
The boxplots clearly outline that in zone II, 50% of cases with south and south-west oriented spaces
fulﬁlled the Danish requirements for sDA300,50% in a ﬂoor area of 80% to 100%. However, 50% of cases in
North-West, North, North-East, and East passed criterion 2 in a ﬂoor area of 60% to 100%. On the other
hand, in zone I, 50% of cases with the low risk orientations revealed an sDA300,50% up to 70%. Nonetheless,
less than 25% of cases with the high risk orientations revealed an sDA300,50% from 50% to 55%. The boxplots
show that the agreement with criterion 2 was diﬃcult to be achieved in deep zones for every orientation
except for South and South-West.

5.2.3
Thermal mass
The eﬀect of the thermal mass in daylight penetration is visible because of the thickness of the wall
that the window was applied, casting some shadows in the space. Figure 5.49 demonstrates the relation
between criterion 2 agreement and the parameter of thermal mass.
Figure 5.49: Criterion 2: Bin analysis for thermal mass
According to Figure 5.49, the cases that failed criterion 2 with thermal mass TM1 in both zones are more
than the cases with TM2. This means that increased wall thickness yielded a slight decrease in spatial
Daylight Autonomy. The eﬀect of thermal mass in daylight, though, is more obvious in zone I. A percent-
age of 71% of its cases failed criterion 2 with TM1, which has a higher thickness than TM2, while 58% of
cases of this zone failed in spatial Daylight Autonomy with TM2. The shadowing eﬀect of TM1 in zone I
is attributed to the fact that this zone is hard to be illuminated into its depth, thus an extra obstruction
exacerbates the daylight insuﬃciency in its large ﬂoor area. Moreover, tables with the bin analysis of
each thermal mass in relation to orientation are presented in Figure C.29 and Figure C.30.
In Figure 5.50, the boxplots depict the numerical range of sDA300,50%, DFavg and mean DA of each thermal
mass level for each zone. These boxplots show the diﬀerences in daylight availability based on thermal
mass. Also, in Figure 5.50, it is apparently seen that the cases with TM1 reduced slightly the daylight
autonomy over 300 lux for at least half of the annual occupied hours in the space. This reduction was
greater in zone I, as already explained.

5.2.4
Window type
Figure 5.53 indicates the bin analysis of the parameter of window type regarding its relation with the
agreement with criterion 2. It explicates the strong relation of the window type with the criterion 2.
Since the visible transmittance is this property of the window type that matters in terms of daylight
access, the results were discussed based on this. As expected, cases with window types with low visible
transmittance had higher risk to fail in criterion 2. It is interesting to observe that 91% of cases with 5025
in zone I failed in criterion 2. The low visible transmittance of this window type involved in this high
failure percentage. In addition, it is worth noticing that more than 50% of the cases in zone I for each
level of window type failed criterion 2. On the other hand, less than 50% of cases in zone II for each level
of window type failed criterion 2. Moreover, the cases that failed with window type of 6633 and 7040 had
similar failure percentages in both zones due to the close values of their visible transmittance.

5.2.5
Glazing to ﬂoor ratio
Figure 5.60 documents the relation between agreement with criterion 2 and the parameter of glazing to
ﬂoor ratio for each zone.
Figure 5.60: Criterion 2: Bin analysis for glazing to ﬂoor ratio
The results presented in Figure 5.60 disclose the strong relation between glazing to ﬂoor ratio and daylight
availability in the space. That can be retrieved by observing that an increase of 10% in the glazing to ﬂoor
ratio, can reduce the percentages of failure in criterion 2 more than two times in each zone. Moreover,
the corrected ratio that is suggested by Danish building regulations as the minimum and could document
a suﬃcient daylight in a room, is proved inadequate, as the percentages of 84% and 99% of cases in zone I
and II indicate respectively. In order to ensure high probabilities in agreement with criterion 2, the glazing
to ﬂoor ratio needs to be increased signiﬁcantly. However, this observation can be made only based on
the occupancy schedule and not the daylit hours that the standard document, as already mentioned. It is
worth mentioning that in deep zones similar to zone I, there is no more than 60% likelihood of achieving a
spatial DA over 300 lux for more than 50% of the occupied time in more than 50% of the space, even with
a glazing to ﬂoor ratio of 25%, rendering such zones quite challenging. It is important to remind at this
point that the glazing area that comes from the glazing to ﬂoor ratio of 25% in zone I, resulted in almost
75% of the external wall area. Thus, the glazing area in such zones can not be much greater than this.
On the other hand, in zones such as zone II, glazing to ﬂoor ratio of 45% can ensure a certain a suﬃcient
spatial Daylight Autonomy almost for all cases.
Figure 5.61 and Figure 5.64 illustrate distribution in space of the daylight availability for each variation
level of glazing to ﬂoor ratio in each zone.
==PAGE:171==

The increase in agreement with criterion 2 if the glazing area is increased can be apparently seen in
Figure 5.61 and Figure 5.64. It is notable that the maximum space where the Daylight Autonomy ranged
was up to 70% in zone I with R25, while in zone II there were cases that reached the total ﬂoor area,
for the same or bigger ratios. Moreover, it can be observed that there were some cases with Rmin that
passed criterion 2 in the deep zone, whereas almost all cases with Rmin in the less deep zone II failed.
This is associated to the correction factor of the minimum glazing to ﬂoor ratio for deep zones up to 8 m
based on the Danish standard, which resulted in high glazing to wall ratio. In addition, the metric of DFavg
indicated a similar trend in agreement. The duration of the occupied time when a suﬃcient mean DA was
depicted, could be characterized as insuﬃcient for the most cases of zone I. The mean DA was reached
barely above the minimum required in a small group of cases, mainly with a ratio of 25%. Conversely, in
zone II, the two higher simulated glazing to ﬂoor ratios resulted in half or even more cases that had DA
over 65% of occupied time.

5.2.6
Window placement
This section presents, ﬁrstly, the inﬂuence of the window sill height and secondly, the inﬂuence of window
head height in daylight. Additional tables concerning the bin analysis for each glazing to ﬂoor ratio in
relation to the sill and head height can be found from Figure C.31 to ??.
Figure 5.67 documents the relation between agreement with criterion 2 and the parameter of window sill
height. It is considered essential to mention that the sill height was evaluated based on Hmax for zone I
and only the minimum glazing areas which covered less than 56% of the facade were taken into account.
For the evaluation of sill height in zone II, both window head heights Hmax and Hmin were considered,
in addition with all the minimum glazing to ﬂoor ratios arose from this less deep zone. Moreover, the
ratio 45% and 63% of glazing to facade with Hmax were taken into account in the evaluation of sill height.
Figure 5.67: Criterion 2: Bin analysis for window sill height
According to Figure 5.67, the vertical placement of the window at a sill height of 1 m in both zones was
preferable. However, the failure percentages of window placement at 1 m was still higher than 50%.
Increasing the window sill height means a wider distribution of glazing area in order for the glazing area
to be kept the same. The table data show that in zones where the distance from the window is longer,
such as zone I, cases with lower placed windows resulted in higher failure percentage in achieving the
acceptable spatial Daylight Autonomy. The lower placement of the windows also in this study meant
that the windows were narrower. Therefore, a high window placement but in a wider form, gives better
results.
Figure 5.68 presents the relation of the window sill height to the daylight availability for each zone in
three boxplots .
==PAGE:175==

height

2.1%. However, this is an unrealistic indication since about 50% of the cases with this sill height could not
achieve sDA300,50% in 50% of the space. The improvement in daylight autonomy with a sill height of 1 m
can be seen in the boxplot of sDA300,50% and mean DA, as well, in both zones. Instead of almost none of
the cases with S0 complying with the criterion 2 in zone I, the agreement of more cases, around 25%, was
achieved by increasing the sill height.

illuminance levels for the half of the annual working hours.

Figure 5.69: Boxplot of percentage of space with UDI100 > 50% (left), UDI100-300 > 50% (middle) and

Figure 5.70 illustrates the metrics for estimation of glare occurrence in case of window sill height param-
eter for each zone. It can be plainly viewed from this graph, that increase of sill height intensiﬁed glare
problems for both zones for more than 5% of the occupied time. Basically, the sDA max result show that
75% of the cases have 10 to 40% of their space above the 5% threshold when windows are higher-placed
in the deep zone. However, as seen in the UDI result, there are some extreme instances that above 50%
of the time their space is glary and this is limited to 5% of the space. In zone II, the sDA max show that
75% of the cases have 5 to 60% of their space above the 5% threshold when windows are higher-placed in
the less deep zone, while the UDI result depicts that there are also some extreme cases with glare up to
20% of their space for more than 50% of the time. Based on DAmax, the increase of sill height rose glare
problems in only 5% more of space over 5% of the occupied hours.

window sill height

5.2.7
Synthesis in daylighting
This section presents the summary of the results that were explained in the previous sections and also
the combinations of those parameters that had the greatest impact on daylight. Figure 5.75 displays the
number and percentage of cases for each level of parameter that passed criterion 2 for each zone type. The
data bars correspond to the percentage of cases that complied with the criterion 2 out of the simulated
cases for each level of parameter.
==PAGE:180==
158
Data analysis and results
Figure 5.75: Criterion 2: Bin analysis for the synthesis of all parameters
In overall terms, looking at Figure 5.75, all considered parameters seem to have an eﬀect in the daylight
autonomy of a zone. The parameter of zone depth seemed to have one of the greatest impact in achieving
suﬃcient daylight levels inside a zone. Comparing the zones, it is observed that in zone I the results
among the levels of parameters marked higher diﬀerences, and quite lower percentages in agreement
with criterion 2. The percentage of the cases in zone I that passed the criterion 2 was half compared to
the corresponding percentage of zone II that reached 66% out of the total simulated cases. It is important
to highlight that the higher reduction of succeeding cases between the two diﬀerent zones occurred in
the critical levels of the parameters.
North-East and North, were considered high risk orientations in daylight, followed closely by East and
North-West. The impact of the orientation on daylight conditions was intensiﬁed in the deeper zone.
Moreover, the direction of the facade of the deep zone in South, South-West and South-East is considered
quite beneﬁcial to agreement with criterion 2.
Thermal mass aﬀected the results of daylighting. That is attributed to the diﬀerence in wall thickness
that came along with the thermal mass. The shadowing eﬀect of the wall thickness of TM1 resulted to
approximately 15% to 5% reduction in passing cases in each zone respectively.
==PAGE:181==
5.2 Influence on daylight
159
The window type was strongly related to daylighting due to its property of visible transmittance. window
types with lower visible transmittance reserve higher percentages of solar radiation. Thus, as it was
anticipated, window type 5025 was the primary factor for the excessive failing cases in zone I, which
were signiﬁcantly more than those failed with lowe. Thus, the window type of 5025 is not advantageous
in zone I. Similarly, in one II this window type is not favourable but the failure percentage of 5025 was not
that high. Moreover, even though by increasing the visible transmittance, the agreement with criterion 2
rises, the percentage of passing cases of the rest window types did not demonstrate signiﬁcant deviations.
This is attributed to the close values of the visible transmittance of the rest window types.
The results revealed a signiﬁcant improvement in daylight conditions inside the zones with an increase in
glazing to ﬂoor ratio in both zones. In zone I, even with an increase of the glazing to ﬂoor ratio to R25, the
percentage of cases that complied with criterion 2 was not that high. In zone II, it is notable that almost
all cases failed passing criterion 2 with Rmin, while using a glazing to ﬂoor ratio of 25% the percentage
of cases climbed up to almost 70%.
Window sill height showed an inﬂuence on spatial daylight autonomy of the zones since it aﬀected the
distribution of the glazing area as resulted by the glazing to ﬂoor ratio parameter. It was observed that
a glazing area at 1 m distance from the ﬂoor level contributed to improve the daylight autonomy in
both zones, particularly in zone I. Moreover, the window sill height at the ﬂoor level showed a higher
percentage of agreement with criterion 2 in zone II compared to zone I. This was attributed to the higher
agreement of zone II with criterion 2. Due to the minimum glazing areas in zone I when they were
placed at the ﬂoor level, their distribution was more narrow in the facade. Whereas the distribution of
these glazing areas at 1 m was wider, hence, a higher passing percentage in zone I.
As regards the window head height, it showed a slight impact as already mentioned in the corresponding
section. The cases that passed criterion 2 were those with Hmax, even though the distribution of the
glazing area is more narrow. Thus, from the window sill and head height standpoint, the distribution of
glazing area can be beneﬁcial for daylight autonomy when is wide and placed at 1 m with a window head
as high as possible.
Figure 5.76 to Figure 5.78 present in boxplots the combination of some of the most inﬂuential parameters
on spatial Daylight Autonomy, separately for each glazing to ﬂoor ratio. These are the parameters of zone
depth, orientation, window type and window sill height. Regarding the window sill height, it is necessary
to highlight once again that when the glazing to ﬂoor ratio resulted in higher glazing areas than those
resulted from the settings of window placement, ﬁrstly the sill height was set. In particular, in case of the
window placement at S1 and Hmin, the head height increased for all cases in zone I. The same occurred
in zone II for glazing to ﬂoor ratios more than R25. Therefore, the graphs below indicate the window sill
height as the only parameters that aﬀects the window placement.
These graphs were considered essential to demonstrate the signiﬁcant points for which conﬁguration of
parameters is suitable or not in terms of agreement with criterion 2. There are more extreme cases in zone
II, due to the greater number of the simulated cases which arose from the variation of levels as regards
the glazing to ﬂoor ratio. The trend for the glazing to ﬂoor ratio Rmin and R25 was similar in the two
zones. The main diﬀerence is depicted in the higher ranges of sDA300,50% in zone II, where illuminance
levels can be easily distributed into its lower depth. Moreover, the agreement with criterion 2 is achieved
by using a sill height of 1 m in the deep zone. On the contrary, some cases with a sill height at 0 m in zone
II passed criterion 2 as well. The appropriate combinations of the parameters in each zone are presented
below.

More speciﬁcally, Figure 5.76 depicts that the agreement with criterion 2 in cases with Rmin in zone I

The glazing to ﬂoor ratio of R25 increased the agreement with criterion 2 in both zones. In zone I, the

However, in North, North-East or North-West, windows passed criterion 2 when placed at the ﬂoor level
with window types of lowe and occasionally with 7040.

Figure 5.78: Relation of sDA300,50% of glazing ratios R35 (left) and R45 (right) to orientation, window type

According to Figure 5.78, both glazing to ﬂoor ratios were proved determinant factors in providing suf-

the glazing ratio was R35 or R45, the cases were above the limit of 50% in sDA300,50%. Only the cases

Furthermore, windows with increased glazing surface arose from a glazing to ﬂoor ratio R35 or R45 and
placed at a sill height of S0 provided a signiﬁcant spatial daylight autonomy over the limit.
Although Figure 5.75 presents the combinations of parameters that were proﬁted in terms of higher than
50% of sDA300,50%, there might be an occurrence of glare risk in too high percentages. Thus, Figure 5.79 was
regarded as important to indicate which conﬁgurations of parameters might encounter glare issues. This
graph shows the combination of zone depth, orientation, glazing to ﬂoor ratio, window type and window
sill height, in terms of the percentage of space that glare problems might be occurred for more than 50%
of the annual occupied hours. As already mentioned, sDA3000,5% was considered more appropriate for a
glare estimation since glare conditions are commonly identiﬁed for more than 5% of the annual occupied
time. However, the percentage of space with UDI3000,50% was intended to be displayed in Figure 5.79 to
indicate in which cases the beneﬁts of high daylight autonomy (higher illuminance values than 3000 lux
for more than 50% of the time) were overshadowed by glare.

Figure 5.79: Glare risk: Bin analysis for all parameters
No glare conditions occurred in more than half of the annual occupied hours in North and North-East
orientations. Moreover, it is more essential to reveal some of the combinations that had glare risk in
higher percentages, such as in South, South-West and South-East. These orientations were deemed as
low risk orientations, because most of their cases complied with criterion 2. Figure 5.79 illustrates that
the combination of R25 for zone I, and R35 and R45 for zone II with a window type of lowe and 7040 at
a sill height of 1 m is prevalent. In addition, the diagram shows that the combination of Rmin with a
window type of lowe, 7040 and 6633 in zone I was also at risk in occurrence of glare conditions. This is
associated with its great depth, which led to high glazing to ﬂoor ratios, thus high glazing to wall ratios
due to these aforementioned window types. In zone II, it is clearly viewed that in almost all orientations,
glare problems might occur also in some cases with a placement of the window at the ﬂoor level. This
could be tied to the lower depth of this zone, where good illumination levels can be distributed in most
of the space.
5.3
Synthesis of all investigation ﬁelds
Figure 5.80 indicates the parameters which passed both criterion 1 for less overheating hours than 25 in
case that operative temperature exceeds 27C and criterion 2 for daylight suﬃciency in space. At ﬁrst
sight, the percentages of cases that passed both criteria are not promising at all. As regards zone depth,
no more than 4% of the cases simulated in zone I were able to pass both criteria at the same time. The
corresponding percentage of the cases of zone II was higher, but still low enough, reaching only 13%.
Since the combinations that were evaluated for window sill and head height were not the same as for the
evaluation of the rest parameters, were separated because the passing cases were retrieved from the total
cases that were evaluated. These ﬁndings revealed some conﬁgurations of these parameters that were
good enough to comply with the strict requirements for avoiding overheating and providing suﬃcient
==PAGE:186==
164
Data analysis and results
daylight autonomy in the space simultaneously. Thus, with no further implementation of strategies, they
can set the basis for decision making in the early design stages.
Figure 5.80: Criterion 1 and 2: Bin analysis for the synthesis of all parameters
The results show that both overheating and daylight conditions were more satisfactory in north-oriented
oﬃce rooms than for south-oriented rooms. More speciﬁcally, this low percentage of agreement of North
with both criteria in zone I is associated with the failure in the daylight criterion, since north oriented deep
zones did not experience overheating issues at all. Furthermore, there was no case with the investigated
parameters in both zones that complied with both criterion 1 and 2 in South-East. In case of zone II,
even though 70% of the cases passed criterion 2 in this orientation, none of them coincided with the
cases that passed criterion 1. Additionally, zone I was unable to pass criterion 1 on South-East, South-
West and South with any of the conﬁgurations of parameters, since a total failure was marked in these
orientations in the deep zone. Thus, the exclusion of these orientations for the synthesis of criteria was
anticipated. Since no combination of these parameters was proved suﬃcient enough to avoid overheating
and simultaneously achieve a sDA300,50% in at least half of the space, other strategies should be followed
to provide a solution in order to comply with the requirement.
The agreement in both criteria was achieved with a wall thickness of the thermal mass TM2, and it is tied
to the less shadowing eﬀect of this wall in space. Therefore, even though thermal mass showed a slightly
==PAGE:187==
5.3 Synthesis of all investigation fields
165
better performance in overheating risk, but it was not that clear, its thickness should be always taken into
account for its shadowing eﬀect in terms of daylight.
As far as the window type is concerned, the ﬁndings in the two zones are contradictory. In zone I, no case
with window type 5025 complied with both criteria, while in zone II, this window type performed best,
marking the highest passing percentage. This can be explained further as follows. Overheating problems
in zone I were not that considerable compared to the other zone. However, the required spatial daylight
autonomy in this deep zone was diﬃcult to be achieved. The results showed that by increasing the
visible transmittance, the agreement with criterion 2 was also increased. Thus, as Figure 5.80 indicates,
the agreement with both criteria can be achieved with the window type 7040. The window type lowe
could not be a safer choice for this zone to allow more daylight to penetrate as its high solar heat gain
coeﬃcient resulted to signiﬁcant overheating risks for both zones. In contrast, in case of the less deep
zone, the choice of window type 5025 can be considered the most preferable. This choice was beneﬁcial to
control the excessive solar heat gains observed in this zone. On the other hand, as the agreement with the
daylight criterion was more possible to be achieved, the choice of this type together with an appropriate
glazing to ﬂoor ratio could give the desired results in terms of daylight. Therefore, the window type that
is to be used in a zone should be depended on the zone depth.
Looking at the ﬁnding of glazing ratios in Figure 5.80, as a general comment can be drawn the fact that
the minimum glazing to ﬂoor ratio that the Danish Building Regulation 2018 (BR18) has set as a limit
to provide suﬃcient daylight in space was not enough to cover both criterion 1 and 2 in most cases. A
greater glazing to ﬂoor ratio of 25% or 35% performed better, showing greater passing percentages. The
glazing to ﬂoor ratio R45 even though resulted to almost 100% success in criterion 2, increased the risk of
overheating thus, the decision of its use should be made with precautions.
The window height based on sill and head height as mentioned, inﬂuenced daylight autonomy but not
the overheating. Thus, the eﬀect of these parameters on agreement with both criteria, basically followed
the same trend as in criterion 2 only. The vertical placement of the window together with the right
window ratios were considered safe design options for being compatible with both criteria. In zone II 25
cases passed with Hmax which had a sill height at S0 with glazing to ﬂoor ratios R25 and R35, while one
passing cases with this head height included S1 with Rmin. As regards the window head height Hmin, the
compatible cases with both criteria included the highest glazing to ﬂoor ratios placed at the ﬂoor level,
and one case with Rmin placed at 1 m. In zone I, as for the head height, only one case with Hmax at the
ﬂoor level with the highest glazing ratio passed. Regarding the window sill height in the deep zone, apart
from this case with Hmax and S0, two cases with S1 and Rmin were compatible. These ﬁndings indicate
the balance in both glazing ratios and window placement in order to achieve the daylight criterion and
overheating criterion simultaneously. The sill height at 1 m was combined with a minimum glazing area
in order to achieve the desired daylight availability, while the vertical window placement at the ﬂoor level
was combined with high glazing ratios in order to give the optimal for daylight.
The aforementioned combinations of the simulated parameters that passed both criteria are presented
in Figure 5.81 and Figure 5.82 for each zone. Only the parameters that were most inﬂuential for both
overheating and daylight were synthesized. Each combination is scattered for spatial daylight autonomy
over 300 lux in more than 50% of the space for half of the occupied time in relation to the aggregated
hours above 27°C, ﬁltered by the parameters of orientation, window type and glazing to ﬂoor ratio.
==PAGE:188==
166
Data analysis and results
Figure 5.81: Synthesis of orientation, window type and glazing to ﬂoor ratio in zone I for agreement
with criterion 1 and 2
According to Figure 5.81, it is apparently seen that from a combined daylight and thermal standpoint,
the direction of the facade and the window of a deep zone in North, North-West and North-East provides
agreement with both criteria. In these orientations, the optimum size of the window for the investigated
deep zone is the one that arose from R25, creating a glazing surface in 73% of the facade. More speciﬁcally,
the window type lowe can be used in north oriented windows, whereas the window type 7040 can be used
in all the aforementioned orientations. Additionally, in north-west oriented windows, the window type
6633 is an alternative. Furthermore, the direction of the window in West is also proved safe in terms of a
combined agreement with criteria, but only in case of glazing to ﬂoor ratio Rmin with window types 6633
and 7040. These combinations of glazing ratio and type mean that a glazing area facing west, covering
47-52% of the facade for the aforementioned window types, can be also a design decision.
==PAGE:189==
5.3 Synthesis of all investigation fields
167
Figure 5.82: Synthesis of orientation, window type and glazing to ﬂoor ratio in zone II for agreement
with criterion 1 and 2
According to Figure 5.82, the minimum glazing to ﬂoor ratio is only proper for a south oriented window
with window types 6633 and lowe. The glazing part can cover 18-22% of the south facade with these
combinations. This can be attributed to the small glazing area that allows the direction to be in a high
risk orientation in terms of overheating, such as the south orientation, with window types that have high
Tvis values to enhance the daylight penetration. It could be noticed that most passing cases included
the glazing to ﬂoor ratio R25 and R35 mainly directed in North, North-West and North-East. However,
some cases with R25 in West, South-West and East with a window type 5025 complied with both criteria.
Even though these orientations were favorable in daylight, they were unfavorable in overheating, and the
overheating risks were compensated by the use of this window type with low SHGC value to reduce the
solar radiation in the space. In north, north-west and north-east windows with a glazing area covering
45% of the facade such as this arose from R25, the choice of 6633 and 7040 are desired. However, it is
interesting to observe that the window type 5025 can be used also in north-west windows, and lowe in
the most unfavorable orientation for daylight, which is the North. The increase of the glazing to ﬂoor
ratio by 10% resulted in 63% of the facade which can be combined with the window types 6633, 7040 and
lowe only in North, North-West and North-East. The glazing to ﬂoor ratio R45 in those orientations is
also preferable only combined with the use of a window type 5025. Its use is associated with the lower
SHGC, to control the solar heat gains from the glazing area of 80% in the facade created by R45. However,
since North is the most favorable in terms of overheating, 6633 and 7040 can also be chosen to improve
the daylight availability in the space.A general observation though can be that lowe is an option only in
low glazing ratios in north-oriented window, otherwise overheating issues are caused.
==PAGE:190==
168
Data analysis and results
5.4
Inﬂuence on health potential
This section presents the ﬁndings of the simulated parameters of the daylighting workﬂow in Lark Spec-
tral Lighting. This simulation workﬂow was set up so as to raise critical questions about balancing the
thermal discomfort with the daylight beneﬁts. Basically, a comparison between two cases, a worst case
I_TM2_lowe_S0_Hmax_R25 and best case I_TM1_5025_S0_Hmin_Rmin in terms of overheating, was made
to contrast the overheating risks with health beneﬁts of daylight. In order to avoid misunderstandings,
for now on, the worst case is referred to as case A, while the best case as case B. To be more speciﬁc, the
impact of the parameter of orientation and the two sky types, overcast and clear, was investigated on the
entrainment of circadian response for a person sitting in the middle of the zone I, looking at the wall.
As already mentioned in section 3.3, it was not intended to deﬁne the circadian stimulus of the person
who is working in the oﬃce zone by the exposure to daylight. The objective, though, was to examine the
eﬀectiveness of the parameters in daylight potential for human health. Thus, a criterion for instigating
100% of circadian stimulus was not necessary to be deﬁned. The ﬁndings can be used to make recommen-
dations according to the extent the orientation parameter beneﬁts human health in two sky illumination
distributions as regards the daylight provision for resetting the circadian rhythms. Moreover, an assess-
ment of the impact of diﬀerent window types can be assumed, since the glazing area of these two cases,
as it is explained in section 3.3, is similar. The results were based on data that were derived from the
photopic and the circadian curves of Rea and Lucas from the workﬂow.
The line charts in Figure 5.83 and Figure 5.84 are displayed to depict the diﬀerence of the photopic and
circadian curves in both cases. In this way, case A with the highest risk in overheating in South-East
and case B with the lowest risk in overheating in North which represents also a high risk orientation in
daylight, were compared.
These line charts show the hourly results of photopic and circadian illuminances, as simulated, for each
case under an overcast sky on January 13th and a clear sky on May 30th. These combinations of the date
and sky type were compared in order to investigate the extreme worst case scenario in winter and the best
case scenario in spring in terms of the human circadian response. Even though the results were retrieved
from point in time simulations, straight lines were drawn connecting the hourly results indicating the
possible intermediate value of the hours that were not simulated.
Figure 5.83: Line chart of the case A and case B on January 13th with overcast sky
==PAGE:191==
5.4 Influence on health potential
169
Looking at Figure 5.83, it can be seen that none curve of case A exceeded 50 lux, whereas the maximum
value for the curves of case B was 15 lux. The reason for these low values is mainly the overcast sky
that was chosen for this winter day, since such sky conditions are more probable to occur in Denmark
on January. Moreover, due to the small duration of the daytime in winter, all curves, even the photopic
resulted in zero values at 8:00, 16:00 and 18:00. These low values combined with the small duration of
exposure were of high risk for the disruption of the circadian rhythms. Interestingly, this graph indicates
that the right combination of the parameters for controlling the overheating problems highly impacts
the human health potential, since this case is also representative of the highest risk in agreement with
criterion 2.
Figure 5.84: Line chart of the case A and case B on May 30th with clear sky
On May 30th, both photopic and circadian results were escalated, as Figure 5.84 depicts. The photopic
values reached up to 1600 lux in case A, whereas only 600 lux in case B. Rea curve’s results were constantly
above 600 circadian-lux from 8:00 till 14:00 in case A. This duration of exposure is regarded as satisfactory
for the regulation of circadian system. Similarly, the exposure in case B during these hours ﬂuctuated
above 200 up to 400 circadian-lux according to Rea and Lucas curve.
In both ﬁgures, circadian curves had similar pattern to the photopic one, as it was anticipated. The
diﬀerence between the circadian curves was attributed to the way they calculate circadian values. It was
observed that the values of the circadian-lux were always proportional to the value of the photopic lux.
Therefore, since consistency was observed among the results of the two circadian curves and the photopic
one, only the results of the Rea model are presented below.
The radal charts in Figure 5.85 and Figure 5.87 show the circadian illuminances of the two cases for the
two sky conditions respectively. The circadian values retrieved from Rea curve were presented for each of
the selected dates of the year row-wise and for the time of day column-wise. It is remarkable to note that
the results can not predict the accurate circadian response of a person that enters the oﬃce at another
time withing the working schedule, instead of 8:00 which is the start for the light dose.
==PAGE:192==
170
Data analysis and results
Figure 5.85: Radar charts of REA curves for case A and case B in relation to orientation under clear sky
conditions
Figure 5.85 presents the radar charts for clear sky for case A and case B. It can be plainly viewed that
case A shows a greater non-visual health potential than case B. In addition, the rate of the circadian
response is higher in case A. This was expected since case A is the worst case in overheating, but had a
suﬃcient daylight admission, which means that its daylight potential could contribute more to enhance
health beneﬁts. This is attributed to the high Tvis of the window type used in case A. Moreover, it can be
seen that the magnitude of circadian response in case B was quite low, as circadian illuminances did not
exceed 500 circadian-lux in any date and time. Case B, which is the extreme case that failed criterion 2
and passed criterion 1 depicts that its low sunlight exposure can lead to detrimental eﬀects on subsequent
sleep quality. On the other hand, case A yielded three times higher circadian-lux units. In both cases, the
circadian values marked the maximum ones at noon. In particular, the circadian response increased from
the morning and after reaching the maximum in the middle of the day, and decreased in the afternoon.
For shorter and darker days, such as on January 13th and February 28th, the light dose that can stimulate
the circadian system was substantially lower in the beginning and in the end of the day. On January 13th,
the circadian values at 8:00 and at 16:00 were minimal, while the last hour of the working schedule the cir-
cadian system stopped functioning. Similarly, on February 28th, the circadian values at those hours were
insigniﬁcant for both cases, even though there were some negligible circadian illuminances at 18:00. In
the spring days, though, the results of non-visual health potential in the ﬁrst and last two hours increased
due to the longer daytime in the spring. However, the diagram shows that from 10:00 to 14:00 for both
cases the light exposure had less eﬀect on resetting the phase of circadian system, since the values were
remaining approximately the same.
It was also interesting to observe that after 2 hours entering the oﬃce on February 28th, the circadian-lux
units rocketed from around 200 lux to 1000 lux in case A. Whereas, as regards case B, the circadian-lux units
reached the peak at 500 lux at 10:00 for two hours more in the same date. At noon, the non-visual health
potential reached the peak of 1500 lux, and then started dropping below 1000 lux reaching approximately
==PAGE:193==
5.4 Influence on health potential
171
200 lux at 16:00. This considerable increase of circadian function in the ﬁrst two hours of the occupancy
schedule can be indicated in every date. However, the ﬂuctuation of circadian illuminances was more
moderate in the spring days.
The results of case A are more visible so they can be used to investigate what is the situation in each
orientation. The radar charts show that the parameter of orientation did not aﬀect the stimulation of
the circadian system to a considerable extent. This is associated with the impact of view direction on
the circadian-lux values in each orientation. The circadian levels for a person facing the window would
result in higher diﬀerences among the diﬀerent orientation levels. Furthermore, it is interesting to denote
from the graphs that the internal circadian body clock can be synchronized to a great extent even in
North orientation. It is also worth noticing that in the winter months in the early and mid-morning, the
orientations of North, North-East, East, South-East, South were more preferable to circadian entrainment.
When it was close to sunset, the West orientation was more favourable. Similar observations can be made
for the spring months, but as the sunset is later in the evening, the positive eﬀect of the orientations in
each period of time, in the circadian regulation was extended until 16:00. Simulation of clear sky in every
hour in every date was presented in Figure 5.86 to clarify these results. On April 14th, in all orientations
the circadian light levels marked the peak, with the South, East and North-East at 12:00 until 14:00 to
have the highest levels. On May 30th the distribution of illuminances in each orientation was suﬃcient
during the day, leading to great circadian health potential.
Figure 5.86: Simulation of clear sky on each date and time
==PAGE:194==
172
Data analysis and results
Figure 5.87: Radar charts of REA curves for case A and case B in relation to orientation under overcast
sky conditions
Figure 5.87 demonstrates the radar charts of case A and case B for overcast sky. It is clear from this
graph as well that case A has a greater non-visual health potential than case B. In addition, it can be
explicitly observed that the overcast sky resulted in considerably lower circadian-lux than clear sky. The
circadian illuminances were below 100 lux in the winter months, while in the spring months these values
slightly rose to approximately 100 circadian-lux units from 10:00 to 14:00. However, the rate of instigating
circadian rhythms in the morning is much lower in the spring days than it was depicted in Figure 5.85.
The rate of increase of daylight illuminance levels from 8:00 to 10:00 is quite low under overcast skies,
which means that the circadian light levels do not rise signiﬁcantly in the morning as under clear skies.
Despite that, roughly constant circadian values appeared from 10:00 to 14:00 for all the dates.
As regards the orientation, any relation of this parameter to the health potential under an overcast sky
can be drawn. This is tied to the fact that the daylight levels do not depend on the window direction
when the daylighting simulation is performed under an overcast sky.
==PAGE:195==
Chapter 6
Discussion
One of the most signiﬁcant advancements of the modern era is the ability to construct comfortable and
brightly lit buildings, as occupants and their needs are of high priority. Large glazing surfaces on facades
cause high overheating risks, on one hand, but assure healthy buildings from a daylight viewpoint on the
other hand. Therefore, research for how to make healthier buildings is growing recently and focuses on
the technologies of facade systems.
In Denmark, overheating problems occur in a limited period throughout a year. Overcast skies are more
often, meaning low levels of daylight autonomy, thus people are not exposed to suﬃcient sunlight for
enough time during a working day. This stresses on the need for the designer to follow strategies to im-
prove the daylight and not neglect it rather than focusing solely on the overheating prevention strategies
from early design stages. Moreover, the use of sophisticated and advanced design practices for assur-
ing visual and biological human health beneﬁts ensures the right direction of the design. Nevertheless,
architects, lighting designers, engineers, contractors and owners have diﬀerent priorities when making
their design and construction decisions. In other words, their focus is on diﬀerent domains, meaning
that energy relevant decisions which are taken in early design phase may lead the design to wrong direc-
tions. Most of the time, the thermal indoor environment is designed apart from daylighting. As a result,
conﬂicts among the practitioners are created, leading to iterative loops. Hence, a design equilibrium be-
tween overheating risks and daylight potentials has to be achieved from the conceptual phase through a
wise planning of the facade and the windows assessing various alternatives. Therefore, special attention
should be paid evaluating the impact of designers’ choices in order to minimize the cost and maximize
the quality of the design.
Parametric modelling has been proved essential to test various design alternatives that can be bench-
marked to drive conscious design decisions. Thus, a new parametric tool was developed to assess facade
design parameters in two oﬃce zones, one narrow deep and one narrow less deep zone. A challenge
to combine independent parameters and evaluate them based on the Danish building regulations and
standards, was met through an integrated simulation of overheating and daylighting. The tool does not
focus on precise design, but aims at informing architects about the inﬂuence of their design thereafter.
Additionally, the tool brings into the daylight health potentials of sunlight exposure in an additional day-
lighting analysis. The intention was to increase the awareness of designing healthy daylit spaces along
with comfortable thermal indoor conditions.
The results are discussed and interpreted in this chapter. In more practical terms, it is attempted to argue
about the patterns, which are intriguing and/or not easily explainable. In this chapter, when overheating
==PAGE:196==
174
Discussion
hours are mentioned, it is meant, occupied hours that exceeded 27°C. There was no case that failed cri-
terion 1a, but passed criterion 1b, meaning that it is more possible the operative temperature to exceed
27°C for more than 25 hours, than 26°C for more than 100 hours. Thus, even though the data analysis and
the results of overheating hours were presented in chapter 5 in tables for both criteria 1a and 1b, only the
exceeded hours of the operative temperature above 27°C, were commented.
It is essential to state that overheating hours were aﬀected by the internal heat gains, ventilation ﬂow
rates, and also, the solar heat gains. The internal heat gains referring to the number of occupants, equip-
ment, and lighting were adjusted based on the depth of the zone. Moreover, the volume ﬂow rates of the
ventilation were based on the number of occupants and the ﬂoor area, keeping a constant airﬂow during
the occupied hours. Additionally, as described in subsection 4.3.6, the supply air temperature of the venti-
lation system was the outdoor air temperature during the cooling season, preventing the ventilation from
favoring the overheating problem. So, the overheating hours were mostly aﬀected by solar heat gains.
Even though overheating is a dominant problem during the summer period, it can occur in the transition
spring and autumn period as well. So, the period for choosing the peak solar heat gains was deﬁned,
based on the operative temperature of the case that showed the most overheating problems. That case
was the II_TM2_SE_lowe_S0_Hmax_R45 and its hourly operative temperatures during the whole year are
depicted in Figure 6.1. Overheating issues were experienced from April to September since the hourly
operative temperature exceeds 26°C most of the time. As a result, the overheating assessment was con-
ducted during this period. The period of overheating assessment is delimited between the dot lines from
April to September in Figure 6.1.
Figure 6.1: Operative temperature of case II_TM2_SE_lowe_S0_Hmax_R45
Furthermore, this chapter invites for an open discussion concerning the potential design conﬂicts of the
facade and window design in a narrow deep and a narrow less deep zone related to agreement with
overheating and daylight criteria. The link between the investigated parameters to compromise both
overheating and daylighting is also discussed extensively. This chapter ends with a discussion regarding
the non-visual health potentials in oﬃces.
==PAGE:197==
6.1 Zone depth
175
6.1
Zone depth
As for the overheating results, even though no relation was drawn between the parameter of zone depth
and agreement with the overheating criterion, the amount of aggregated hours above the limits and the
temperature diﬀerences of the threshold operative temperatures, characterized the zone with the less
depth as more critical. Basically, an extremely high number of overheating hours occurred in the less
deep zone. The number was three times more than the number of overheating hours arose from the deep
zone. This is associated due to its smaller ﬂoor area. Therefore, a deep zone is less prone to high and
longer overheating issues due to the distribution of the solar heat gains in a great ﬂoor area. In general,
the high number of overheating hours can be tied to the airtightness and the ventilation strategy followed.
More speciﬁcally, strategies to cool down the zone were not implemented since the objective of the study
was to assess the eﬀect of the parameters in overheating and not to use prevention strategies to optimize
the thermal conditions. Thus, the heat was accumulated inside without being transferred to outdoors.
An ideal air loads system supplied air at the minimum ﬂow rate based on occupancy and ﬂoor area. This
system operated in a way that the supply air temperature was the outdoor air temperature that in some
limited hours was above 25°C, intensifying the overheating problem.
On the other hand, from the daylight viewpoint, strong relation was indicated between this parameter
and agreement with the daylight criterion. The results of the daylight penetration were more or less
expected, since facades have a limited ability to distribute daylight into the depth of a space (Ruck et al.,
2000). The deep zone had a higher failure percentage, in particular around the double, since suﬃcient
illuminance levels could not be provided into its depth. In contrast, 25% of cases in the less deep zone
experienced 3000 lux for more than 5% of the occupied time for at least half of the space. This indicates
the need for solar shading devices when that extreme illuminance levels occur for long periods.
From a combined perspective of overheating and daylight, the percentages of agreement with both criteria
were quite low, revealing the necessity to follow other strategies. The narrow and shallow zone was
diﬃcult to fulﬁll the overheating criterion with the right combinations whereas the narrow and deep
zone was more critical to achieve the daylight requirement. Natural ventilation or solar shading systems
are necessary to control the solar heat gains for both zones. Light-directing prismatic glazing or light
shelves can be used to achieve deep penetration of natural light (Ruck et al., 2000) in the deep zone.
Moreover, improved reﬂectance materials are suggested to achieve properly daylit spaces in deep spaces
(Luca et al., 2019).
6.2
Orientation
The orientation of the facade plays a decisive role in the operative temperature of the zone since it deter-
mines the amount of solar radiation to that the zone is exposed. This parameter, along with the glazing
deﬁnes the total solar heat gains that penetrate the zone and may result in overheating problems. On the
other hand, orientation can act positively to control solar heat gains and supply suﬃcient daylight. Thus,
the internal heat gains coming from artiﬁcial lighting can be reduced, contributing to the reduction of
energy consumption.
It should be noted though that the orientation of one zone is not always determined by the designer. The
plot and shape of the building often limit the choices and some zones have to be placed in unfavorable
orientations. Thus, the orientation as a parameter cannot be prohibitive, but instead, it can be the factor
==PAGE:198==
176
Discussion
to determine other choices, such as the glazing ratio and window type to control solar radiation. All of
them can form a combination along with other facade design parameters to control the solar heat gains
that the zone is expected to receive according to its orientation. Thus, the solar heat gains to which the
zones are subject in each orientation are of high signiﬁcance.
The levels of the parameter of orientation that led to high overheating risk, led to low risks in daylight
insuﬃciency contrarily. Unfavorable orientations in overheating were South, South-West and South-East,
with East and West to follow. In the deep zone, South, South-West and South-East are considered unsafe
since the failure percentage of the cases reached 100%. This is attributed to the higher glazing areas
of this zone, that in combination with these unfavorable orientations led to failure. For south oriented
facades, overheating can be avoided by adding horizontal shading, but an additional shading may lead
to less daylight for zones with a larger depth. Furthermore, North is considered a safe direction for all
the conﬁgurations in the deep zone. Similarly, North is considered the safest among the others also for
less deep zones since the failure percentage was quite low. Due to prevention of overheating in north
oriented rooms, more ﬂexibility with regard to the choice of the fenestration system is feasible in both
zones. In the less deep zone, the failure percentages in the unfavorable orientations were high enough
to limit design choice. The failure percentages of West and East were quite similar, but also high enough
in the less deep zone. Therefore, there is no dilemma regarding the orientation choice between West and
East in narrow and shallow zones. On the contrary, in the deep zone more attention should be paid in
East to address the overheating issues compared to West. Adjustable vertical shading devices can protect
windows which facing West and East, which are subject to low sun angles all year around.
Unfavorable orientations in daylight were North, North-East, North-West and East, particularly in the
deep zone. Since north-oriented windows are considered safe in terms of overheating, the right combina-
tion of window parameters can be chosen freely in order to fulﬁll the daylight target. Whereas South-West
and South were the most favorable orientations. It was hard to achieve agreement with criterion 2 in deep
zones in all orientations except for South-West and South. On the other hand, in the less deep zones, the
passing percentages were more than 50% without showing high deviations in each orientation. West
orientation is proved to be a bit safer option in both zones compared to East from the perspective of
daylight. Obviously, the orientation parameter creates design conﬂicts and the balance that is needed to
be achieved is delicate concerning the design options that should be applied. For example, solar shading
devices can be implemented in deep and less deeper rooms, especially in south oriented windows, to
compromise either thermal comfort or daylighting. The health potential is necessary to be weighted as
well in the decision-making providing a more human environment. This human-centric approach gives
advantage to daylight as overheating can be controlled by other passive ways, such as natural ventilation
or solar control systems.
According to lark daylighting simulation, the parameter of orientation did not depict any strong inﬂuence
with the health beneﬁts from daylight, especially under overcast skies in Denmark. This is associated with
the direction of the viewpoint which was set for a sitting person to face the side wall. The results are
expected to indicate a more clear impact if the person was facing the window, receiving more photopic
and circadian light than the person sitting parallel to the window (Inanici et al., 2015). In addition, it is also
predicted that increased distance from the window, inﬂuences the human circadian system. This would
be visible if viewpoints of occupants sitting in the back of the zone I would be simulated. Furthermore,
an occupant that is sitting closer to the window is supposed to be exposed to more sunlight and beneﬁted
more according to the non-visual health eﬀect. So the slight impact of orientation in improving the
regulation of circadian rhythms is owing to this setting as well. Eastern and western exposures to sunlight
provides better light at the beginning and ends of workdays. However, southern exposures improve the
performance of the occupants over the duration of the workday (Pechacek et al., 2008). Morning light
==PAGE:199==
6.3 Thermal mass
177
exposure is more eﬀective due to the bluish white light, thus accelerating the grogginess in the morning
(Andersen, Mardaljevic, & Lockley, 2012). On the other hand, afternoon exposure to sunlight could disrupt
the circadian system, by increasing the alertness late in the day (Andersen et al., 2012). So low circadian-
lux units are recommended in the evening to avoid sleep latency and low sleep quality. East oriented
oﬃce buildings, even though require appropriate solutions against overheating, are suggested because of
high daylight levels in the morning, to enhance the sensation of workers’ alertness.
6.3
Thermal mass
Thermal mass is an essential factor to consider when choosing a construction system. However, an in-
signiﬁcant relation was found between the thermal mass parameter and the overheating criterion. Simi-
lar failing percentages in criterion 1 were identiﬁed, even though more hours above 27°C arose from the
construction type of steel panels. Usually, spaces with thermally more massive materials experience less
temperature ﬂuctuation and less overheating hours in extreme outdoor air temperatures. By storing or
ejecting a large amount of heat, thermal mass moderates the internal temperature of the space, by aver-
aging out diurnal day and night extreme temperatures, controlling in this way the overheating problem.
Due to the placement of the two thermal mass levels to only the building envelope, the results do not
clearly show the ability of an increased thermal mass to lower the indoor temperature ﬂuctuations. Both
curves of thermal mass depicted a similar pattern, without smoothing the heat peaks signiﬁcantly during
the summer. At this point, it is signiﬁcant to point out that concrete material was also used on the ﬂoor.
This fact, in combination with the high glazing to ﬂoor ratios applied to the facade, explains the reason
why the eﬀect of thermal mass was not visible in this thesis.
Concerning heating energy savings, due to the accumulation of the heat gains during the day and the
release of them at night, the heating energy demand is expected to be reduced more with the thermal
mass of concrete wall system. However, heating energy is not contained in the investigation of this
thesis. The eﬀect of the thermal mass would be more positive in the residential buildings during the
heating season when the heating demand would be reduced at night. But in oﬃce buildings, thermal
mass is essential in mitigating the discomfort from overheating and thus, needs to be correctly used in
the surfaces and the right facade orientations to avoid intensiﬁcation of the overheating problem.
It should be denoted that in general, suﬃcient thermal capacity is required to reserve part of the accu-
mulated heat for reducing the overheating problem during the summertime. However, in this project,
the parameter of the thermal mass was applied only to the facade. As a result, the eﬀect of the thermal
mass parameter was not apparent on overheating levels. Presumably, the impact of this parameter could
be more visible if it would be applied to all the construction elements compensating the variations of the
thermal load diﬀerently.
Thermal mass is essential to be coupled with an adequate ventilation strategy, which can discharge the
thermal cycles quickly by extracting the heat stored in the building and prepare the envelope for another
thermal cycle. In this way, its potential to reduce overheating hours to reach the comfort thresholds of the
Danish building regulations is maximized by appropriate shading, ventilation, and proper glazing areas
appropriately oriented.
From a daylight standpoint, the wall construction has shadowing eﬀect, due to its wall thickness. The
increased wall thickness of concrete resulted in slightly higher failure percentages in criterion 2. This
eﬀect was more visible in the deep zone, since the non-agreement with criterion 2 was at higher risk
==PAGE:200==
178
Discussion
generally in this zone. The passing percentages of thermal mass in both criteria were based on those
which revealed agreement with the daylight criterion.
6.4
Window type
Solar heat gains are inseparably linked to the window type as the right choice of a window type can
constrain them, while allowing adequate daylight to penetrate. Window types with low solar heat gain
coeﬃcient and low visible transmittance decrease overheating risks but decrease daylight penetration
respectively. A strong relation between window type and agreement with criteria 1 and 2 was found.
As regards the overheating, almost the same failure percentages were identiﬁed for both zones. Moreover,
it is important to point out that the solar gains were more controlled by a window type 5025 with a SHGC
equal to 0.25 rather than by the rest window types. On the other hand, using a window type with around
double the solar heat gain coeﬃcient such as lowe the solar gains can reach extremely high values leading
to a considerable amount of overheating hours. Therefore, it is hard to alleviate the overheating issue
and careful selection of the proper window conﬁguration is of high importance. Additionally, the failure
percentage arose from the window type 7040 was just a bit higher than 6633 because the SHGC value of
these two types were close. So when a designer wants to control the solar radiation entering the space by
using another window type, the choice should be based on SHGC that is quite diﬀerent from the initial.
In contrast, using window types with low visible transmittance, increases the risk to fail the daylight to
a great extent. Again, the failure percentage arose from the window type 7040 was a bit lower than 6633
because the visible transmittance values of these two types were close. The window type 5025 controls
considerable amount of solar radiation that is considered unsafe for a proper daylighting design. This
was witnessed by the signiﬁcantly high failure percentage of cases with 5025 in deep zones where the
daylight penetration is already an issue. Thus, the window type that is to be used in a zone should be
depended on the zone depth.
It was discerned that facades in North, North-West and North-East in deep zones do not experience over-
heating problems, using window types of 5025, 6633 and7040 according to the Danish standards. In con-
trast, the use of lowe is more appropriate in north-directed windows in deep zones as it beneﬁts the
daylight without causing overheating problems in this orientation. One of the most interesting ﬁndings
were that window types such as 5025 in deep zones are unfavorable since these zones do not often en-
counter overheating issues, whereas solutions for daylight access are more necessary. A window type
7040 is more proper to be used in those zones for agreement with both criteria, as lowe causes more over-
heating risks. On the contrary, in less deep zones where designers have to tackle overheating problems
more often, window types with lowe SHGC, such as 5025 and 6633 are more suitable since these spaces
can be lit more easily.
The advantage of using window types like 5025 to reserve high amounts of solar radiation is not that great
as the disadvantage of this type to provide adequate daylight. When the designers attempt to control
unwanted solar gains for avoiding overheating phenomena, they should always consider the drawbacks
of daylight insuﬃciency. The daylight target can only be achieved for visible transmittance of at least
0.6-0.7, but in general glazing products ranging from high to low light transmittance can be used if they
are combined with the right glazing-to-ﬂoor ratio.
==PAGE:201==
6.5 Glazing to floor ratio
179
6.5
Glazing to ﬂoor ratio
The glazing to ﬂoor ratio was one signiﬁcant parameter to investigate since the minimum ratio was de-
ﬁned based on provisions of BR18 for daylight suﬃciency. Basically, one of the provisions is to have a
glazing surface which at least covers 10 per cent of the relevant ﬂoor area. Alternatively, the other provi-
sion is to achieve over 300 lux in at least half of the space for more than half of the sunlit hours. Having in
mind both these documentations, it was intended to evaluate if this minimum required glazing ratio was
enough to fulﬁll the required spatial daylight autonomy. Nevertheless, the simulation considered only
the occupied hours so a modiﬁcation is suggested for further work in order for the comparison to be more
accurate. The required glazing area had to be adjusted to some corrections in order to take into account
daylight reduction or exploitation (Johnsen & Lumbye, 2018). The parameter of glazing to ﬂoor area was
corrected based on the daylight reduction that some factors could cause. Therefore, the correction factors
for reduced visible transmittance, wall thickness of the window and room depth were taken into account
to deﬁne this required minimum glazing area. The correction factor of the wall thickness was not con-
sidered since the thickness of the wall construction was less than 0.5 m. However, the corrected required
glazing area for the deep zone was based on both reduced visible transmittance and room depth, while in
case of the less deep zone the correction was based only on the reduced visible transmittance. Therefore,
the glazing area arose from the minimum glazing to ﬂoor ratio in the deep zone was quite high covering
47% (with lowe) to 75% (with 5025) of the facade. In the less deep zone, the glazing area for the minimum
ratio covered 18% to 29% of the facade. Moreover, since the window area had to be less than the facade
area, some of the chosen glazing to ﬂoor ratios were excluded for the deep zone, due to its large ﬂoor
area. In particular, the minimum glazing areas in the deep zone were similar to those ones arose from
R25, R35 and R45 in the other zone.
Glazing to ﬂoor ratio showed a strong relation with criteria 1 and 2. As regards the overheating, the failure
percentages increased dramatically when another glazing to ﬂoor ratio was chosen instead of Rmin in the
less deep zone. More speciﬁcally, the failure percentage in overheating with the minimum ratio in the
narrow and shallow zone was only 3%. Instead, in the other zone the failure percentages of Rmin and R25
were already high. At this point, it is important to mention that the minimum glazing areas in the deep
zone were quite high with the window type 5025 to create the highest glazing surface, increasing in this
way the failure percentage of Rmin.
Overheating problems are tackled if a minimum glazing to ﬂoor ratio is placed in the deep zone in North-
West and North-East, apart from North, which as already mentioned is the orientation that gives freedom
in design options. However, R25 is not a safe choice in west and east oriented deep zones. As regards the
other zone, designing a window with a minimum glazing area can cause minimum overheating risks in all
orientations, and careful combinations with other parameters such as window type or extra shading can
annihilate the overheating phenomena. Whereas, a window facing North with a glazing area covering up
to 45% of the facade is a totally safe option. Increasing the glazing area, the failure increases substantially.
Especially a window with R45 that covers 80% of the facade in the narrow and shallow zone causes high
overheating issues even in North, North-West and North-East. It is worth mentioning that high glazing
ratios with high window types cause undoubtedly high risks in overheating regardless of orientation.
From the daylight point of view, increasing the glazing area, the agreement with criterion 2 increases to
a great extent. Increasing the glazing area from the minimum to 73% in the facade with R25 in the deep
zone, the failure percentage is reduced by half. However, even with this quite large glazing area, which
is the maximum the facade can ﬁt, inadequate daylight levels are provided, since only 60% of cases was
compatible with criterion 2. But this is owing to the depth of this zone, which required special treatment
==PAGE:202==
180
Discussion
in daylighting. The combination of R25 with window types 6633, 7040 and lowe in south, south-east and
south-west oriented deep zones is considered advantageous for suﬃcient daylight access. As for the less
deep zone, where the minimum glazing area covers up to 29% of the facade, the failure percentage was
almost 100%. This is a quite intriguing ﬁnding, since the minimum 10 per cent of glazing to the relevant
ﬂoor area proved inadequate. However, it can not be said that it contradicts to the second provision of
BR18, as the occupied hours were taken into account for the annual climate-based daylight analysis. But
increasing the glazing to ﬂoor ratio by 10%, the passing percentage increased dramatically, especially from
R25 to R35, which assures daylit spaces for South, South-West and South-East. In case of R45, daylight
suﬃciency can be ensured, regardless of other choices.
A suitable window type in small glazing ratios can contribute to the agreement with the criterion of
daylight. However, as the glazing ratio increases, the window type does not play such signiﬁcant role.
However, a wrong strategy that is usually followed in Nordic countries is to apply small glazing surfaces
to reduce the thermal heat losses. This approach is used for the winter season, but has a beneﬁt for the
summer season when solar heat gains are also eliminated through smaller window apertures. Neverthe-
less, this strategy has negative consequences concerning the daylight performance of the building. The
eﬀect of the design parameters in daylight and human health should be considered. So, is it preferable to
have smaller glazing areas with high Tvis and SHGC or larger glazing areas with low Tvis and low SHGC?
Despite the design conﬂicts occur to balance solar gains and meet the requirements, conﬁgurations of
these parameters that fulﬁll both criteria have to be identiﬁed. According to the results, agreement with
both criteria was indicated if large glazing areas are chosen to let daylight enter the space but with a win-
dow type of low Tvis and low solar heat gain coeﬃcient to control as much as possible the overheating
phenomena. However, the agreement with both criteria is achieved if these window conﬁgurations are
placed North, North-West and North-East. More speciﬁcally, large windows enhance occupants’ mood by
providing view to outside. More speciﬁcally, the following table presents which combinations of glazing
to ﬂoor ratio, window type and orientation were complied and can be considered good starting solutions.
