6.6 Window placement
181
The equilibrium between the overheating and daylight is achieved by using solutions that would help
more the agreement in that criterion with higher failure percentage. In general, both zones had approx-
imately the same failure percentage in overheating. Apart from the overheating issues, the deep zone
was more critical to comply with the daylight criterion, and thus, less cases complied with both criteria.
Therefore, deep zones are generally challenging for designers who should turn to design alternatives for
suﬃcient daylight access into the depth. On the other hand, higher and longer overheating hours experi-
enced in the less deep zone turn the equilibrium to those design options that would help to comply with
the overheating criterion.
Table 6.1 outlines that either the glazing to ﬂoor ratio towards the south should be decreased or the glazing
to ﬂoor ratio towards the north should be increased. Almost all the combinations which are compatible
with both criteria contain north directions towards east or west since the overheating issues were lower.
Moreover, the fact that higher glazing to ﬂoor ratios are more suitable renders the parameter of glazing
to ﬂoor ratio of major importance when attempting to achieve suﬃcient daylight in space. Additionally,
the choice of window types is strongly dependent on the glazing to ﬂoor ratio, orientation, and the zone
depth. In deep zones, where the space can not be adequately illuminated, the contribution of window
types with high visible transmittance is high enough. Likewise, in the less deep zones, if the window is
placed in South, a minimum glazing area is better to be used for preventing overheating issues, but with
window types of high visible transmittance. Higher glazing to ﬂoor ratios in the less deep zone proﬁt
the daylight availability but increase the overheating hours so window types with low solar heat gain
coeﬃcient should be used especially in orientations that are unfavorable in terms of overheating. It is
also remarkable that the use of the window type with the highest solar heat gain coeﬃcient and visible
transmittance is appropriate only in three combinations where the need for increasing the agreement
with criterion 2 is high.
6.6
Window placement
The window placement based on the sill and head height did not have any inﬂuence in overheating. This
was anticipated since the size of the window did not change. The distribution of the glazing area was
adjusted based on the sill height meaning that when the window is placed at 1 m is wider and when the
window is placed at 0 m is more narrow. Moreover, the window placement based on the head height
aﬀected the distribution. Some combinations with higher glazing areas than those could ﬁt based on the
speciﬁc arrangement of the height were not evaluated in terms of window sill and head height. This was
considered appropriate for determining the impact of the window height, which in general, is great on
the daylight distribution.
In particular, as for the window sill height in the deep zone, keeping constant the window head to be
up to the ceiling, a signiﬁcant impact on daylight distribution was identiﬁed. The choice of window
sill height on daylight availability varies to a great extent in each window orientation for deep zones.
More speciﬁcally, the vertical placement of the window at 1 m from the ﬂoor level beneﬁts the deep
zone to comply with criterion 2, even in the unfavorable orientations. Interestingly, all cases with a
minimum glazing ratio placed at 1 m in south-oriented windows passed criterion 2, while north-oriented
windows placed at this height marked total failure percentages. The vertical position of the window at
the lowest part of the facade did not cause such great deviations between the orientations. Similarly, in
the less deep zone, the window sill height at 1 m is more preferable. The choice of window sill height
at 1 m in this zone does not yield results with high variability in each orientation. This means that a
wide window in each orientation has similar impact on failing or passing criterion 2. Whereas, if the
==PAGE:204==
182
Discussion
window is placed at 1 m, there is a greater possibility to pass the criterion 2 even if it is facing North,
North-East or North-West. Basically, the placement of window at 1 m increased the agreement with the
daylight criterion in all orientations when it was combined with the glazing to ﬂoor ratios except for
the minimum one. Additionally, the vertical placement of the window at the ﬂoor level showed a higher
percentage in agreement with criterion 2 in the less deep zone compared to the other zone, which is owing
to the smaller area of the zone that can be more easily illuminated. However, this fact was observed in
case that the glazing area was greater than the minimum. This is veriﬁed from this study (Vartiainen et
al., 2000), which indicated that lower-placed windows are allowed in rooms which have shorter distance
from the window and larger window areas. In fact, wider higher-placed windows are beneﬁcial to achieve
suﬃcient daylight access.
Concerning the window head height, a relation between this parameter and agreement with criterion
2 was indicated. Windows placed up to the ceiling height marked higher agreement with the daylight
criterion. However, the eﬀect in daylight was slighter than the one of window sill height. This is attributed
to the height value that was set for the minimum window head height to be at 0.6 m lower than the
maximum. While the maximum window sill height was set at 1 m, hence the results showed a greater
eﬀect. It is also noteworthy that the maximum head height narrowed the window distribution when the
window was placed at the ﬂoor level as in the deep zone, but still the results showed a slightly better
daylight distribution in case of maximum head height.
6.7
Non-visual health potential
Daylight health potentials according to the regulation of the circadian system is signiﬁcant to bring into
the design of oﬃce environments. In oﬃces, the stimulation of circadian system is restricted since people
are not exposed to sunlight. So their productivity and performance at work may be disturbed by insuﬃ-
cient daylight provision which disrupts their sleep or wake cycles. The additional daylighting simulation
oﬀers the possibility to calculate circadian light metrics and evaluate the pattern of circadian rhythms
based on the exposure to sunlight in spaces.
Two cases were simulated which were extreme in terms of overheating. In particular, the glazing area
was the same in both cases, while the window type was lowe in the worst case and 5025 in the best case.
Moreover, the best case in overheating meant also worst case in daylight with the window placed at
a lower level from the ceiling. The circadian illuminance values that were derived from Rea and Lucas
curve were calculated at every two hours from 8:00 to 18:00 which is the working schedule at four selected
dates. This excludes the non-visual health eﬀect that the occupant may have from the exposure to other
light levels before he/she enters the oﬃce and after he/she leaves. The results showed that the best
case in overheating (case B) experiences signiﬁcantly lower circadian-lux compared to the other case.
This is mostly associated with the parameter of the window type (5025) which had the lowest visible
transmittance, hence reducing the daylight penetration in the zone. Under overcast sky during a winter
day of January in Denmark, the circadian-lux are quite low for both cases. This stresses on the necessity
for an appropriate daylighting design so that even the winter, where the days are shorter and darker,
people are exposed to light inﬂuencing positively their mood and performance at work. Moreover, the
results under a clear sky in a spring day in Denmark showed substantially higher circadian illuminances
with the worst case in overheating (case B) to dominate reaching three times more circadian illuminance
values than the other case. These results inform that the non-visual health eﬀects in a sunny day of
May in Denmark are quite higher than those ones under overcast sky in January. However, overcast
days are prevailing in the danish climate, which means that the health potentials from daylight can be
==PAGE:205==
6.7 Non-visual health potential
183
reduced dramatically if the facade is not carefully designed. Additionally, it is estimated that the circadian
illuminance values of all the other cases that were not simulated in Lark Spectral Lighting are amongst the
ones arose from the extreme cases. So proximity to the high values of circadian metrics is recommended
with respect to acceptable thermal and visual conditions, in order to introduce the concept of a healthy
daylit space from the beginning of the design phase.
The pattern of the regulation of the circadian rhythms of the occupant is based on the placement of
the viewpoint which was at the middle oﬃce desk facing the wall. This implies that the results are more
realistic than those would arise from a person that would face the window. The position of a person facing
the window would be unrealistic since the geometry of the zone indicates a furnishing arrangement with
desks placing in each side of the wall. However, although the orientation was intended to be evaluated,
the pattern of circadian metrics under both overcast and clear sky was similar due to the position of the
occupant. So the eﬀect of orientation could not be clearly observed. In all the dates, the circadian response
is improved during the early to mid-morning hours, enhancing in this way the occupant’s alertness and
advance the biological clock (Andersen et al., 2012). The circadian illuminance values reached the peak in
the middle of the day, with less eﬀect of resetting the circadian system (Pechacek et al., 2008), which was
still operating but decreasing until the sunset. This decrease was considerable at 16:00 in the afternoon
in the spring, whereas in the winter the decrease at this time was sharper. This is owing to the short
daytime of the winter in Denmark. The simpliﬁed simulation and the generalized results necessitate the
validation of the simulation on real-world conditions, where the occupant proﬁle varies and thus, aﬀects
the non-visual responses to light in a diﬀerent way. However, even though orientation did not show any
particular impact, east oriented facades as the sun is at low angle experience bright light in the morning,
thus are highly recommended to promote healthy work environments.
To sum up, balance is needed as higher illuminance levels may lead to glare and thermal discomfort.
Window design plays a crucial role in the value of a good design. Small windows are not the solution
for combating the overheating problem. Other alternatives should be taken into account choosing proper
window types or other strategies to address overheating phenomena depending on the direction of the
facade in order to achieve both daylit and comfortable thermal indoor environments. Some of them are the
natural ventilation which is also beneﬁcial to cool down a place when the outdoor temperature is lower
than the indoor. The installation and use of solar shading systems is also quite eﬀective in buildings to
limit unwanted solar gains and oﬀer the occupant the opportunity to control by themselves the daylight
levels. However, people should be aware of the daylight beneﬁts they gain so that the operation of the
blind system should be proper. In this way, overheating risks can be decreased and also daylight visual
discomfort can be prevented, using appropriate exposure to daylight thus considering good circadian
entrainment.
==PAGE:206==
==PAGE:207==
Chapter 7
Conclusion
The growing threat of overheating, as the climate gets warmer, alerts the designer to eliminate potential
risks at early design stages with respect to daylight potentials. The beneﬁts of daylight in occupants’
health as regards the improvement of the regularity of their circadian rhythms, should be maximized,
always by guaranteeing comfortable thermal conditions, with the minimum overheating risks. However,
thermal comfort analysis is usually isolated from a daylighting analysis, and overheating issues are ad-
dressed separately from daylight. As a result, wrong decisions in the early design stages iterate the design
process. This study integrated an overheating and daylighting analysis to identify the design equilibrium
according to Danish building regulation and standards. This thesis addressed the objectives that were set
in Introduction, concluding towards guidelines that direct design in the right way.
A proper facade and window design is necessary to harmonize the thermal and visual and non-visual
behavior of the building by controlling solar heat gains from the conceptual phase. The favorable combi-
nations of facade and window conﬁgurations could be found using parametric design principles. Thus, a
parametric design tool was developed to set up one simulation workﬂow in Grasshopper 3D computing a
thermal simulation analysis dependently on the daylighting. Furthermore, non-visual beneﬁts of daylight
were evaluated through the circadian metrics derived from an additional daylighting workﬂow that was
set up in the tool. In this thesis, the impact of diﬀerent facade and window parameters (orientation, ther-
mal mass, glazing to ﬂoor ratio, glazing type, window placement) related to the parameter of zone depth
was evaluated on overheating and daylighting according to the newly integrated standards of BR18 that
require climate-based daylight assessments. Two criteria were considered for taking into account the tar-
get for not exceeding speciﬁc overheating hours above 26°C and 27°C and providing suﬃcient daylighting
in space. The main ﬁndings of this thesis can be summarized as follows, along with some guidelines.
The depth of a zone subjected to solar gains inﬂuences both the overheating and daylight. The longer the
distance from the window is, the less the spatial daylight autonomy in the zone. Contrarily, the operative
temperature is less probable to be increased in higher levels or for a longer period as in case of a narrow
and deep zone. Nevertheless, this impact can not be related to the criteria. Therefore, adequate illumi-
nation in decisions concerning the depth should be the priority. The diﬀerences in thermal mass applied
only to the external wall system is not suﬃcient enough to provide satisfactory protection against rising
operative temperature in the summertime, neither minimize suﬃciently the duration of such phenom-
ena. More thermal capacity is required to discharge a zone from accumulated heat. However, the wall
construction casts shadows due to its thickness, that above a speciﬁc limit can be an essential obstacle for
suﬃcient illumination. The amount of solar radiation that enters a zone is determined by its orientation.
Thus, the design solutions should be adjusted to this parameter. More freedom in choices is given in ori-
==PAGE:208==
186
Conclusion
entations where the window is subjected to low solar radiation, as the overheating risk is low. However,
the regulation of daylight is an issue that needs to be addressed, using window types with high visible
transmittance and large window areas. Additionally, as the regulation of the circadian rhythms relies on
the illuminance levels that humans are exposed to, the orientation plays a signiﬁcant role. However, the
direction of the occupant’s view can reduce the eﬀect.
The window type is able to ﬁlter quite separately the visible transmittance and the heat gains of the
solar radiation, allowing to use the type that is more relevant to each case. So the penetration of solar
radiation into the zone can be regulated, rendering this parameter highly inﬂuential. In zones with high
overheating risks, window types with low solar heat gain coeﬃcient are recommended, while the day-
light insuﬃciency in deep zones can be addressed using a window type with high visible transmittance.
Window type is also associated with exposure of the human eye to circadian-lux, to avoid the disruption
of the circadian rhythms. The overheating problems can be tackled in unfavorable orientations using just
the minimum required window to ﬂoor ratio. However, larges glazing areas are preferable for adequate
daylight distribution. As the glazing area increases the eﬀect of the ability of window type to control the
solar gains is minimized. Thus, the right combination of the glazing to ﬂoor ratio with the window type is
necessary to lead to safe results. The ﬁndings propose large glazing areas in orientations that experience
the lowest solar radiation with window types of high visible transmittance to increase daylight penetra-
tion, and window types of low visible transmittance in zones with extremely high overheating issues.
As for an additional strategy to increase the daylight availability of a zone, the vertical placement of the
window can contribute signiﬁcantly when placed at a height that reaches up to the ceiling. Moreover,
since a glazing area below 1 m from the ﬂoor level does not provide much of daylight, the same glazing
area is preferable to be distributed horizontally, having no impact in overheating.
The estimation of the non-visual health beneﬁt is essential in the danish building sector and constitutes
a newly integrated way to predict the daylighting beneﬁts to the human health informing the designer
about the high quality of circadian light levels to the human eye. Thus, such design practices should
supplant conservative design approaches that focus only on satisfying thermal comfort conditions by
reducing daylight penetration in a space.
Overall, the major contribution of this study is the development of a parametric tool to assist designers
and researchers in reducing overheating risks meeting appropriate daylight levels during early design
stages. The balance between overheating and daylighting was proved diﬃcult to be achieved. Therefore,
the necessity for evaluation of more design parameters in the tool is drawn, that together with other
design strategies, such as solar shading, would lead to optimal solutions. New methods for simultaneous
assessment of overheating and daylight have to be adopted accounting also for a circadian sensitive de-
sign, venturing beyond existing methods that often direct to wrong decisions. Thus, the basis for right
design decisions is set not only tackling overheating issues but also designing bright lit spaces to house
healthy people.
==PAGE:209==
Chapter 8
Future work
8.1
Limitations
A limitation of the project on daylight performance is that it is based on the oﬃce occupancy hours.
The daylight criterion that was taken into account in this study did not include the sunlit working hours
as Danish Building Regulation 2018 (BR18) states, but the occupancy schedule was considered in the
daylighting simulation, as explained in section 4.4.
Regarding the thermal comfort, only overheating hours are evaluated in this study. Other thermal comfort
indices, such as Predicted Mean Vote (PMV) and Predicted Percentage Dissatisﬁed (PPD) have not been
considered in this study. In a similar manner, glare discomfort is only evaluated based on horizontal
illuminance-based threshold and discomfort glare metrics have not been considered in this study.
In terms of the evaluated parameters, in this study the HVAC system is not speciﬁed. Consequently, the
total energy consumption of the zone per square meter cannot be evaluated in accordance with Danish
building regulations. Moreover, the eﬀect of shading from a surrounding context, e.g. other buildings or
vegetation, have not been considered in this results.
Finally, general limitations of simulation-based studies apply to this study as well. The randomness ex-
isting in several assumptions, algorithms and some initial settings and models included in EnergyPlus,
Daysim or Radiance that are the engines behind the calculations, could eﬀect the results. If the simu-
lations are re-run or diﬀerent software (Cammarano et al., 2015) are used, the results might be slightly
diﬀerent.
8.2
Future
The developed tool currently is set up based on buildings in Denmark and Danish regulations. It associates
daylighting and overheating strategies that comply with the Danish building regulations and standards,
which means that other climate data cannot be included. According to a climate-based approach the
annual dynamic variation in sunlight and skylight conditions throughout the year can be taken into
account. A future step is to further develop the tool so that ﬁrstly, change of climate could be easily
implemented in the set up and secondly, the output results could meet other thresholds and requirements
==PAGE:210==
188
Future work
set up by users according to other requirements, i.e. in case other climate and location than Denmark or
Copenhagen are being evaluated.
Due to climate change and temperature rise, overheating in future years is expected to extend in time.
Two concerns rises, the uncertainties in weather ﬁles and the change in occupancy or sunlit hours. A
future work with focus on these issues can enhance the versatility of this tool to predict the performance
of the building envelope in changing climate conditions. Such development could allow to investigate the
impact of the climate change on the indoor environment in terms of overheating, resulting in beneﬁcial
conclusions that can lead to the drawing of new guidelines or recommendations.
Another important development of the developed tool, is to process optimization of solutions. At its cur-
rent state, the output results are processed to provide information about the impact of diﬀerent design
parameters, which are related to building conﬁguration and facade design, on overheating and daylight-
ing.
The lighting simulation accuracy can be improved from the current 3-channel to a 9-channel spectral sim-
ulation for a complex interaction of wavelengths between skies, glazing, colored surfaces and viewpoints.
The utilization of spectral properties of glazing and other material properties is beneﬁcial to achieve bet-
ter accuracy in lighting simulations. The impact of diﬀerent viewpoints could be assessed through Lark
workﬂow to give the designer information about how to place the furnishing layout in the space.
Finally, inclusion of more complex geometries and facade strategies should be considered in the tool.
In terms of facade solutions, use of bidirectional scattering distribution data for complex fenestration
systems can extend the accuracy of the daylighting analysis for such systems, e.g. shading systems, by
far.

Appendix B
Parametric design tool
Visual graphs of the results
Figure B.1: 3D chart of hourly operative temperature
Figure B.2: Monthly bar chart of energy balance
==PAGE:228==
206
Parametric design tool
Figure B.3: A representative colored mesh of a climate based daylight metric (Daylight Autonomy)
Air Wall
Two cases of zone I were compared, with and without air wall, in order to ﬁnd the diﬀerence in indoor
temperatures. A coloured mesh from the results of air temperature and operative temperature was created
in a way thatFigure B.4 presents. The component of Honeybee_Indoor View Factor Calculator was used
to generate test points and calculate the view factor from each of these points to the other surfaces
of the zone. It is important to clarify that the test points created in each case were not at the same
position, as their generation is based on the width and depth of the ﬂoor surface. The Honeybee_Adaptive
Comfort Analysis Recipe component took into account the indoor surface temperature and was linked to
Honeybee_Microclimate Map Analysis. The ﬁrst hour of the ﬁrst day of the occupancy schedule was set in
analysis period. Through Honeybee_Visualize Microclimate Map, the results of either the air temperature
or the operative were plugged into.
Figure B.4: Annual comfort assessment of EnergyPlus results
Figure B.5 shows the view factor coloured mesh of the air temperature for each test point in both cases
for June 21st at 12:00 at noon. It is considered important to mention that diﬀerent lighting schedules were
==PAGE:229==
207
applied to each case.
Figure B.5: Air temperature in each test point without air wall (left) and with air wall (right) with dif-
ferent lighting schedules
As can be seen from Figure B.5, there is a good air mixing between the two zones which were separated
with an air wall. However, a diﬀerence of 1°C is observed when comparing both cases, with and without
airwall. This temperature diﬀerence of 1°C is attributed to the diﬀerent heat gains from lighting. and
Heat gains from lighting are higher in the ﬁrst case (without air wall), since the lights were almost always
switched on. In the case with an air wall, the air temperature was slightly less, since two lighting schedules
were created in order to reduce the lighting energy.
In order for both cases to be more comparable, the same lighting density per area and the same lighting
==PAGE:230==
208
Parametric design tool
schedule were applied. Thus, zone I was not divided in two lighting zones, but only in two thermal zones
and the function of the air wall could be observed more easily in terms of thermal exchanges. Figure B.6
shows the view factor coloured mesh of the air temperature for each test point in both cases for June 21st
at 12:00 at noon.
Figure B.6: Air temperature in each test point without air wall (left) and with air wall (right) with the
same lighting schedule
According to the Figure B.6, the air temperature in the summer solstice was slightly higher in the case of
the separation with an air wall. Moreover, the temperature diﬀerence was insigniﬁcant. For this reason,
the implementation of the air wall in the model could not be considered as invalid.
Figure B.7 shows the view factor coloured mesh of the operative temperature for each test point in both
cases for June 21st at 12:00 at noon.
==PAGE:231==
209
Figure B.7: Operative temperature in each test point without air wall (left) and with air wall (right) with
the same lighting schedule
According to Figure B.7, the operative temperature was quite close but not the same in two cases since
it was perceived in diﬀerent test points. At that point, it was considered necessary to take a decision
of where the operative temperature could be measured. The distance from the ﬂoor was set to 1.4, in
Honeybee_Indoor View Factor Calculator component, which is the half height of the zone, in order to verify
if EnergyPlus calculates the operative temperature in the middle of the zone. The operative temperature
was found to be 23.36°C in the middle of the room, which was the same with the one retrieved from
EnergyPlus. Thus, EnergyPlus calculates the operative temperature in the middle of the entire zone both
horizontally and vertically.
It was diﬃcult to determine the operative temperature in the middle of the entire zone I, in case of the air
wall. It could be done only by means of a grid surface so as to calculate the operative temperature in the
==PAGE:232==
210
Parametric design tool
test point close to the middle. Even though, the results of the operative temperature in each of these test
points could be saved in a csv ﬁle with hourly results for each test point, it was impossible to deﬁne the
operative temperature in that way. The number of simulated cases was quite signiﬁcant to follow this
process. Moreover, Honeybee_Indoor View Factor Calculator component does not allow the generation of
a rectangular grid cell so as to generate the test point in the middle.
The operative temperature was deﬁned in the middle of the lighting zone I of zone I, since the hours
when operative temperature was higher in the front than in the back were considerably high.
